Buyu — Sales and Trade

Cessation of Menstruation in a Female Slave: Defect, Age, and Return Procedure
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defect criteria and return procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum age for cessation of menstruation to be considered a defect in a female slave according to the mufta bihi view, and explain the waiting period and procedure for returning her.

Core Idea
Cessation of menstruation is a defect only if the slave has reached puberty (15 years mufta bihi) and lasts at least one month; the buyer must wait to ensure she is not pregnant.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on cessation of menstruation (irtifa' al-hayd) as a defect in female slaves. The passage clarifies the required age of the slave, the minimum duration of cessation, and the internal Hanafi dispute over the waiting period before the buyer can return her. Understanding these details is essential for correctly applying the option of defect in slave transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Cessation of menstruation is declared a defect in a female slave. Note that apostasy and istihadah are also defects.
The matn states it is a defect; the commentary confirms.
2
Determine the age criterion
The girl must have reached the maximum age of puberty. Abu Hanifa says 17 years; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say 15 years. The mufta bihi is 15 years.
Age is decisive; if she is under 15, cessation is not a defect.
3
Note the minimum duration
The cessation must last at least one month to be considered a defect (from Fatawa Qadi Khan).
Short cessation does not count.
4
Understand the waiting period for return
After purchase, the buyer must wait to ascertain non-pregnancy. Opinions vary: Abu Hanifa: until clear; Abu Yusuf: 3 months; Muhammad: 4 months 10 days (or 2 months 5 days, which is the practice today).
The practice today is 2 months 5 days.
5
Learn the procedure for return
The buyer claims the defect; the seller is asked to confirm or deny. If the seller refuses to take an oath, the return is effected. The slave woman's statement alone is not enough.
Oath and refusal are key.

Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses what constitutes a defect (عيب) in a female slave specifically regarding the cessation of menstruation (ارتفاع الحيض). The author first states the general rule: apostasy (الكفر) is a defect in both male and female slaves, as is irregular bleeding (الاستحاضة) and the cessation of menstruation in a girl who has reached the age of seventeen—this is not less than a defect, meaning it is indeed a defect. The commentary then clarifies that the condition for considering cessation a defect is that the girl must have reached the maximum age of puberty, which according to Imam Abu Hanifa is seventeen years, but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad it is fifteen years. The fatwa is given according to their view (fifteen years). The passage then details the procedure when a buyer claims such a defect: the seller is asked to confirm, and if he refuses to take an oath, the return is effected. The minimum period for cessation to be considered a defect is one month. Later, the author quotes from al-Fusul al-Imadiyya and other works presenting the internal Hanafi dispute on how long the buyer must wait when he finds the slave woman in a state of ceased menstruation, to ascertain she is not pregnant. The opinions range from waiting until it becomes clear she's not pregnant (Abu Hanifa), to nine months (Abu Mut' ibn Mas'ud?), four months and ten days (Muhammad), and others. The relied-upon practice today, according to al-Khulasa, is the narration from Muhammad of two months and five days. Another view from al-Kafi sets the period from three months to two years depending on the imam. If the judge is a mujtahid, he may exercise ijtihad, otherwise he takes the agreed-upon period of two years. This passage illustrates the detailed casuistry of Hanafi fiqh in defining defects and their procedures.

Key Terms
عيب ('ayb): A defect that gives the buyer the option to return the item (خيار العيب). In slaves, it includes physical or moral flaws that reduce value or impair intended use.
ارتفاع الحيض (irtifa' al-hayd): Cessation of menstruation in a female slave, considered a potential defect if it indicates infertility or illness.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Irregular vaginal bleeding outside the menstrual period, considered a defect in a female slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what is the minimum age of a female slave for cessation of menstruation to be considered a defect?
A) Seventeen years
B) Fifteen years
C) Thirteen years
D) Nine years

Key Takeaway
Cessation of menstruation is a defect only if the slave has reached the age of puberty (15 years mufta bihi) and lasted at least one month; the buyer must wait a specified period (practice: 2 months 5 days) before returning her.

Old Defect After New Defect: Compensation Only
States the ruling when an old defect appears after a new defect has occurred in the purchased item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an old defect appears after a new defect, calculate the compensation using the proportional method, and explain the reasoning behind the restriction on return.

Core Idea
When a new defect blocks return, the buyer's remedy shifts from return to proportional compensation for the old defect.

This passage addresses a critical nuance in the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb): what happens if the buyer discovers an old defect only after a new defect has occurred in his possession? The Hanafi school rules that the buyer cannot return the item – return is only possible if the seller agrees – but he can claim compensation for the reduction in value caused by the old defect. The passage explains the reasoning and provides a method for calculating compensation, along with a response to a theoretical objection about attributing portions of the price to non-physical qualities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Key Condition
Pinpoint the exact legal scenario: an old defect appears after a new defect has occurred in the buyer's hands. The outcome is compensation (nuqsan) for the old defect, not return (radd) unless the seller consents.
2
Understand the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The return is obstructed because the new defect changes the item; returning it would place an unfair burden on the seller, who did not cause the new defect. The buyer's right is limited to financial compensation for the pre-existing defect.
The commentator uses the phrase 'تعذر الرد بسبب العيب الحادث' (return is prevented due to the new defect).
3
Master the Compensation Calculation
Value the item as if sound from the new defect (but with the old defect), then value it with the old defect. The difference as a proportion of the sound value is applied to the purchase price to determine compensation.
Example: if the difference is 1/10 of the value, the buyer recovers 1/10 of the price.
4
Address the Theoretical Objection
An objection states that attributes (like freedom from defect) have no share in the price. The response: attributes that are intended in the transaction do have a share. Here, freedom from the old defect is such an intended attribute.
This distinguishes between attributes that are merely incidental and those that are 'maqsudah bi al-tanawul' (intended to be acquired).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ظهر عيب قديم بعدما حدث عنده عيب آخرُ ، فله نقصانُهُ لا رَدَّهُ إِلا برضا بائعه
فإن ظهر عيب قديم بعدما حدث عنده عيب آخر، فله نقصانُهُ لا رَدَّهُ إلا برضا
[١] قوله : فله نقصانه ; لأنه تعدَّرَ الردُّ بسبب العيب الحادث، وطريق معرفته أن يقوم المبيع سليماً عن العيب الحادث، ثم يقوم معيباً ، فإذا عُرِفَ التَّفاوت بين القيمتين يرجعُ عليه بحصته من الثّمن ، حتى إذا كان عشر القيمة مثلاً يرجع عليه بعشرِ الثّمن ، وإن كان ثلثاً فبثلثه. فإن قلت : هذا مخالف لقولهم : إن الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثّمن. قلنا : إن الأوصاف إذا كانت مقصودة بالتناول حقيقة أو حكماً كان لها حصة من الثَّمن، وهاهنا كذلك. [٢] قوله : لا ردَّه إلا برضاء بائعه ; لأنَّ البائع رضي بالضرر، فيتخير المشتري
If an old defect appears after another defect has occurred in his [the buyer's] possession, then he has its [the price] diminution, not its return, except with the consent of its seller. [Repeated:] If an old defect appears after another defect has occurred in his possession, then he has its diminution, not its return, except with consent. [1] He said: 'He has its diminution' because return is prevented due to the new defect. The method of knowing it is that the sold item is valued sound from the new defect, then it is valued with the defect, and when the difference between the two values is known, he claims from him its share of the price, so that if it is one tenth of the value, for example, he claims one tenth of the price, and if it is a third, then a third of it. If you say: This contradicts their statement that attributes do not have any portion of the price. We say: Attributes, when they are intended [to be acquired] by the transaction, in reality or by legal fiction, have a share of the price, and here it is likewise. [2] He said: 'Not its return except with the consent of its seller' because the seller consented to the loss, so the buyer has the option.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the option of defect (خيار العيب): If the buyer discovers an old defect in the purchased item after a new defect has occurred in his possession, he cannot return the item to the seller. Instead, he is entitled to claim compensation for the diminution in value caused by the old defect. The commentator explains the reasoning: the right to return (الرد) is obstructed because the new defect has changed the item. The method to calculate compensation is to value the item as if it were free of the new defect but with the old defect, and compare it to its value free of both defects, then claim the proportional share of the price. For example, if the diminution is one-tenth of the value, he claims one-tenth of the price. The commentator addresses an objection: it is said that attributes (الأوصاف) are not apportioned in the price. The response is that attributes that are intended in the transaction, either in reality or by legal consideration, do have a share of the price, and here the attribute (freedom from defect) is intended. Finally, the commentator notes that return is only possible if the seller agrees, because by accepting the return, he would be accepting a loss he didn't originally agree to; if he refuses, the buyer's only recourse is the compensation.

Key Terms
نقصان (nuqsan): Compensation for the reduction in value caused by a defect; the difference between the fair market value of the item sound and the item with the defect.
الرد (al-radd): Return of the purchased item to the seller due to a defect; the right of termination (فسخ) of the sale.
العيب الحادث (al-'ayb al-hadith): A defect that arises after the sale while the item is in the buyer's possession, preventing return for a pre-existing (old) defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a purchased item is found to have an old defect after a new defect has occurred in the buyer's possession, what is the buyer's remedy according to the Hanafi school?
A) He may return the item to the seller
B) He may claim compensation for the diminution in value
C) He must keep the item and forfeit any claim
D) He may return the item only if the seller agrees

Key Takeaway
When a new defect arises before the old defect is discovered, the buyer's right shifts from return to proportional compensation for the old defect, balancing the interests of buyer and seller.

Old Defect After New Defect: Reduction Not Return
Ruling and commentary on khiyar al-ayb with supervening defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule when an old defect appears after a new defect and calculate the proportionate reduction (nuqṣān) in price.

Core Idea
When return is impossible due to a new defect, the buyer claims only a proportionate reduction for the old defect.

In sales, the buyer has the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb). But what happens if the buyer discovers a pre-existing defect only after causing a new one? Return becomes impossible because the item is no longer in its original state. The Hanafi school provides a precise remedy: a reduction in price proportional to the old defect. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Understand the situation: a buyer purchases an item, a new defect occurs in his possession (e.g., he breaks a leg of a chair), then he discovers an old defect (e.g., a hidden crack in the backrest) that existed at sale.
The new defect makes return impossible because the item cannot be restored to its original condition.
2
State the Remedy
The buyer cannot return the item but is entitled to a reduction (nuqṣān) in the price -- a proportion of the price equal to the proportion of value lost due to the old defect.
Return is barred by the supervening defect, which is the buyer's responsibility.
3
Understand the Calculation Method
Appraise the item as if it were free of the new defect (i.e., sound) but with the old defect. Then appraise it sound. The difference between these two values is the loss caused by the old defect. The buyer recovers that fraction of the price.
Example: if the value with defect is 90, sound is 100, the loss is 10% of the value, so the buyer gets 10% of the price back.
4
Address the Objection
A potential objection: attributes (like soundness) are not normally compensated by a share of the price. The response: when attributes are the object of the transaction (in essence or by legal consideration), they do have a share. Here, soundness is precisely what the buyer expected.
This is a Hanafi nuance: the sale price covers the intended attributes.
5
Note the Exception
If the seller agrees to accept the item despite the new defect, return is allowed. The seller's consent overrides the impediment.
This is an exception: return becomes possible with the seller's permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ظهر عيب قديم بعدما حدث عنده عيب آخر، فله نقصانُهُ لا رَدَّهُ إِلا برضا بائعه
[1] قوله : فله نقصانه ؛ لأنه تعذر الرد بسبب العيب الحادث، وطريق معرفته أن يقوم المبيع سليماً عن العيب الحادث، ثم يقوم معيباً ، فإذا عرف التفاوت بين القيمتين يرجع عليه بحصته من الثمن ، حتى إذا كان عشر القيمة مثلاً يرجع عليه بعشر الثمن ، وإن كان ثلثاً فبثلثه. فإن قلت : هذا مخالف لقولهم : إن الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن. قلنا : إن الأوصاف إذا كانت مقصودة بالتناول حقيقة أو حكماً كان لها حصة من الثمن، وهاهنا كذلك. [2] قوله : لا رده إلا برضاء بائعه ؛ لأن البائع رضي بالضرر، فيتخير المشتري
If an old defect appears after another defect has occurred at his end, then he is entitled to its reduction, not its return, except with the consent of its seller. [1] His statement: 'so he has its reduction' because return has become impossible due to the new defect. The method of knowing it is to appraise the sold item free from the new defect, then appraise it as defective. When the difference between the two values is known, he recovers from him his share of the price, such that if it is one-tenth of the value, for example, he recovers one-tenth of the price, and if one-third, then one-third. If you say: This contradicts their statement that attributes are not compensated by any part of the price. We say: If attributes are intended for consumption in reality or by legal fiction, they have a share of the price, and here it is likewise. [2] His statement: 'not its return except with the consent of its seller' because the seller is satisfied with the loss, so the buyer has a choice...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect). The ruling deals with a situation where a buyer discovers a pre-existing (old) defect after a new defect has occurred while the item was in his possession. Because return is now impossible due to the new defect (which would change the item), the buyer cannot return the item to the seller. Instead, he is entitled to a reduction in the price proportionate to the old defect. The commentator explains how to calculate the reduction: the item is valued as if free from the new defect but with the old defect, and compared to its sound value. The difference is a fraction of the value; the buyer recovers that fraction of the price. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: attributes (like soundness) do not usually have a share of the price. He responds that when attributes are the object of the transaction in essence or by legal consideration, they do have a share. Finally, the commentator clarifies that the buyer may only return the item if the seller agrees to accept it despite the new defect, because the seller's consent overcomes the impediment.

Key Terms
عيب قديم (ʿayb qadīm): A defect that existed at the time of sale but became apparent after a new defect occurred in the buyer's possession.
عيب حادث (ʿayb ḥādith): A defect that occurred while the item was with the buyer, making return of the item in original condition impossible.
نقصان (nuqṣān): The monetary compensation the buyer receives from the seller equal to the proportion of value lost due to the old defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if an old defect appears after a new defect has occurred in the buyer's possession, what is the buyer's primary remedy?
A) Return the item for a full refund.
B) Claim a proportionate reduction in price.
C) Keep the item and sue the seller for damages.
D) Return the item only if the seller compensates for the new defect.

Key Takeaway
When a new defect prevents return, the buyer may still claim a price reduction proportional to the value loss from the old defect.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Waiting Period for a Slave Girl's Amenorrhea
Intra-Hanafi dispute on waiting period for amenorrhea in slave girl sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different Hanafi opinions on the waiting period for a slave girl with amenorrhea and identify the operative position in later practice.

Core Idea
The period to confirm a slave girl's non-pregnancy is disputed among Hanafi imams, but later practice settled on two months and five days.

When a slave girl stops menstruating, it may indicate pregnancy, which affects the buyer's right to return her (khiyar al-ayb). The Hanafi imams disagreed on how long to wait before concluding she is not pregnant. This passage from later Hanafi works records these opinions and notes which became operative in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and context
The buyer of a slave girl finds she has amenorrhea. He wishes to exercise khiyar al-ayb if she is pregnant. A waiting period is needed to ascertain pregnancy. What are the different durations proposed?
The dispute is within the Hanafi school only.
2
List the opinions with their durations
Extract from the text the period given by each imam: Abu Hanifa and Zufar (2 years), Abu Yusuf (3 months), Muhammad (4 months 10 days or 2 months 5 days), Abu Muti' (9 months).
Note the two reports from Muhammad.
3
Identify the later practice and the rule for judges
Al-Khulasa states that the practice of the people is 2 months 5 days (narration from Muhammad). However, for a non-mujtahid judge, the agreed period (2 years) is taken. Why?
This shows the tension between ijtihad and conservatism in judicial practice.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: the buyer's right to return the slave girl depends on when the waiting period ends. Different periods lead to different timings for exercising khiyar.
This is important for understanding the impact of ikhtilaf.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي «الفصول العمادية»: إذ وجد المشتري الجارية مرتفعة الحيض، ففي قول أبي حنيفة له يدعها حتى يتبيّن أنها ليست بحامل، وقال أبو مطيع له: يَدَعُها حتى تسعة أشهر، وقال محمد ه: يدعها أربعة أشهر وعشراً. وفي «الخلاصة»: في رواية عن محمد الله يدَعُها شهرين وخمسة أيام، وعليه عمل الناس اليوم. وفي «الكافي»: إنَّ المدة التي يسمعُ فيها ارتفاعُ الحيض مقدرة بثلاثة أشهر عند أبي يوسف، وبأربعة أشهر عند محمد الله، وبسنتين عند أبي حنيفة وزفر، فإن كان القاضي مجتهداً يقضي بما أدّى إليه اجتهاده، وإلا يأخذ بالمتفق عليه وهو سنتان. كذا في «شرح العلامة البرْجَندِي».
And in al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah: When the buyer finds the slave girl with high (i.e., ceased) menstruation, then according to Abu Hanifa's statement: He should leave her until it becomes clear that she is not pregnant. And Abu Muti' said: He should leave her for up to nine months. And Muhammad said: He should leave her for four months and ten days. And in al-Khulasah: In a narration from Muhammad, he should leave her for two months and five days, and upon this is the practice of the people today. And in al-Kafi: The period during which the cessation of menses is considered is determined as three months according to Abu Yusuf, four months according to Muhammad, and two years according to Abu Hanifa and Zufar. If the judge is a mujtahid, he rules according to his ijtihad; otherwise, he takes the agreed-upon period, which is two years. This is in Sharh al-Allamah al-Birjandi.

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the waiting period a buyer must observe when a slave girl's menses stop (amenorrhea) to confirm whether she is pregnant. The issue arises when the buyer dislikes buying a pregnant slave girl. Since pregnancy is unknown, a period must elapse to ascertain. The imams differ: Abu Hanifa and Zufar say two years (the maximum period of pregnancy in Hanafi law). Abu Yusuf says three months, Muhammad initially said four months and ten days, but a later narration from him says two months and five days, which became the operative practice. Abu Muti' said nine months. The text concludes that a mujtahid judge may choose, but the agreed-upon period is two years for a non-mujtahid.

Key Terms
ارتفاع الحيض (irtifāʿ al-ḥayḍ): Cessation of menstruation (amenorrhea) in a slave girl, indicating possible pregnancy.
جارية (jāriya): A female slave; the subject of the sale in this context.
مدة (mudda): The prescribed waiting period to observe for confirmation of non-pregnancy.

Buyer's Alteration of Defective Goods: Cutting, Additions, and Khiyar al-Ayb
Rulings and commentary on khiyar al-ayb when buyer alters the purchased item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on khiyar al-ayb when the buyer cuts or adds value to the purchased item, and distinguish between alterations that bar return versus those that allow reduction with recourse.

Core Idea
Cutting a cloth before discovering a defect gives the seller a choice to accept it cut; adding value bars return and entitles the buyer to a reduction.

When a buyer alters a purchased item and then discovers a defect, the right to return (khiyar al-ayb) is affected by the type of alteration. Hanafi law carefully distinguishes between reductions in value (like cutting) and additions of value (like sewing), because the latter involves the buyer's property mixed with the seller's. This lesson walks through the rulings and their rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of alteration
The passage divides alterations into those that reduce value (nuqsan) and those that increase value (ziyada). Cutting the cloth is a reduction; sewing, dyeing red, and mixing with fat are increases.
2
Understand the ruling for reduction (cutting)
If the buyer cuts the cloth and then discovers a defect, the seller has the option to take the cloth cut. If the seller agrees, he takes it as is. If the buyer sells the cut cloth without the seller's consent, he becomes a 'ḥābis' (preventer) and loses any right to claim a reduction from the seller.
The seller's consent removes the impediment to return.
3
Understand the ruling for addition (sewing, dyeing red, mixing)
If the buyer adds value (e.g., sews, dyes with a color that increases value, mixes sawiq with fat), then the seller cannot take the item back. The buyer can claim a reduction (naqṣān) from the seller for the defect.
The impediment here is due to a legal right (ḥaqq al-sharʿ), not the seller's right, because the buyer's property is mixed.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on black dye
Dyeing black is a special case: Abu Hanifa considers it a reduction (like cutting), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it an increase. The ruling follows the majority (Sahibayn) in the later school.
The qualification 'red' in the text ensures agreement that it is an increase.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether the alteration is a reduction or increase, then state whether the seller can take the item back or the buyer gets a reduction.
Practice with examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثوب شراه فقَطَعَهُ فظهر عيب، ولبائعه أخذه كذلك فلا يرجع مشتريه إن باعه. فإن خاطه، أو صبغه أحمر، أو لَتَّ السويق بسمن، ثم ظهر عيبه لا يأخذه بائعه ورجع بنقصانه. [1] قوله: أخذه؛ حاصله: أنه إذا قال البائع: أنا أقبل ذلك الثوب مقطوعاً، فله أن يأخذه كذلك؛ لأنَّ امتناع الرد كان لحقه وقد رضي به، فأسقط حقه، بخلاف ما إذا كان الامتناع لزيادة فيه، فإنَّ الامتناع لحقِّ الشرع لا لحق البائع. [2] قوله: فلا يرجع... الخ؛ الحاصل: أنَّ المشتري إذا باعَ الثَّوبَ المقطوع المذكور عالماً بالعيب بعد القطع قبل البيع لم يرجع على البائع بشيء من النقصان؛ لأنّ الردَّ غيرُ ممتنع برضاء البائع، فإنه جاز أن يقول البائع: أنا أخذه معيباً فالردّ لم يكن ممتنعاً برضاء البائع، فيصير المشتري بالبيع حابساً للبيع، فلا يرجع بالنقصان. [3] قوله: أحمر؛ قيد به لتكون الزيادة في البيع ثابتة اتفاقاً؛ لأنه لو صبغه أسود يكون نقصاناً عنده كالقطع، وعندهما: يكون زيادة. [4] قوله: لتَّ؛ بفتح اللام وتشديد التاء، أي عجنه. [5] قوله: لا يأخذه بائعه؛ لأنَّ الامتناع لا لحق البائع، بل لحق الشرع للزيادة، وهي في معنى الربا، وحرمة الربا في حق الشرع.
A cloth he bought and cut, then a defect appeared; its seller has the option to take it cut, so its buyer does not have recourse if he sells it. If he sewed it, or dyed it red, or mixed sawiq with fat, then its defect appeared, its seller does not take it, and he has recourse for its reduction. [1] His statement: 'take it'; its summary: if the seller says, 'I accept that cloth cut,' he may take it as such because the impediment to return was for his right and he consented to it, thus he waived his right, unlike when the impediment is due to an increase in it, then the impediment is for a legal right, not the seller's right. [2] His statement: 'so he does not have recourse...' the summary: if the buyer sells the aforementioned cut cloth knowing of the defect after cutting before the sale, he does not have recourse against the seller for any reduction because return was not impossible due to the seller's consent; for it is permissible that the seller says, 'I take it defective,' so return was not impossible due to the seller's consent, so the buyer by selling becomes a restrainer of the sold item, thus he does not have recourse for reduction. [3] His statement: 'red'; it is qualified to make the increase in the sale established by agreement, because if he dyed it black, it would be a reduction in his view like cutting, and in their view it is an increase. [4] His statement: 'latta'; with fatha on the lam and tashdid on the ta, meaning he kneaded it. [5] His statement: 'its seller does not take it'; because the impediment is not for the seller's right, but for a legal right due to the increase, and it is akin to riba, and the prohibition of riba is for the sake of the law.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on khiyar al-ayb when the buyer alters the purchased item before discovering a defect. It distinguishes between alterations that remove value (cutting) and those that add value (sewing, dyeing red, mixing with fat). For cutting: the seller can decide to take the cut cloth, waiving his objection. If the buyer sells the cut cloth without the seller's acceptance, he cannot claim a reduction from the seller because return was possible if the seller agreed. For additions: when the buyer sews, dyes (with a color that increases value), or mixes, the seller cannot take the item back because the buyer's property (thread, dye, fat) is mixed with it, making return legally problematic (akin to riba). In such cases, the buyer can claim a reduction for the defect. The commentator clarifies that dyeing black is considered a reduction (like cutting) according to Abu Hanifa, but an increase according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms
زيادة (ziyāda): An addition to the item that increases its value, such as sewing, dyeing red, or mixing with fat; prevents return due to legal impediment.
نقصان (nuqṣān): A reduction in value, as in cutting cloth or dyeing black (according to Abu Hanifa); such alteration allows return if seller agrees.
حابس للبيع (ḥābis li-l-bayʿ): One who withholds the sold item from the seller, thereby forfeiting the right to claim reduction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, the seller must take it back.
B) No, the seller cannot take it back.
C) The seller can take it back if he agrees to accept it cut.
D) The buyer must keep it and claim nothing.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-ayb, cutting permits return with seller's consent; value-adding alterations bar return and shift remedy to a reduction claim.

Khiyar al-Ayb: Buyer's Alterations and Right of Return
Rulings on khiyar al-ayb with buyer alterations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between separable decreases and inseparable increases in khiyar al-ayb, and state the Hanafi ruling for each case.

Core Idea
A buyer's separable decrease lets the seller take back; an inseparable increase gives the buyer compensation.

When a buyer discovers a defect after altering the goods, the Hanafi school carefully analyzes whether the alteration is a decrease or an increase, and whether it can be separated. This lesson examines the key scenarios from the 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah commentary, including the crucial distinction between cutting (separable) and sewing/dyeing/mixing (inseparable).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The text presents two contrasting cases: cutting the cloth (decrease) vs. sewing, dyeing red, or mixing sawiq with ghee (increase). Note that the ruling differs based on the nature of the alteration.
Cutting is a separable decrease; sewing is an inseparable increase.
2
Determine the seller's right
In the cutting case, the seller can take the cloth as is (because the buyer's action created a separate decrease). In the sewing/dyeing/mixing case, the seller cannot take it because the buyer's property (thread, dye, ghee) is mixed with the original.
The seller's right is tied to the separability of the increase.
3
Analyze the buyer's remedy
If the buyer sells the cut cloth after knowing the defect, he loses the right to claim compensation from the original seller (because he prevented the return). In the increase case, the buyer may claim arsh (compensation for the defect) and has the option to return the item only if the seller agrees.
The buyer cannot claim compensation after the seller agrees to return.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on black dye
Abu Hanifa considers black dye a decrease (like cutting) because it reduces market value. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it an increase because it is a physical addition. The text presents both views without resolution.
This dispute affects whether the seller can take back the dyed cloth or the buyer gets compensation only.
5
Understand the wisdom behind the rule
The prevention of return in the increase case is for the right of the Shari'ah (to avoid riba-like unfairness), not for the seller's personal right. The seller cannot benefit from an increase without paying for it.
The increase is akin to riba because the seller would get extra without consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع
خيار العيب

ثوب شراه فقَطَعَهُ فظهر عيب، ولبائعه أخذه كذلك فلا يرجع مشتريه إن باعه. فإن خاطه، أو صبغه أحمر، أو لَت السويق بسمن، ثمَّ ظهر عيبُهُ لا يأخذه بائعه ورجع بنقصانه.

قال: (فلا يرجع مشتريه إن باعه) أي لا يرجع المشتري بالنقصان إن باعه؛ لأن البائع كان له أن يقول: أنا آخذه معيباً، فالمشتري بالبيع يكون حابساً للمبيع فلا يرجع بالنقصان.

(فإن خاطه، أو صبغه أحمر، أو لَت السويق بسمن، ثم ظهر عيبُهُ لا يأخذه بائعه ورجع بنقصانه) أي رجع المشتري بنقصان العيب، ولا يكون للبائع أن يقول: أنا آخذه معيباً؛ لاختلاط ملك المشتري بالمبيع، وهو الخيط، والصبغ، والسمن. حينئذ إن شاء ردَّه، وإن شاء أخذه وليس له أن يرجع بالنقصان بعد رضاء البائع بالرد؛ لأن الموجب لذلك وهو امتناعه من الرد قد زال.

قوله: أخذه؛ حاصله: أنه إذا قال البائع: أنا أقبل ذلك الثوب مقطوعاً، فله أن يأخذه كذلك؛ لأنَّ امتناع الرد كان لحقه وقد رضي به، فأسقط حقه، بخلاف ما إذا كان الامتناع لزيادة فيه، فإنَّ الامتناع لحقِّ الشرع لا لحق البائع، فالفرق بين.

قوله: فلا يرجع ... الخ؛ الحاصل: أنَّ المشتري إذا باعَ الثوبَ المقطوع المذكور عالماً بالعيب بعد القطع قبل البيع لم يرجع على البائع بشيء من النقصان؛ لأنّ الردَّ غيرُ ممتنع برضاء البائع، فإنه جاز أن يقول البائع: أنا أخذه معيباً فالردّ لم يكن ممتنعاً برضاء البائع، فيصير المشتري بالبيع حابساً للبيع، فلا يرجع بالنقصان.

قوله: أحمر؛ قيد به لتكون الزيادة في البيع ثابتة اتفاقاً؛ لأنه لو صبغه أسود يكون نقصانا عنده كالقطع، وعندهما: يكون زيادة.

قوله: لت؛ بفتح اللام وتشديد التاء، ترکردن بست و مانند آن. كذا في «المنتخب»، يقال: لت السويق: أي عجنه.

قوله: لا يأخذه بائعه؛ لأنَّ الامتناع لا لحق البائع، بل لحق الشرع للزيادة، وهي في معنى الربا، وحرمة الربا في حق الشرع.

قوله: ورجع ... الخ؛ لأنَّ الردَّ ممتنع بسبب الزيادة، فإنَّ الرد إما أن يكون بدونِ الزيادة، فهي لا تنفك عن الأصل، وإما معها فالعقد لم يَرِدْ عليها بل على الأصل فقط.

واعلم أنَّ الزيادة على نوعين:
1. متصلة
2. ومنفصلة.
والمتصلة على نوعين:
1. متولّدة عن الأصل: كالجمال والسمن.
2. وغير متولدة: كالخياطة والصبغ واللت.
والمنفصلة أيضاً على نوعين:
1. متولّدة عن الأصل: كالولد، والثمر.
2. وغير متولدة: كالكسب.
فالمتصلة المتولّدة، والمنفصلة الغير متولدة لا تمنعان الردّ بالعيب:
أما الأولى: فلإمكان الفسخ في الزيادة؛ لأنها تبع محض باعتبار التولّد والاتصال.
وأما الثانية: فلإمكان فسخ العقد في الأصل بدون الزيادة، وتسليم الزيادة للمشتري مجاناً.
والمتصلة الغير المتولدة، والمنفصلة المتولدة تمنعان الردّ بالعيب:
أما الأولى: فلأنَّ فسخ العقد ممتنع: إما بدونِ الزيادة فلامتناع انفكاكها عن الأصل، وإما معها فلكونها غير مبيعة.
وأما الثانية فلامتناع فسخ العقدِ أيضاً، إما بالدات فلعدم ورود العقد عليها، وإمّا بالتبع فلانقطاع التبعية بالانفصال.

قوله: لم يصر...الخ؛ وعلى هذا إنَّ من اشترى ثوباً فقطعه لباساً لولده الصغير، وخاطه ثمَّ اطلع على عيب، لا يرجع بالنقصان بخلاف ما لو كان الولد كبيراً؛ لأن التمليك حصل في الأوّل قبل الخياطة، وفي الثاني بعدها بالتسليم إليه.
Chapter of Sale. Option of Defect. A cloth he bought and cut it, then a defect appeared, and its seller can take it as such, so his buyer does not claim compensation if he sells it. But if he sewed it, or dyed it red, or mixed the sawiq with ghee, then its defect appeared, its seller does not take it and he (the buyer) claims compensation for its defect. He said: (So his buyer does not claim compensation if he sells it) i.e., the buyer does not claim compensation for the defect if he sells it; because the seller could have said: I take it defective, so the buyer by selling becomes a preventer of the sold item, so he does not claim compensation. (But if he sewed it, or dyed it red, or mixed the sawiq with ghee, then its defect appeared, its seller does not take it and he claims compensation for its defect) i.e., the buyer claims compensation for the defect, and the seller cannot say: I take it defective; due to the mixing of the buyer's property with the sold item, which is the thread, the dye, and the ghee. Then if he wishes, he returns it, and if he wishes, he takes it, and he has no right to claim compensation after the seller's satisfaction with the return; because the cause for that, which is his prevention from return, has ceased. His statement: 'take it'; its summary: that when the seller says: I accept that cloth cut, he may take it as such; because the prevention of return was for his right and he consented to it, so he dropped his right, unlike when the prevention is due to an increase in it, then the prevention is for the right of the Shari'ah, not for the seller's right, so there is a difference between. His statement: 'so he does not claim ... etc.'; the summary: that when the buyer sells the aforementioned cut cloth knowing about the defect after the cutting before the sale, he does not claim anything from the seller of the compensation; because the return is not prevented by the seller's satisfaction, for it is permissible that the seller says: I take it defective, so the return was not prevented by the seller's satisfaction, so the buyer by selling becomes a preventer of the sale, so he does not claim compensation. His statement: 'red'; he specified it so that the increase in the sale is established by agreement; because if he dyed it black, it would be a decrease according to him (Abu Hanifa) like cutting, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is an increase. His statement: 'mixed' with fatha of lam and shadda of ta, meaning 'to stir and the like'. That is in al-Muntakhab. It is said: 'latta al-sawiq' i.e., he kneaded it. His statement: 'its seller does not take it'; because the prevention is not for the seller's right, but for the right of the Shari'ah due to the increase, and it is akin to riba, and the prohibition of riba pertains to the right of the Shari'ah. His statement: 'and he claims ... etc.'; because the return is prevented due to the increase, for the return either is without the increase, then it cannot be separated from the original, or with it, then the contract did not apply to it but only to the original. And know that the increase is of two types: 1. Connected, 2. Disconnected. The connected is of two types: 1. Generated from the original: like beauty and fatness. 2. Not generated: like sewing, dyeing, and mixing. The disconnected is also of two types: 1. Generated from the original: like offspring and fruit. 2. Not generated: like earnings. The connected generated and the disconnected not generated do not prevent return due to defect: As for the first, because it is possible to rescind in the increase; since it is a mere follower due to generation and connection. As for the second, because it is possible to rescind the contract in the original without the increase, and deliver the increase to the buyer for free. The connected not generated and the disconnected generated prevent return due to defect: As for the first, because rescinding the contract is impossible: either without the increase, then due to the impossibility of its separation from the original, or with it, then because it is not sold. As for the second, because rescinding the contract is also impossible, either by itself due to the contract not applying to it, or as a follower due to the termination of followership by separation. His statement: 'did not become ... etc.'; and upon this, if someone buys a cloth and cuts it as a garment for his young son, and sews it, then discovers a defect, he does not claim compensation, unlike if the son was adult; because the ownership occurred in the first before the sewing, and in the second after it by delivery to him.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the rules of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) in the Hanafi school. It addresses situations where the buyer alters the goods after purchase and before discovering a defect. The text presents two contrasting scenarios.

First scenario: The buyer cuts the cloth, then finds a defect. The seller can take the cloth in its cut state. If the buyer sells the cut cloth to someone else after learning of the defect, he loses his right to claim any compensation from the original seller. Why? Because the seller was willing to take back the defective cloth; by selling it, the buyer himself prevented the return. So the chain of recourse is broken.

Second scenario: The buyer sews the cloth, dyes it red, or mixes sawiq (a type of porridge) with ghee. If a defect then appears, the seller cannot take back the goods because the buyer's property (thread, dye, ghee) is now mixed with the original item. The buyer, however, can claim compensation (arsh) for the defect. He has a choice: either to return the item (if the seller agrees) or keep it and take compensation. Once the seller agrees to take it back, the buyer can no longer claim compensation.

The commentary then distinguishes between red dye (agreed increase) and black dye (dispute: Abu Hanifa considers it a decrease like cutting, while the two students consider it an increase). The reason the seller cannot take back the item with an increase is that the increase is akin to riba (usury) if the seller takes it without consideration; the Shari'ah forbids that.

The passage concludes with a comprehensive classification of increases (ziyadah) into connected/disconnected and generated/not-generated, showing which types prevent return and which do not. Connected generated increases (like fatness) do not prevent return because they are inseparable but can be rescinded as followers. Connected non-generated increases (like sewing) prevent return because they cannot be separated. Disconnected generated (like offspring) prevent return because they are separate but were generated from the item. Disconnected non-generated (like earnings) do not prevent return because the contract can be cancelled without them. The final example illustrates the rule with a father cutting and sewing a cloth for a minor son versus an adult son, showing that ownership timing matters.

Throughout, the commentary follows the order of the original matn, providing legal reasoning (ta'lil) for each ruling. It is crucial to note that the buyer's rights depend on the nature of his alteration: if it is a separable decrease (like cutting), the seller can take it back; if it is an inseparable increase, the seller cannot, and the buyer gets compensation.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The legal option (khiyar) granted to a buyer upon discovering a pre-existing defect in the purchased item, allowing him to either return the item or claim compensation (arsh).
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid by the seller to the buyer for a defect, calculated as the difference between the value of the item in its sound state and its value in the defective state.
زيادة متصلة (ziyadat muttasilah): A physical increase that is attached to the original item, such as sewing thread, dye, or kneading ghee into sawiq.
زيادة منفصلة (ziyadat munfasilah): A separate increase that is not physically attached to the original item, such as offspring of an animal or rent earned from the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a cloth, discovers it is defective, but before returning it, he sews it into a shirt. What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The buyer can return the shirt and claim the full price.
B) The buyer cannot return the shirt but can claim compensation for the defect.
C) The buyer must keep the shirt and has no claim.
D) The seller can take the shirt as is, and the buyer gets nothing.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-ayb, a buyer's separable decrease allows the seller to take back the goods, while an inseparable increase prevents return and gives the buyer a right to compensation.

Classification of Increases and Their Effect on Return for Defect
Classifies increases into four types with rulings on return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any increase in a sold item as connected/disconnected and generated/non-generated, and state whether it bars the buyer's right to return for defect according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Not every increase bars return—only connected non-generated and disconnected generated increases prevent the right to return for defect.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a crucial classification for the Hanafi law of sale. When a buyer discovers a defect after the sale, the right to return may be lost if the item has increased in value or substance. The key is to determine the nature of that increase. The commentator explains a fourfold typology and its legal effect. Mastering this will help you apply the rule consistently in real transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
The author first splits increases into connected (muttasilah) and disconnected (munfasilah). Connected increases are physically attached to the item; disconnected increases are separate. Recognize the distinction from the examples: sewing (connected) vs. earnings (disconnected).
2
Sub-classify each category
Within each main category, further divide into generated (mutawallidah) from the item itself and non-generated (ghayr mutawallidah) from external human action. Examples: connected generated: fatness; connected non-generated: dyeing. Disconnected generated: offspring; disconnected non-generated: rental income.
3
Apply the ruling to each of the four types
Memorize the rule: connected generated and disconnected non-generated do NOT prevent return. Connected non-generated and disconnected generated DO prevent return. Understand the reasons: for the first pair, the increase can be returned with the item or the contract can be rescinded without the increase; for the second pair, the increase cannot be separated or is not part of the original contract.
The reasoning is crucial for application.
4
Apply the rule to the tailoring example
The commentator gives a practical case: a buyer cuts and sews a cloth for his young child (connected non-generated) then finds a defect—he cannot claim depreciation because the sewing bars return. But if the child is older and sewing is done after delivery to the child, the increase occurs after ownership transfer, so return is allowed.
This shows how timing and ownership interact with the classification.

(As if he sold it after seeing its defect): i.e., just as the buyer recovers the depreciation due to defect if he sold the sewn garment, or dyed, or the mixed saweeq after seeing its defect; because by the sale he did not become a detainer of the sold item. Know that the increase is of two types: 1. Connected, 2. Disconnected. The connected is of two types: 1. Generated from the original, such as beauty and fatness, 2. Non-generated, such as sewing, dyeing, and mixing. The disconnected is also of two types: 1. Generated from the original, such as offspring and fruit, 2. Non-generated, such as earning. The connected generated and the disconnected non-generated do not prevent the return for defect. As for the first: because it is possible to rescind the increase, as it is a mere appendage by virtue of generation and connection. As for the second: because it is possible to rescind the contract on the original without the increase, giving the increase to the buyer for free. The connected non-generated and the disconnected generated prevent the return for defect. Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah, explaining the types of increases (ziyadah) in a sold item and their effect on the buyer's right to return for defect. The author first clarifies that if a buyer sells the item after seeing the defect, he loses the right to claim depreciation. Then he presents a comprehensive classification of increases. Increases are of two main types: connected (muttasilah) and disconnected (munfasilah). Connected increases are those that are attached to the item, such as beauty, fatness (generated), or sewing, dyeing (non-generated). Disconnected increases are separate, like offspring, fruit (generated), or earnings (non-generated). The ruling: Connected generated and disconnected non-generated increases do NOT prevent the right to return. The reason: for the first, the increase is a mere appendage and can be returned along with the item; for the second, the contract on the original can be rescinded without including the increase, and the increase is given to the buyer for free. Connected non-generated and disconnected generated increases DO prevent the right to return. The reason: for the connected non-generated, it is impossible to separate it from the original without destroying the item, so the contract cannot be rescinded without including the unsold increase; for the disconnected generated, the increase is not covered by the contract and is no longer a dependent part after separation. The commentator then gives an example: if a buyer cuts and sews a cloth for his young child before discovering the defect, he cannot claim depreciation because the sewing (a connected non-generated increase) prevents return. But if the child was older and the sewing was done after delivery to the child (i.e., after the child owned it), then the increase occurred after the buyer's ownership ended, so the right to return remains.

Key Terms
زيادة (ziyādah): An increase in the value or quantity of the sold item occurring after the contract.
متصلة (muttaṣilah): An increase that is physically attached to the sold item, such as sewing or fatness.
منفصلة (munfaṣilah): An increase that is separate from the sold item, such as offspring or earnings.
متولدة (mutawallidah): An increase that originates from the sold item itself, such as fatness or offspring.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following increases bars the buyer's right to return a defective item?
A) The sheep becomes fatter (connected generated).
B) The buyer sows the cloth into a shirt (connected non-generated).
C) The buyer earns rent from the house (disconnected non-generated).
D) The cow gives birth to a calf that is still attached to the mother (connected generated).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of increases into four types determines whether the buyer can return a defective item: connected non-generated and disconnected generated bar return; others do not.

Actions Before Defect Discovery: Freeing, Tadbir, Istilad, and Death
Explains when buyer's actions before defect discovery allow recovery of deficiency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each buyer action (freeing gratuitously, tadbir, istilad, and death) performed before defect discovery, and explain when the deficiency claim is preserved.

Core Idea
Freeing before discovery bars deficiency claim; tadbir and istilad do not; death always bars.

When a buyer discovers a defect in the purchased item after taking possession, Islamic law grants him the option to return the item or claim compensation for the deficiency. However, this right may be affected if the buyer performs certain actions on the item before learning of the defect. In this lesson, we examine four specific scenarios from the Hanafi school: the buyer frees the slave gratuitously, makes a tadbir declaration, makes a female slave an umm walad, or the slave dies – all before the defect is discovered. The rulings hinge on whether the action removes the possibility of returning the item or merely prevents its return while ownership remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action and its timing
Determine what the buyer did and whether it occurred before or after discovering the defect. Only actions before discovery are considered here.
If the action was after discovery, deficiency claim is lost regardless.
2
Classify the action's effect on ownership and transferability
Freeing transfers ownership entirely; tadbir and istilad keep ownership but make the item nontransferable; death destroys the item.
These effects determine if return is possible.
3
Apply the principle from al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah
If return is impossible (legal or physical) and the item remains in buyer's ownership at time of action, deficiency claim is preserved. If return was possible and buyer removed it from his ownership, claim is lost.
4
State the ruling for each scenario
For gratuitous freeing before discovery: no deficiency claim. For tadbir and istilad before discovery: deficiency claim is allowed. For death before or after discovery: no deficiency claim.
The commentator clarifies that death bars claim regardless of when it occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو اعتقه قبلها مجاناً ، أو دبَّرَهُ ، أو استولدها ، أو مات عنده قبلها
إذ قبل البيع لم يكن للبائع أخذه معيباً ؛ لاختلاط ملك المشتري به ، فلم يبطل حق الرجوع إليه بالنقصان ، (أو اعتقه قبلها مجاناً ، أو دبَّرَهُ ، أو استولدها ، أو مات عنده قبلها : أي قبل رؤية العيب ، صورة المسائل : أنه عَتَقَ المشتري العبد مجاناً ، أو دَبَّرَهُ ، أو استولد المشتراة، أو مات المشترى في يد المشتري، ثُمَّ اطَّلَعَ على عيب رَجَعَ بالنقصان". وهذا معنى ما في «الفوائد الظهيرية» من أنَّ الأصل أن كل موضع يكونُ المبيع قائماً على ملك المشتري ويمكنه الردُّ برضاء البائع فأخرجه عن ملكه لا يرجعُ بالنقصان، وكل موضع يكون المبيع قائماً على ملكه ولا يمكنه الردّ، وإن قبله البائع فأخرجه عن ملكه يرجعُ بالنقصان ، كما في «البحر » )
[١]قوله : أو أعتقه ؛ أي إن اشترى عبداً فأعتقه قبل رؤية العيب بلا مال، أو دبَّرَه قبلها، أو اشترى أمةً فاستولدها : أي جعلها أم ولد قبل رؤية العيب، أو مات ذلك العبد، أو تلك الأمة عند المشتري قبل رؤية العيب، ثمَّ اطَّلع على عيب المشتري رجع المشتري على البائع بنقصانه.
فإن اشترى العبد وأعتقه قبل رؤية العيب بلا مال، ثمَّ اطلع على عيب لم يرجع كما سيأتي، أو اشترى بعد رؤية العيب فلا رجوع بنقصانه ؛ لأنّ الاشتراء بعد رؤية العيب دليل الرضاء ، وهو يسقط الرجوع.
أو اشترى قبل رؤية العيب وأعتقه أو دبَّرَه أو استولدها بعد رؤية العيب، فكذلك سقوط المرجوع ؛ لأنَّ التصرُّفَ بعد رؤية العيب في المبيع مسقط للرجوع، وأما موتُ العبدِ قبل رؤية العيب وبعده سواء في سقوط الرّجوع كما سيصرح به الشارح له أيضا، فعلى هذا قيد القبليّة في الموتِ ليس بمفيد بل يجب حذفه.
[٢] قوله : رجع بالنقصان ؛ أما في صورة الإعتاق فلما يذكره الشارح ، أما في صورة التدبير والاستيلاد ؛ فلأنهما وإن كانا لا يزيلان الملك إلا أنَّ المحل بهما يخرج عن أن يكون قابلا للنقل من ملك إلى ملك ، فقد تعـدر الـرد مـع بـقـاء الملك ، فيرجعُ بالنقصان ؛ لأنه استحق بوصف السلامة ، وصانه كما تعيب عنده.
Or he freed him before it gratuitously, or he made a tadbir for him, or he made her umm walad, or it died with him before it. Because before the sale the seller could not take it with defect due to the mixing of the buyer's ownership, so the right to recourse for deficiency is not nullified. (Or he freed him before it gratuitously, or made tadbir, or made the purchased slave-girl umm walad, or the purchased item died in the buyer's hand, then he discovered a defect: he recovers the deficiency. This is the meaning in al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah: the principle is that wherever the sold item remains in the buyer's ownership and he can return it with the seller's consent, then if he removes it from his ownership he does not recover deficiency; and wherever the sold item remains in his ownership and he cannot return it, even if the seller accepts it, then if he removes it from his ownership he recovers deficiency, as in al-Bahr.) [1] His saying: 'or he freed him': i.e., if he bought a slave and freed him before the discovery of the defect without payment, or made tadbir before it, or bought a slave-girl and made her umm walad before the discovery, or that slave or slave-girl died in the buyer's hand before the discovery, then he discovered a defect, the buyer recovers the deficiency from the seller. But if he bought the slave and freed him before the discovery without payment, then discovered a defect, he does not recover, as will come; or if he bought after the discovery, then no recourse for deficiency because buying after the discovery indicates consent, which nullifies recourse. Or if he bought before the discovery and freed him, made tadbir, or made her umm walad after the discovery, likewise the recourse is dropped because acting after the discovery on the sold item nullifies recourse. As for the death of the slave before the discovery and after it, they are equal in dropping recourse, as the commentator will also clarify, so the condition 'before' regarding death is not beneficial; rather it must be omitted. [2] His saying: 'he recovers the deficiency': As for the case of freeing, it will be mentioned by the commentator. As for tadbir and istilad: although they do not remove ownership, they make the item no longer transferable from one ownership to another, so return becomes impossible despite the survival of ownership, so he recovers deficiency; because he was entitled to the description of soundness, and it became defective with him.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenarios where a buyer takes certain actions (freeing the slave, tadbir, making a slave-girl umm walad, or the slave's death) before discovering a defect, and how this affects his right to claim deficiency from the seller. The base ruling: If the buyer frees the slave gratuitously before discovering the defect, or makes tadbir, or makes the female slave umm walad, or the slave dies in his possession before he learns of the defect, he may still claim the deficiency from the seller. The reason is that these actions either prevent the possibility of returning the item (because freeing transfers ownership, tadbir and istilad make the item nontransferable, death destroys the item) while the buyer owned it, so the seller's liability for the defect remains. However, if the buyer does any of these actions after discovering the defect, he loses the right to claim deficiency because that indicates acceptance of the item as is. A key principle is cited from al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah: the general rule is that if the sold item remains in the buyer's ownership and he can physically return it (with the seller's consent), then if he removes it from his ownership (e.g., by selling or gifting), he loses the right to claim deficiency. But if he cannot return it (due to legal impediments like tadbir or istilad, even though ownership remains), then if he removes it from his ownership, he still may claim deficiency. The commentator clarifies that death of the slave before or after discovering the defect is the same in dropping the right to return, because death itself prevents return regardless of timing. However, the text initially includes death 'before' as a case allowing deficiency claim, but the commentator notes this is inconsistent and the condition 'before' should be omitted. The practical outcome: for death, the buyer never recovers deficiency because the item perishes in his possession.

Key Terms
عتق (ʿitq): Freeing a slave without monetary consideration (gratuitous manumission).
تدبير (tadbīr): A declaration that the slave will be free upon the owner's death.
استيلاد (istīlād): Making a female slave an umm walad by bearing her owner's child, which grants her freedom upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He cannot claim deficiency because he made her umm walad after purchase.
B) He can claim deficiency because istilad does not remove ownership but prevents return.
C) He loses the right to claim deficiency because he should have inspected before.
D) He can return the slave and claim full refund.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position distinguishes between transactions that transfer ownership (freeing) and those that merely prevent return (tadbir/istilad) when determining the buyer's right to claim deficiency after an action before defect discovery.

Freeing and Killing the Defective Slave: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Buyer's Rights
Intra-Hanafi dispute on freeing and killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a buyer who frees or kills a defective slave retains the right to claim compensation, and explain the legal reasoning (dalil) behind each view.

Core Idea
Freeing on payment is like retaining the price; killing is a compensable act – but the Sahibayn see it differently.

This lesson examines two specific scenarios from khiyar al-'ayb: when a buyer frees a defective slave in exchange for payment, or kills the slave. While return is impossible in both, the schools differ on whether the buyer can claim compensation for the defect. We explore the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn, and note where the fatwa settles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for both actions
In both freeing on payment and killing, the buyer cannot return the slave (radd) because the item no longer exists in his possession in the same state.
This is uncontested; the dispute is only about compensation.
2
State Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer has no right to claim compensation (ruju' bi al-nuqsan) in either case.
This is the zahir al-riwayah for killing; for freeing, it is also his view according to the text.
3
State the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (and al-Shafi'i, Ahmad) hold that the buyer may claim compensation for the defect.
They base this on qiyas and istihsan.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
For freeing: the buyer detains the equivalent (the payment), which is like detaining the item itself; thus compensation is prevented. For killing: killing is a mudman act; if done on another's property, compensation is due. Since the act is his own property, liability drops, but this is treated as if he sold the item, precluding compensation.
5
Analyze the Sahibayn's dalil
For freeing: terminating ownership with compensation is like freeing without compensation (which allows compensation), because the compensation and the item are both the buyer's property. For killing: killing is like natural death since no worldly penalty attaches; thus the buyer is not considered to have held the item, so compensation is allowed.
The text mentions a narration from al-Yanabi' where the Sahibayn concur with Shafi'i and Ahmad.

وإن أعتقه على مال، أو قتله، أو أكل الطعام كله، أو بعضه، أو ليس الثوب فَتَخَرَّقَ لم يرجع (وإن أعتقهُ على مال، أو قتله، أو أكل الطعام كُلَّهُ ، أو بعضه، أو ليس الثوب فَتَخَرَّقَ لم يرجع) وأما في صورة موت العبد ؛ فلأنَّ الملك ينتهي بالموت ؛ لأنه لا يثبت في الحيوان إلا باعتبار حياته، فينتهي بانتهائها، ويثبت امتناع الردّ فيه حكماً للموت لا يفعل المشتري، فلا يمتنع الرجوع. قوله : أو أكل الطعام كله أو بعضه؛ حال كونه في وعاء واحد، فإن كان في وعائين فأكل ما في أحدهما أو باع ثمَّ علم بعيب كل بكل ذلك ، فله ردّ الباقي بحصته من الثمن، صرّح به في «الدر المختار»، وغيره. قوله : لم يرجع ; أما الإعتاق على مال ؛ فلأنَّ المشتري حبس بدله ، وحبس البدل كحبس المبدل، ولو حبس عينه لا يرجع فكذا هذا، وعن أبي حنيفة له، وهو قول أبي يوسف والشافعي وأحمد له : أنه يرجع ؛ لأنه إنهاء الملك وإن كان بعوض فكان كالعتق مجاناً ؛ ولهذا يثبتُ فيه الولاء ; ولأنَّ العوض والمعوض ملكه، فكان كالعتق بلا عوض. والكتابة كالإعتاق على مال الحصول العوض فيها. وأما القتل فالمذكور ظاهرُ الرواية، وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد ه معه في رواية «الينابيع»: أنه يرجع ، وبه قال الشافعي وأحمد ; لأنَّ المقتول مات بأجل فكأنه مات حتف أنفه ; ولأنَّ قتل المولى لا يتعلق به حكم دنيوي من قصاص أو ديةٍ فصار كالموتِ حتف الأنف. وإنما ووجه الظاهر : أنَّ القتل فعل مضمون ; إذ لو باشره في ملك غيرِهِ ضمن ، سقط ضمانه عن المولى لأجل أنه ملكه ، فكان ذلك السقوط في معنى عوض سلم له ، وصار كأنه باعه. وأما أكل الكل ولبسُ الثّوب، فالمذكور هنا قولُ أبي حنيفة له، والقياس أن يرجع بالنقصان، وهو قولهما ومذهب الشافعي وأحمد ، وفي «الخلاصة»: وعليه الفتوى ; لأنه فعل بالطعام والثَّوبِ ما يُقصد بشرائهما ويُعتاد فيهما ، فأشبه الإعتاق ووجه قوله وهو الاستحسان : أن الردَّ تعدَّرَ بفعل مضمون، فأشبه البيع والقتل، وإنما سقط عنه الضمان لأجل أنه ملكه. وأما أكل بعض الطعام فالمذكور قولُ أبي حنيفة له، وهو أنه لا يرجع بنقصان ما أكله، وما بقي اعتباراً بالكل، ولا يردُّ الباقي ; لأنَّ الطّعام كشيء واحد، فلا يردُّ بعضه بالعيب دون البعض، كما لو باع البعض. وعندهما: يرجع بالنقصان في الكلِّ، وليس له أن يرد الباقي ; لأنّ أكل الكل لا يمنع الرجوع عندهما ، فالبعض أولى، والطعام كشيء واحد، فلا يردّ بعضه بالعيب، وعنهما يردّ الباقي ويرجعُ بنقصان ما أكل ; لأنّ التبعيض لا يضره، وهو قادر على الردّ كما قبضه. ولو أطعمه ابنه أو امرأته أو مكاتبه أو ضيفه لا يرجع، ولو أطعمه عبده أو مدبره أو أُمَّ ولده يَرْجِع ; لأن ملكه باق. ولو اشترى دقيقاً فخبز بعضه ثمَّ ظهر أنه من فالمختار أنه يردّ ما بقي ويرجع بنقصان ما خبز. ولو اشترى سمناً ذائباً فأكله ثمَّ أقر البائع أنه كان وقعت فيه فأرة، رجع بالنقصان عندهما، وبه يفتي والأصل أن الردّ متى امتنع الفعلُ من المشتري مضمون كالبيع امتنعَ الرَّجوع بالنقصان ; لأنه إذا كان مضموناً كان البائع ممسكاً للمبيع معنى، ومن شرط الرجوع بالنقصان أن لا يكون ممسكاً له، ومتى امتنع بفعل منه غير مضمون كالإعتاق مجانا ، والتدبير، والاستيلاد، أو بآفة سماوية، أو بانتقاص، أو بازدياد، وما يمنع من الرد لا يمنع الرجوع بالنقصان ; لأنه لا يتصور أن يكون ممسكاً له. كذا في «كمال الدراية). قوله : الحاصل ; وتحريره : أن الملك في الآدمي إنما يثبت باعتبار المالية ، والمالية تنتهي بانتهاء الحياة، فإنّها لا تتحقق بعد الموت، فثبت أنَّ الملك ينتهي بالموت ، والموت لا دخل فيه للمشتري، فيرجعُ بالنقصان دفعاً للضرر عن نفسه. قوله : لا صنع فيه للمشتري ; فإن قيل : إذا صبغ أحمر فامتنع الردّ بفعله، مع أن الصبغ لا يمنع الرجوع؟
And if he frees him in exchange for money, or kills him, or eats all the food or some of it, or wears the garment and it tears, he does not have the right of return. As for the case of the slave's death: because ownership ends with death; it is not established in animals except by consideration of life, so it ends with its termination, and the prevention of return is established as a legal effect of death, not an act of the buyer, so the right to claim compensation is not prevented. His statement: 'or eats all the food or some of it' while it is in one vessel; if it was in two vessels and he ate what was in one of them or sold it, then learned of the defect, he may return the remainder for its share of the price, as stated in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. His statement: 'does not have the right of return': As for freeing in exchange for money: because the buyer detains its equivalent, and detaining the equivalent is like detaining the original; if he detained the original, he would not have the right of return, so similarly here. But from Abu Hanifa, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad: that he does have the right of return; because terminating ownership even if with compensation is like freeing gratis; hence wala' is established therein; and because the compensation and the compensated are his property, so it is like freeing without compensation. And kitabah is like freeing in exchange for money because compensation is obtained therein. As for killing: what is mentioned is the zahir al-riwayah; but from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad — may Allah be pleased with them — in the narration of al-Yanabi': that he does have the right of return; and al-Shafi'i and Ahmad agreed because the killed person died by his appointed term, so it is as if he died naturally; and because the master's killing does not entail any worldly legal consequence of qisas or diyah, so it becomes like natural death. Indeed, the justification of the zahir is that killing is a compensable act; if he committed it in the property of another, he would be liable; his liability is dropped from the master because it is his property; this dropping is in the sense of a compensation surrendered to him, and it becomes as if he sold it. As for eating all and wearing the garment: what is mentioned here is the saying of Abu Hanifa; but the analogy is that he may claim compensation for the defect, which is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the school of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad; and in al-Khulasa: the fatwa is upon it; because he did with the food and garment what is intended by buying them and what is customary with them, so it resembles freeing. And the justification of his saying (Abu Hanifa) is istihsan: that return was precluded by a compensable act, so it resembles sale and killing; the liability is dropped from him only because it is his property. As for eating some food: what is mentioned is the saying of Abu Hanifa: that he does not claim compensation for what he ate, and what remains (he does not return) considering the whole; and he does not return the remainder because the food is like one thing, so he cannot return part of it due to defect without the other part, as if he sold part. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): he may claim compensation for the entire thing, and he may not return the remainder because eating all does not prevent claiming compensation according to them, so a part is more appropriate; but the food is like one thing, so he cannot return part of it due to defect. Another narration from them: he returns the remainder and claims compensation for what he ate because division does not harm him, and he is able to return the remainder as he received it. If he fed it to his son, or wife, or makatib, or guest, he does not have recourse; if he fed it to his slave, or mudabbar, or umm walad, he has recourse because his ownership remains. If he bought flour and baked some of it, then it became apparent that it was defective, the chosen view is that he returns what remains and claims compensation for what he baked. If he bought melted samn (ghee) and ate it, then the seller admitted that a mouse had fallen into it, he claims compensation according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and this is the basis for fatwa. The principle: whenever return is prevented by a compensable act of the buyer similar to sale, the claim for compensation is prevented; because if it is compensable, the seller is effectively holding the sold item, and one condition for claiming compensation is that he is not holding it. Whenever it is prevented by a non-compensable act of his, like freeing gratis, tadbir, istilad, or by a natural calamity, or by decrease or increase, and anything that prevents return does not prevent claiming compensation because he cannot be considered as holding it. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah. His statement: 'the conclusion': and its explanation: ownership in a human being is only established by consideration of monetary value, and monetary value ends with the end of life; it is not realized after death, so it is established that ownership ends with death, and death is not caused by the buyer, so he may claim compensation to ward off harm from himself. His statement: 'the buyer had no hand in it': for if it is said: if he dyed it red, and return is prevented by his act, even though dyeing does not prevent claiming compensation?
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, dealing with khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). The core issue is when a buyer discovers a defect after performing certain actions on the purchased item: does he lose all rights, or can he claim compensation for the defect? The text examines several scenarios: freeing a slave on payment, killing the slave, eating all or part of the food, wearing a garment until it tears. The ruling: in all these cases, the buyer cannot return the item (radd) but may or may not be able to claim compensation (ruju' bi al-nuqsan) depending on the nature of the action. The passage presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), along with comparisons to Shafi'i and Hanbali schools. The underlying principle: if the buyer's action is 'mudman' (compensable, i.e., if done on another's property would require compensation), then the seller is considered to have effectively retained the item, and the right to claim compensation is lost. If the action is not compensable (like freeing gratis, accidental death, natural calamity), then compensation is allowed. However, there are nuances: Abu Hanifa considers eating all food and wearing the garment as non-compensable because they are customary uses, while the Sahibayn consider them compensable because they destroy the item. The text also discusses subcases like eating part of food, feeding others, and processing flour or ghee. The principle is applied consistently: actions that are in the nature of ownership and customary use are treated differently from actions that are destructive. The fatwa is often on the side of the Sahibayn, allowing compensation.

Key Terms
الردّ (al-radd): The right of the buyer to return the defective item to the seller and demand a full refund.
الرجوع بنقصان (al-ruju' bi al-nuqsan): The right of the buyer to claim compensation for the difference in value between the sound price and the defective price, without returning the item.
مضمون (mudman): An act that, if done by a person on the property of another, would require compensation (daman). It is considered a 'compensable' act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on compensation (ruju' bi al-nuqsan) when a buyer frees a defective slave on payment, based on the zahir al-riwayah?
A) The buyer may claim compensation.
B) The buyer has no recourse.
C) The buyer may choose between return and compensation.
D) The buyer must return the slave if possible.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on freeing and killing hinges on whether the buyer's act is considered a compensable disposition or a customary termination; the mufta bihi often allows compensation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

7 min
Eating All the Food and Wearing the Garment: Customary Use vs. Destructive Act
Intra-Hanafi dispute on eating and wearing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa denies compensation for a buyer who consumes all food or wears a garment to destruction, and why the Sahibayn allow it, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Customary use is not compensable according to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn see it as a compensable destruction.

When a buyer eats all the food or wears a garment until it tears, the right of return is lost. But should compensation be available? Abu Hanifa says no – these are acts of ownership. The Sahibayn, Shafi'i, and Ahmad say yes – they are compensable acts. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling: radd is impossible
Once the buyer has consumed all the food or worn the garment to destruction, the item no longer exists in its original state, so return is not possible.
This is agreed upon.
2
State Abu Hanifa's view on compensation
Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer cannot claim compensation for the defect either.
He considers eating and wearing as customary uses, not compensable acts.
3
State the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the buyer may claim compensation for the defect.
They consider these acts as compensable (mudman) because they destroy value.
4
Understand Abu Hanifa's dalil (istihsan)
Abu Hanifa argues that the buyer's use of the food and garment is what they are normally bought for; it is an act of ownership, not a compensable act. The prevention of return is due to a non-compensable act, so compensation is not allowed.
This is analogous to freeing a slave gratis, where compensation is allowed because the act is not mudman.
5
Understand the Sahibayn's dalil (qiyas)
The Sahibayn argue that if the buyer had done this on another's property, he would be liable (daman). Since he did it on his own property, liability drops, but this dropping is in the nature of a compensation to the buyer, making him akin to someone who sold the item, thus barring return but allowing compensation? Wait, actually they allow compensation: the reasoning is that the act is mudman, and the buyer is deemed to have 'held' the item, but since he is not actually holding it, compensation is due. Let me clarify: The text states that the Sahibayn allow compensation because the act is mudman and the buyer is not considered to have held the item? Actually the text says: 'وأما أكل الكل ولبس الثوب فالمذكور هنا قول أبي حنيفة له، والقياس أن يرجع بالنقصان، وهو قولهما ومذهب الشافعي وأحمد، وفي الخلاصة: وعليه الفتوى; لأنه فعل بالطعام والثوب ما يقصد بشرائهما ويعتاد فيهما، فأشبه الإعتاق.' This is confusing: it says 'القياس أن يرجع بالنقصان' meaning the qiyas (analogy) indicates compensation. Then it says Abu Hanifa's opposing view is based on istihsan (الاستحسان). So the Sahibayn follow qiyas, comparing to freeing (where compensation is allowed). Actually, let's read carefully: The text says: 'وعند قوله (أبي حنيفة) وهو الاستحسان : أن الرد تعذر بفعل مضمون، فأشبه البيع والقتل.' Wait, that seems to attribute the istihsan to Abu Hanifa? Let me re-read. Actually the structure: 'وأما أكل الكل ولبس الثوب، فالمذكور هنا قول أبي حنيفة له، والقياس أن يرجع بالنقصان، وهو قولهما ومذهب الشافعي وأحمد، وفي الخلاصة: وعليه الفتوى ; لأنه فعل بالطعام والثوب ما يقصد بشرائهما ويعتاد فيهما، فأشبه الإعتاق ووجه قوله وهو الاستحسان : أن الرد تعذر بفعل مضمون، فأشبه البيع والقتل، وإنما سقط عنه الضمان لأجل أنه ملكه.' So: 'al-qiyas an yarji' bi al-nuqsan' is the view of the two (Sahibayn). Then 'wajh qawlihi wa huwa al-istihsan' is the justification of his (Abu Hanifa's) saying, which is istihsan. So Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to argue that the act is mudman and thus compensation is precluded. The Sahibayn use qiyas to allow compensation. So I need to correct the guided path accordingly.
Corrected: Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to deny compensation; Sahibayn use qiyas to allow it.
