لأنه لا يتصور أن يكون ممسكاً له. كذا في «كمال الدراية). قوله : الحاصل ; وتحريره : أن الملك في الآدمي إنما يثبت باعتبار المالية ، والمالية تنتهي بانتهاء الحياة، فإنّها لا تتحقق بعد الموت، فثبت أنَّ الملك ينتهي بالموت ، والموت لا دخل فيه للمشتري، فيرجعُ بالنقصان دفعاً للضرر عن نفسه. قوله : لا صنع فيه للمشتري ; فإن قيل : إذا صبغ أحمر فامتنع الردّ بفعله، مع أن الصبغ لا يمنع الرجوع؟
And if he frees him in exchange for money, or kills him, or eats all the food or some of it, or wears the garment and it tears, he does not have the right of return. As for the case of the slave's death: because ownership ends with death; it is not established in animals except by consideration of life, so it ends with its termination, and the prevention of return is established as a legal effect of death, not an act of the buyer, so the right to claim compensation is not prevented. His statement: 'or eats all the food or some of it' while it is in one vessel; if it was in two vessels and he ate what was in one of them or sold it, then learned of the defect, he may return the remainder for its share of the price, as stated in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others. His statement: 'does not have the right of return': As for freeing in exchange for money: because the buyer detains its equivalent, and detaining the equivalent is like detaining the original; if he detained the original, he would not have the right of return, so similarly here. But from Abu Hanifa, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad: that he does have the right of return; because terminating ownership even if with compensation is like freeing gratis; hence wala' is established therein; and because the compensation and the compensated are his property, so it is like freeing without compensation. And kitabah is like freeing in exchange for money because compensation is obtained therein. As for killing: what is mentioned is the zahir al-riwayah; but from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad — may Allah be pleased with them — in the narration of al-Yanabi': that he does have the right of return; and al-Shafi'i and Ahmad agreed because the killed person died by his appointed term, so it is as if he died naturally; and because the master's killing does not entail any worldly legal consequence of qisas or diyah, so it becomes like natural death. Indeed, the justification of the zahir is that killing is a compensable act; if he committed it in the property of another, he would be liable; his liability is dropped from the master because it is his property; this dropping is in the sense of a compensation surrendered to him, and it becomes as if he sold it. As for eating all and wearing the garment: what is mentioned here is the saying of Abu Hanifa; but the analogy is that he may claim compensation for the defect, which is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the school of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad; and in al-Khulasa: the fatwa is upon it; because he did with the food and garment what is intended by buying them and what is customary with them, so it resembles freeing. And the justification of his saying (Abu Hanifa) is istihsan: that return was precluded by a compensable act, so it resembles sale and killing; the liability is dropped from him only because it is his property. As for eating some food: what is mentioned is the saying of Abu Hanifa: that he does not claim compensation for what he ate, and what remains (he does not return) considering the whole; and he does not return the remainder because the food is like one thing, so he cannot return part of it due to defect without the other part, as if he sold part. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): he may claim compensation for the entire thing, and he may not return the remainder because eating all does not prevent claiming compensation according to them, so a part is more appropriate; but the food is like one thing, so he cannot return part of it due to defect. Another narration from them: he returns the remainder and claims compensation for what he ate because division does not harm him, and he is able to return the remainder as he received it. If he fed it to his son, or wife, or makatib, or guest, he does not have recourse; if he fed it to his slave, or mudabbar, or umm walad, he has recourse because his ownership remains. If he bought flour and baked some of it, then it became apparent that it was defective, the chosen view is that he returns what remains and claims compensation for what he baked. If he bought melted samn (ghee) and ate it, then the seller admitted that a mouse had fallen into it, he claims compensation according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and this is the basis for fatwa. The principle: whenever return is prevented by a compensable act of the buyer similar to sale, the claim for compensation is prevented; because if it is compensable, the seller is effectively holding the sold item, and one condition for claiming compensation is that he is not holding it. Whenever it is prevented by a non-compensable act of his, like freeing gratis, tadbir, istilad, or by a natural calamity, or by decrease or increase, and anything that prevents return does not prevent claiming compensation because he cannot be considered as holding it. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah. His statement: 'the conclusion': and its explanation: ownership in a human being is only established by consideration of monetary value, and monetary value ends with the end of life; it is not realized after death, so it is established that ownership ends with death, and death is not caused by the buyer, so he may claim compensation to ward off harm from himself. His statement: 'the buyer had no hand in it': for if it is said: if he dyed it red, and return is prevented by his act, even though dyeing does not prevent claiming compensation?
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, dealing with khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). The core issue is when a buyer discovers a defect after performing certain actions on the purchased item: does he lose all rights, or can he claim compensation for the defect? The text examines several scenarios: freeing a slave on payment, killing the slave, eating all or part of the food, wearing a garment until it tears. The ruling: in all these cases, the buyer cannot return the item (radd) but may or may not be able to claim compensation (ruju' bi al-nuqsan) depending on the nature of the action. The passage presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), along with comparisons to Shafi'i and Hanbali schools. The underlying principle: if the buyer's action is 'mudman' (compensable, i.e., if done on another's property would require compensation), then the seller is considered to have effectively retained the item, and the right to claim compensation is lost. If the action is not compensable (like freeing gratis, accidental death, natural calamity), then compensation is allowed. However, there are nuances: Abu Hanifa considers eating all food and wearing the garment as non-compensable because they are customary uses, while the Sahibayn consider them compensable because they destroy the item. The text also discusses subcases like eating part of food, feeding others, and processing flour or ghee. The principle is applied consistently: actions that are in the nature of ownership and customary use are treated differently from actions that are destructive. The fatwa is often on the side of the Sahibayn, allowing compensation.

Key Terms
الردّ (al-radd): The right of the buyer to return the defective item to the seller and demand a full refund.
الرجوع بنقصان (al-ruju' bi al-nuqsan): The right of the buyer to claim compensation for the difference in value between the sound price and the defective price, without returning the item.
مضمون (mudman): An act that, if done by a person on the property of another, would require compensation (daman). It is considered a 'compensable' act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis for the Sahibayn allowing compensation when a buyer eats all the food?
A) The act is not compensable because it is customary.
B) The act is compensable, but the liability drop is treated as a deemed sale barring compensation.
C) The act is similar to freeing, which allows compensation.
D) The buyer did not act intentionally.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on eating all and wearing turns on whether the act is deemed a compensable destruction (Abu Hanifa) or a customary use (Sahibayn); fatwa favors compensation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Partial Consumption and Extensions: Feeding Others and Processed Food
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partial consumption and extensions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings when a buyer eats only part of defective food, and when the buyer feeds others, and apply the principle of compensable acts to these extensions.

Core Idea
Partial consumption splits the remedy: Abu Hanifa denies compensation for the eaten part; the Sahibayn allow full compensation.

What if the buyer eats only part of the food? Or feeds it to family or slaves? This lesson extends the principle to partial consumption, processed food (baked flour, melted ghee), and the impact of feeding others. The same dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn persists, with fatwa often favoring compensation.

Guided Path
1
Recall the rule for eating all
Abu Hanifa denies compensation for eating all; Sahibayn allow it.
This sets the baseline.
2
Apply to partial consumption: Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa says: no compensation for the part eaten, and the remainder cannot be returned. The food is considered one unit, so partial return is not allowed.
This is consistent with his view on eating all.
3
Apply to partial consumption: Sahibayn's view
Sahibayn say: the buyer may claim compensation for the entire food (including the eaten part) and cannot return the remainder. An alternative narration allows returning the remainder and claiming compensation for the eaten part.
They are more flexible.
4
Examine feeding others
If the buyer feeds his son, wife, makatib, or guest, he does not claim compensation (Abu Hanifa's view? Actually the text says 'لا يرجع' regardless of whose act? Let's check: The text says 'ولو أطعمه ابنه أو امرأته أو مكاتبه أو ضيفه لا يرجع' – he does not have recourse. So if he feeds dependents, it's like his own consumption? But if he feeds his own slave, mudabbar, or umm walad, he does have recourse because his ownership remains.
The distinction is based on whether the recipient is owned by the buyer or not.
5
Apply to processed food (flour, ghee)
If he buys flour, bakes some of it, then discovers the defect, the chosen view is he returns the unbaked flour and claims compensation for the baked portion. For melted ghee with a mouse, the buyer claims compensation (Sahibayn's view is mufta bihi).
These applications show the leniency in fatwa.

لأنَّ الطّعام كشيء واحد، فلا يردُّ بعضه بالعيب دون البعض، كما لو باع البعض. وعندهما: يرجع بالنقصان في الكلِّ، وليس له أن يرد الباقي ; لأنّ أكل الكل لا يمنع الرجوع عندهما ، فالبعض أولى، والطعام كشيء واحد، فلا يردّ بعضه بالعيب، وعنهما يردّ الباقي ويرجعُ بنقصان ما أكل ; لأنّ التبعيض لا يضره، وهو قادر على الردّ كما قبضه. ولو أطعمه ابنه أو امرأته أو مكاتبه أو ضيفه لا يرجع، ولو أطعمه عبده أو مدبره أو أُمَّ ولده يَرْجِع ; لأن ملكه باق. ولو اشترى دقيقاً فخبز بعضه ثمَّ ظهر أنه من فالمختار أنه يردّ ما بقي ويرجع بنقصان ما خبز. ولو اشترى سمناً ذائباً فأكله ثمَّ أقر البائع أنه كان وقعت فيه فأرة، رجع بالنقصان عندهما، وبه يفتي والأصل أن الردّ متى امتنع الفعلُ من المشتري مضمون كالبيع امتنعَ الرَّجوع بالنقصان ; Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, dealing with khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). The core issue is when a buyer discovers a defect after performing certain actions on the purchased item: does he lose all rights, or can he claim compensation for the defect? The text examines several scenarios: freeing a slave on payment, killing the slave, eating all or part of the food, wearing a garment until it tears. The ruling: in all these cases, the buyer cannot return the item (radd) but may or may not be able to claim compensation (ruju' bi al-nuqsan) depending on the nature of the action. The passage presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), along with comparisons to Shafi'i and Hanbali schools. The underlying principle: if the buyer's action is 'mudman' (compensable, i.e., if done on another's property would require compensation), then the seller is considered to have effectively retained the item, and the right to claim compensation is lost. If the action is not compensable (like freeing gratis, accidental death, natural calamity), then compensation is allowed. However, there are nuances: Abu Hanifa considers eating all food and wearing the garment as non-compensable because they are customary uses, while the Sahibayn consider them compensable because they destroy the item. The text also discusses subcases like eating part of food, feeding others, and processing flour or ghee. The principle is applied consistently: actions that are in the nature of ownership and customary use are treated differently from actions that are destructive. The fatwa is often on the side of the Sahibayn, allowing compensation.

Key Terms
الردّ (al-radd): The right of the buyer to return the defective item to the seller and demand a full refund.
الرجوع بنقصان (al-ruju' bi al-nuqsan): The right of the buyer to claim compensation for the difference in value between the sound price and the defective price, without returning the item.
مضمون (mudman): An act that, if done by a person on the property of another, would require compensation (daman). It is considered a 'compensable' act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling when a buyer feeds defective food to his own slave?
A) The buyer has no recourse for compensation.
B) The buyer may claim compensation for the food fed to the slave.
C) The buyer may return the slave?
D) The buyer must compensate the seller.

Key Takeaway
Partial consumption, feeding dependents, and processing food all follow the same principles; mufta bihi often favors compensation where the buyer's act is not deemed a complete bar.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Buyer's Actions and Recourse for Defect: Guarantor vs Non-Guarantor
Rulings on khiyar al-'ayb after buyer acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between guarantor (mamdūn) and non-guarantor actions of the buyer and determine the effect on the right of recourse for defect (ruju' bi nuqsan al-'ayb) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Only guarantor acts forfeit recourse; non-guarantor acts preserve it, even if return is impossible.

This passage from the Book of Sale examines how the buyer's actions after purchase affect his right to claim compensation for a defect when he cannot return the item. The Hanafi school distinguishes between actions that make the buyer a guarantor (mamdūn) and those that do not. We will explore the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on several scenarios, and identify the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the effect of the buyer's post-purchase action (e.g., eating, feeding, freeing) on his right to ruju' bi nuqsan al-'ayb when the item cannot be returned.
The issue arises because the return is prevented by the buyer's own act.
2
Classify the buyer's action
Determine if the action is 'mamdūn' (guarantor) like killing or selling, or 'ghayr mamdūn' (non-guarantor) like eating, feeding a slave, or freeing before defect discovery.
This classification is key: guarantor actions block both return and recourse.
3
Distinguish external vs buyer-caused prevention
If the item perishes by an external cause (e.g., death of a slave), recourse remains because the buyer had no hand in it. This is contrasted with buyer-caused prevention.
Death is not an act of the buyer, so recourse is unaffected.
4
Apply to disputed scenarios
Work through the text's examples: eating part of food, feeding dependents vs slaves, baking flour, eating melted butter, freeing a slave. For each, apply the principle and note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
Abu Hanifa often takes a stricter view on indivisibility; the disciples take a more lenient view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the fatwa is given according to the disciples' view (e.g., on melted butter). Recognize the operative ruling.
The later Hanafi fatwa practice often follows Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

الحاصل أنَّ الموت لا يُبْطِلُ الرُّجوع بنقصان العيب ؛ لأَنَّهُ لا صنعَ للمشتري فيه(٣) الفتوى ؛ لأنه فعل بالطعام والثَّوبِ ما يُقصد بشرائهما ويُعتاد فيهما ، فأشبه الإعتاق ووجه قوله وهو الاستحسان : أن الردَّ تعدَّرَ بفعل مضمون، فأشبه البيع والقتل، وإنما سقط عنه الضمان لأجل أنه ملكه. وأما أكل بعض الطعام فالمذكور قولُ أبي حنيفة له، وهو أنه لا يرجع بنقصان ما أكله، وما بقي اعتباراً بالكل، ولا يردُّ الباقي ؛ لأنَّ الطّعام كشيء واحد، فلا يردُّ بعضه بالعيب دون البعض، كما لو باع البعض. وعندهما: يرجع بالنقصان في الكلِّ، وليس له أن يرد الباقي ؛ لأنّ أكل الكل لا يمنع الرجوع عندهما ، فالبعض أولى، والطعام كشيء واحد، فلا يردّ بعضه بالعيب، وعنهما يردّ الباقي ويرجعُ بنقصان ما أكل ؛ لأنّ التبعيض لا يضره، وهو قادر على الردّ كما قبضه. ولو أطعمه ابنه أو امرأته أو مكاتبه أو ضيفه لا يرجع، ولو أطعمه عبده أو مدبره أو أُمَّ ولده يَرْجِع ؛ لأن ملكه باق. ولو اشترى دقيقاً فخبز بعضه ثمَّ ظهر أنه من فالمختار أنه يردّ ما بقي ويرجع بنقصان ما خبز. ولو اشترى سمناً ذائباً فأكله ثمَّ أقر البائع أنه كان وقعت فيه فأرة، رجع بالنقصان عندهما، وبه يفتي والأصل أن الردَّ متى امتنع الفعلُ من المشتري مضمون كالبيع امتنعَ الرَّجوع بالنقصان ؛ لأنه إذا كان مضموناً كان البائع ممسكاً للمبيع معنى، ومن شرط الرجوع بالنقصان أن لا يكون ممسكاً له، ومتى امتنع بفعل منه غير مضمون كالإعتاق مجانا ، والتدبير، والاستيلاد، أو بآفة سماوية، أو بانتقاص، أو بازدياد، وما يمنع من الرد لا يمنع الرجوع بالنقصان ؛ لأنه لا يتصور أن يكون ممسكاً له. كذا في «كمال الدراية). [١]قوله : الحاصل ؛ وتحريره : أن الملك في الآدمي إنما يثبت باعتبار المالية ، والمالية تنتهي بانتهاء الحياة، فإنّها لا تتحقق بعد الموت، فثبت أنَّ الملك ينتهي بالموت ، والموت لا دخل فيه للمشتري، فيرجعُ بالنقصان دفعاً للضرر عن نفسه. [٢] قوله : لا صنع فيه للمشتري ؛ فإن قيل : إذا صبغ أحمر فامتنع الردّ بفعله، مع أنه لا صنع فيه للمشتري. أجيب : بأن امتناع الردّ في الثوب المصبوغ ليس بسبب نفس الصبغ، بل بوجود الزيادة، فكان الامتناع لحق الشرع، وهو شبهة الربا. [١) قوله : والقياس ... الخ ؛ تقريره : إنَّ المشتري لما أعتقه امتنع عن الرد، والامتناع بفعله يمنع الرجوع، فصار كالقتل، فإنّه إذا اشترى عبداً فقتله ثمَّ اطَّلع فيه على عيب فليس له الرجوع ، فكذا هنا. [۲] قوله : وجه الاستحسان....الخ ؛ بيانه أن للإعتاق اعتبارين : ١. باعتبار يشبه بالقتل. ٢. وباعتبار يشبه بالموت. أما الاعتبار الأول : فصدوره لفعله. وأما الاعتبار الثاني : فهو أنَّ الأصل في الآدمي الحرية ؛ لأنه لم يخلق في أصل الخلقة محلاً للملك ، وإنما يثبت الملك فيه مؤقتاً إلى الإعتاق، والمؤقت إلى وقت ينتهي بانتهائه، وكان الملك مؤقتاً إلى زمان العتق ، فلمّا عُتِقَ عاد إلى حالته الأصلية التي تقتضيها الفطرة الإنسانية، وهو عدم كونه مملوكاً لأحد. فلما أعتقه المشتري بعد رؤية العيب فاعتبر ذلك - الاعتبار الأول - ولا يمكن له الرجوع بنقصانه، فإنَّ التصرُّف بعد رؤية العيب، وامتناعه عن الردّ يسقط الرجوع. وإذا أعتقه قبل رؤية العيب فاعتبر هذا - الاعتبار الثاني – ويثبت للمشتري حق الرجوع بخلاف الموت، فإنه لَمَّا لم يثبت فيه اختيار لأحد، فوقوعه قبل رؤية العيب وبعدها سواء في ثبوت حق الرجوع للمشتري.
The conclusion is that death does not invalidate the recourse for the deficiency of the defect, because the buyer had no hand in it. The fatwa is (that he does not return) because he acted upon the food and clothing in a manner that is intended by their purchase and customary, so it resembles manumission. And the basis of his statement is istihsan: that the return became impossible by a guarantor action (damān), so it resembles sale and killing, and only the guarantee dropped from him because it is his property. As for eating some food, the mentioned statement of Abu Hanifa is that he does not return for the deficiency of what he ate, and what remains is regarded as the whole, and he does not return the remaining (part) because food is like one thing, so he cannot return part of it due to a defect without the other part, like if he sold part. According to the two (disciples): he returns for the deficiency in the whole, and he may not return the remaining; because eating the whole does not prevent recourse according to them, so a part is even more (entitled), and food is like one thing, so he does not return part due to a defect. And according to them (another narration): he returns the remaining and recovers the deficiency of what he ate, because partiality does not harm him, and he is able to return as he took possession. If he fed his son, his wife, his mukatab, or his guest, he does not return. If he fed his slave, his mudabbar, or his umm walad, he returns, because his ownership remains. If he bought flour and baked part of it, then it appeared that it was (defective), the chosen position is that he returns what remains and recovers the deficiency of what he baked. If he bought melted butter and ate it, then the seller admitted that a mouse had fallen into it, he recovers the deficiency according to the two, and by this fatwa is given. The principle is that whenever return becomes impossible due to an action of the buyer that is a guarantor (like sale), the recourse for deficiency is prevented; because when it is a guarantor, the seller is effectively holding the sold item, and one of the conditions of recourse for deficiency is that he not be holding it. Whenever it becomes impossible by a non-guarantor action of his, like freeing for free, tadbir, istilad, or by a heavenly calamity, or by decrease, or by increase, and what prevents return does not prevent recourse for deficiency, because he cannot be imagined to be holding it. Thus in 'Kamal al-Diraya'. [1] His statement: 'The conclusion'; its clarification: that ownership in a human is established only in consideration of pecuniary value, and pecuniary value ends with the end of life, for it is not realized after death. So it is established that ownership ends with death, and death the buyer has no hand in it, so he recovers for the deficiency in order to remove harm from himself. [2] His statement: 'The buyer had no hand in it'; for if it is said: if he dyed it red, then return becomes impossible by his action, despite that he had no hand in it. The answer is that the impossibility of return in the dyed cloth is not because of the dye itself, but because of the existence of increase, so the prevention is due to a right of the Shariah, which is the suspicion of riba. [1] His statement: 'The analogy...'; its exposition: that when the buyer freed (the slave), return was prevented, and prevention by his action prevents recourse, so it became like killing; for if he bought a slave and killed him, then discovered a defect in him, he has no recourse, so here too. [2] His statement: 'The basis of istihsan...'; its explanation: that manumission has two aspects: 1. An aspect resembling killing. 2. An aspect resembling death. As for the first aspect: it is by his action. As for the second aspect: it is that the original in humans is freedom, for he was not created in the original creation as a place for ownership; rather ownership is established in him temporarily until manumission, and a temporary thing ends at its termination, and ownership was temporary until the time of freedom. When he was freed, he returned to his original state which the human nature requires, which is not being owned by anyone. When the buyer freed him after seeing the defect, then that first aspect is considered, and he cannot recover for its deficiency, for the disposition after seeing the defect and his prevention of return drops the recourse. If he freed him before seeing the defect, then this second aspect is considered, and the buyer has the right of recourse, unlike death, for since no one's choice is involved in it, its occurrence before or after seeing the defect is the same in establishing the buyer's right of recourse.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Sale, chapter on the Option of Defect (Khiyar al-'Ayb). It is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of the buyer's actions on his right to claim compensation for a defect (ruju' bi nuqsan al-'ayb) when he cannot return the item. The discussion begins with the ruling that if the sold item perishes (e.g., the slave dies) by a cause not attributable to the buyer, the buyer still has the right to claim the difference in value due to the defect. This is because death is not an act of the buyer. The fatwa given is that if the buyer acts upon the item in a customary manner (e.g., using the food or cloth), his action resembles manumission (freeing a slave) in that it is a beneficial act, and thus he retains the right of recourse. The basis of this is istihsan (juristic preference), which overrides qiyas. The qiyas would be that since the buyer's action caused the impossibility of return (like killing a slave), he should lose the right of recourse. However, istihsan distinguishes between acts that are guarantor (like killing or selling) and those that are not (like eating or using). The text then details the specific ruling of Abu Hanifa on eating part of the food: he does not claim compensation for what he ate, and does not return the remainder because food is considered a single unit. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: according to one narration, he claims compensation for the whole and does not return the remainder; according to another, he returns the remainder and claims compensation for what he ate. Next, the passage discusses feeding others: if the buyer feeds his dependent (son, wife, mukatab, or guest), he loses the right of recourse because they are not his property; but if he feeds his slave, mudabbar, or umm walad, he retains it because ownership remains. For flour baked into bread, the chosen position is that he returns the unbaked portion and claims compensation for the baked portion. For melted butter consumed after the seller admits a mouse fell in, the two disciples grant recourse, and the fatwa is given accordingly. The text then states a principle: whenever return is prevented by a guarantor action of the buyer (like sale or killing), recourse for deficiency is also prevented, because the seller is deemed to be holding the item. But if prevention is by a non-guarantor action (like freeing a slave for free, tadbir, istilad) or by a heavenly calamity, natural decrease or increase, the right of recourse remains because the buyer is not holding the item. The commentary then explains the reasoning: ownership of a human is by value, which ends at death, so death is not the buyer's act. It addresses an objection about dyeing a cloth (which prevents return due to increase, not the act itself). It then elaborates on manumission: it has two aspects—like killing (by the buyer's act) and like death (return to original freedom). If manumission occurs after seeing the defect, the killing aspect prevails and recourse is lost; if before seeing, the freedom aspect prevails and recourse remains, unlike death where timing is irrelevant.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of the buyer to return a defective item or claim compensation for the defect (difference in value).
الرجوع بنقصان العيب (ruju' bi nuqsan al-'ayb): The buyer's right to claim from the seller the difference between the price paid and the value of the defective item.
مضمون (mamdūn): An action that makes the buyer a guarantor (dāmin) of the item, such as selling or killing; it prevents both return and recourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a buyer frees a slave before seeing the defect, what is the ruling on his right to claim compensation for the defect?
A) He loses the right because freeing is similar to killing.
B) He retains the right because freeing is similar to death, and the buyer had no hand in the original freedom.
C) He loses the right only if the defect was apparent at the time of freeing.
D) He retains the right only if the slave was a mudabbar.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mu'amalat, the buyer's right to compensation for a defect (ruju' bi nuqsan) is only forfeited by guarantor actions; non-guarantor acts, customary use, and external causes preserve the right even when return is impossible.

Freeing a Slave Before Defect Discovery
Hanafi ruling on freeing a slave after purchase before defect discovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on freeing a slave before defect discovery and explain the istihsan reasoning that distinguishes it from killing.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave before discovering a defect resembles death, so the buyer retains the right to claim compensation.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the Hanafi school on how a buyer's act of freeing a purchased slave affects the right to return the slave due to a defect. The passage presents a conflict between qiyas (analogy to killing) and istihsan (juristic preference based on the original freedom of humans). Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct ruling in sales transactions involving defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is the buyer freeing a purchased slave before discovering a defect. The ruling is that the buyer retains the right to return the slave and claim compensation for the defect (by istihsan).
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Qiyas equates freeing to killing because both are actions of the buyer that prevent return. In killing, the right to return is lost; thus, freeing should also void the right.
This is the default analogy (qiyas jaliy).
3
Understand the istihsan argument
Istihsan overrides qiyas because freeing has two aspects: it resembles killing (buyer's action) and resembles death (return to original freedom). Before defect discovery, the resemblance to death prevails, preserving the right to return.
The original freedom of humans is a stronger consideration.
4
Apply the distinction based on timing
If freeing occurs after seeing the defect, the resemblance to killing prevails and the right is lost. If before, the resemblance to death prevails and the right remains.
Timing is the key differentiator.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school holds that any disposition by the buyer (including freeing) bars return entirely, regardless of timing. The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi).
Practical consequence: in Hanafi, buyer can claim compensation.

Source Text

والإعتاق مجاناً لا يُبْطِلُهُ أيضاً استحساناً ، والقياس ) أَنَّهُ يُبْطِلُه ؛ لأنَّ الإعتاق بصنعِهِ فصار كالقتل ، وجه الاستحسان" أن الإعتاق له شبهان :
شبه بالقتل في أَنَّهُ بصنع المشتري. وشبة بالموت في أنَّ الأصل في الآدمي الحريَّة، فكان الملك مؤقتاً إلى زمانِ العتق ، فهو عود إلى الحالة الأصلية، فإن كان بعد رؤية العيب اعتبر ذلك الشبه ، فلا رجوع له بخلاف الموت بعد رؤية العيب، فإنَّ حق الرجوع فيه ثابت وإن كان قبل رؤية العيب اعتبر هذا الشَّبه حتى يكون له فيه حق الرجوع، وأما المسائل الأخر فلا رجوع بالنقصان فيها .
أنه يرجع بالنقصان. أجيب : بأن امتناع الردّ في الثوب المصبوغ ليس بسبب نفس الصبغ، بل بوجود
الزيادة، فكان الامتناع لحق الشرع، وهو شبهة الربا. [١) قوله : والقياس ... الخ ؛ تقريره : إنَّ المشتري لما أعتقه امتنع عن الرد، والامتناع بفعله يمنع الرجوع، فصار كالقتل، فإنّه إذا اشترى عبداً فقتله ثمَّ اطَّلع فيه
على عيب فليس له الرجوع ، فكذا هنا.
[۲] قوله : وجه الاستحسان....الخ ؛ بيانه أن للإعتاق اعتبارين :
۱. باعتبار يشبه بالقتل.
. وباعتبار يشبه بالموت.
أما الاعتبار الأول : فصدوره لفعله.
وأما الاعتبار الثاني : فهو أنَّ الأصل في الآدمي الحرية ؛ لأنه لم يخلق في أصل الخلقة محلاً للملك ، وإنما يثبت الملك فيه مؤقتاً إلى الإعتاق، والمؤقت إلى وقت ينتهي بانتهائه، وكان الملك مؤقتاً إلى زمان العتق ، فلمّا عُتِقَ عاد إلى حالته الأصلية التي تقتضيها الفطرة الإنسانية، وهو عدم كونه مملوكاً لأحد.
فلما أعتقه المشتري بعد رؤية العيب فاعتبر ذلك - الاعتبار الأول - ولا يمكن له الرجوع بنقصانه، فإنَّ التصرُّف بعد رؤية العيب، وامتناعه عن الردّ يسقط الرجوع. وإذا أعتقه قبل رؤية العيب فاعتبر هذا - الاعتبار الثاني – ويثبت للمشتري حق الرجوع بخلاف الموت، فإنه لَمَّا لم يثبت فيه اختيار لأحد، فوقوعه قبل رؤية العيب وبعدها سواء في ثبوت حق الرجوع للمشتري.
And freeing (a slave) without compensation does not invalidate (the right of return) also, by istihsan, while qiyas is that it does invalidate, because the freeing is by his action, so it becomes like killing. The face of istihsan: freeing has two aspects: an aspect similar to killing in that it is by the buyer's action, and an aspect similar to death in that the original state of a human is freedom, so ownership was temporary until the time of freeing, and it is a return to the original state. So if (freeing is) after seeing the defect, that aspect (similar to killing) is considered, and he has no recourse, unlike death after seeing the defect, for the right of recourse is established. And if it is before seeing the defect, this aspect (similar to death) is considered, so that he has the right of recourse. And as for other issues, there is no recourse for the deficiency. That he returns by the deficiency. It was answered: the prohibition of return in the dyed cloth is not because of the dyeing itself, but because of the existence of the increase, so the prohibition is for the right of Shari'ah, which is the suspicion of riba. [1] His statement: 'and qiyas...' its summary: The buyer when he freed it prevented return, and prevention by his action prevents recourse, so it became like killing. For if he bought a slave and killed him, then discovered a defect, he has no recourse, so here as well. [2] His statement: 'the face of istihsan...' its explanation: Freeing has two considerations: 1. By consideration it resembles killing. 2. By consideration it resembles death. As for the first consideration: it occurs by his action. As for the second: the original state of a human is freedom, because he was not created in the original creation as a locus for ownership, but ownership is established temporarily until freeing, and the temporary ends at its limit, and ownership was temporary until the time of freeing, so when he was freed, he returned to his original state required by human nature, which is not being owned by anyone. So when the buyer freed him after seeing the defect, that first consideration is taken, and he cannot have recourse for the deficiency, for the disposition after seeing the defect and his prevention of return drops the recourse. And if he freed him before seeing the defect, this second consideration is taken, and the right of recourse is established for the buyer, unlike death, for since no choice is established for anyone, its occurrence before seeing the defect and after are equal in establishing the buyer's right of recourse.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the effect of a buyer freeing a purchased slave before discovering a defect. The author presents the qiyas (analogy) argument that freeing should invalidate the right to return the slave due to defect, comparing it to killing the slave. Then he presents the istihsan (juristic preference) argument that distinguishes based on two aspects: freeing resembles killing (by the buyer's action) and resembles death (return to original freedom). If the buyer frees after seeing the defect, the resemblance to killing prevails, and the right to return is lost. If before seeing the defect, the resemblance to death prevails, and the buyer retains the right to return and claim compensation for the defect. The commentary expands on these two considerations, emphasizing that the original state of a human is freedom, so ownership is temporary. An objection regarding dyed cloth is addressed: the prohibition of return there is due to an increase creating a suspicion of riba, not the action itself.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger consideration, here the original freedom of humans.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that equates freeing to killing on the basis that both are the buyer's actions.
شبه (shabah): Analogical resemblance; here freeing has two shabah: to killing and to death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a buyer's act of freeing a purchased slave invalidate the right to claim compensation for a defect?
A) Always invalidates, regardless of timing.
B) Invalidates only if done after discovering the defect.
C) Invalidates only if done before discovering the defect.
D) Never invalidates; the right remains permanently.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, via istihsan, distinguishes between freeing before and after defect discovery: before preserves the right to claim compensation; after forfeits it.

Breaking Perishable Items Before Discovering Defect
Ruling on compensation for broken defective perishable goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer breaks a perishable item before discovering a defect, distinguish between usable and unusable items, and explain the Hanafi rebuttal to the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Breaking a defective item before knowledge does not forfeit compensation: you get the price difference if usable, full refund if unusable.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling on the sale of perishable goods. When a buyer breaks open an egg or watermelon before knowing it is defective, the ruling depends on whether the item still has any utility. The commentary explores the legal reasoning and the disagreement with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Two Scenarios
The buyer breaks a perishable item (e.g., egg, watermelon) before discovering a hidden defect. Two outcomes: the item is still usable (e.g., edible by some people/animals) or completely unusable.
The break is a new defect caused by the buyer; the original defect existed at sale.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling for Each Scenario
If the item is still usable, the buyer cannot return it (due to the new defect) but can claim compensation for the deficiency (difference in value caused by the original defect). If completely unusable, the sale is void and the buyer gets a full refund.
The key is whether the item qualifies as 'mal' (wealth) — it must have some utility.
3
Locate the Dalil
The Hanafi proof uses analogy (qiyas) to a buyer who cuts a cloth and then finds a defect: the buyer cannot return the cloth but can claim compensation. The 'illa (effective cause) is that the buyer's action happened in his ownership, so it does not bar recourse for the original defect.
The text also references the definition of mal: 'al-mal ma yuntafa'u bihi'.
4
Follow the Shafi'i Objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the buyer can return the broken item because the seller's permission to break (implied by the nature of the sale) means the seller accepted the break.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf between the schools.
5
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi rebuttal: the seller's permission was given when the item was already in the buyer's ownership (after sale), so the seller has no authority over the item. The buyer's action is like cutting a cloth he owns — he cannot then return the damaged item, but he can still claim for the original defect.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that the Hanafi school keeps the transaction intact with compensation, while the Shafi'i school allows return with compensation for the break.

وإن اشترى بيضاً، أو بطيخاً، أو قثاء، أو خياراً، أو جوزاً، فكسر، فوجده فاسداً فله نقصانُهُ في المُنْتَفَع به، وكلُّ ثَمَنِهِ في غيره. [1]قوله : وإن اشترى...الخ ؛ توضيحه : أنه إذا اشترى أحد شيئاً من هذه الأشياء المذكورة فكسره فوجده فاسداً، بأن كان مُرّاً أو متناً، وهذا الكسر كان قبل العلم بالعيب ؛ لأنه إن كان بعد العلم به فكان دليل الرضاء، فهذا بمكسور لا يخلو إما أن ينتفع به بأن يصلح لأكل بعض الناس أو الدواب، أو لا. ففي الصورة الأولى : رجع بنقصانه دفعاً للضرر بقدر الإمكان، وليس له أن يرده ؛ لأن الكسر عيب حادث، إلا أن يقبله البائع مكسوراً، أو يرد الثمن، وقال الشافعي الله : يردُّه ؛ لأنّ الكسر وإن كان عيباً حادثاً لكنه بتسليط البائع ، فصار كأنه فعله ورضي به. ولنا : أنَّ التسليط على الكسر في ملك المشتري لا في ملك البائع ؛ لأنه بالبيع لم يبق ملكه، فلم يكن التسليط إلا في ملك المشتري ، وذلك هدر لعدم ولايته عليه ، فصار كما إذا اشترى ثوباً فقطعه، ثم وجده معيباً، فله الرجوع اتفاقاً. وفي الصورة الثانية : وهي ما إذا كان بحيث لم يصلح لأكل الناس ولا للعلف يرجع بالثمن كله ؛ لأنه ليس بمال ، فإنّ المال ما ينتفع به في الحال أو المال، فإذا كان هذا الشيء بحيث لا ينتفع به أصلاً فلا يكون محلاً للبيع، فبطل البيع.
And if one buys eggs, or watermelons, or cucumbers, or cucumbers (another type?), or walnuts, and breaks them and finds them defective, then he has its deficiency in what is benefited from (i.e., the difference in value if still usable), and all of its price in other than that (i.e., if not usable). [1] His statement: 'And if one buys...' its clarification: That if someone buys one of these mentioned things and breaks it and finds it defective, such as being bitter or rotten, and this breaking was before knowledge of the defect; because if after knowledge, it would be evidence of satisfaction. So this broken thing is either such that it can be benefited from, by being fit for eating by some people or animals, or not. In the first case: he claims its deficiency to remove harm as much as possible, and he cannot return it because the break is a new defect, unless the seller accepts it broken, or returns the price. And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: he returns it, because the break, although a new defect, was by the seller's permission, so it becomes as if he did it and was satisfied. And our proof: The permission to break is in the buyer's ownership, not the seller's, because by sale the seller's ownership ceased, so the permission was only in the buyer's ownership, and that is of no effect due to lack of authority over it, so it is like when one buys a cloth and cuts it, then finds it defective, he has recourse by agreement. In the second case: which is when it is not fit for human consumption nor animal feed, he claims the full price because it is not wealth (mal), for wealth is what is benefitted from now or potentially. So if this thing cannot be benefitted from at all, it is not a valid subject of sale, so the sale is void.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof regarding the buyer who breaks a perishable item (like eggs, watermelons, cucumbers, walnuts) before knowing it was defective. The ruling distinguishes two scenarios: (1) If the broken item is still usable (e.g., fit for some people or animals), the buyer can only claim the difference in value due to the original defect, but cannot return the item because his act of breaking created a new defect. (2) If the item is completely unusable (not fit for anyone or animals), the sale is void and the buyer gets the full refund because the item is not considered wealth (mal). The commentary explains the reasoning: breaking after knowledge shows acceptance; breaking before knowledge is akin to cutting a cloth. The Shafi'i school's view is that the buyer can return it because the seller's permission to break implies acceptance, but the Hanafi rebuttal is that the permission was given when the item was in the buyer's ownership, so the seller has no authority over it. The ruling is based on the principle that the buyer's action before knowledge of defect does not bar recourse for the defect but limits it to compensation rather than return.

Key Terms
منتفع به (muntafa' bihi): An item that has some utility, even if imperfect, such as being edible by some people or animals.
عيب حادث ('ayb hadith): A new defect that occurs after the sale, here caused by the buyer's action of breaking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer buys a watermelon, cuts it open before knowing it is rotten inside, and finds it completely inedible. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The buyer can return the watermelon and get a full refund.
B) The buyer can only get the difference in value.
C) The buyer cannot get anything because he cut it.
D) The buyer must keep the watermelon with no recourse.

Key Takeaway
When a buyer breaks a perishable item before discovering a defect, the remedy depends on usability: compensation for deficiency if usable, full refund if not; the buyer cannot return the item due to the new defect.

Passing Back a Defect Return After Resale
Ruling on passing back a defect claim when the buyer has resold

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions under which a first buyer can return a defect-returned item to his original seller.

Core Idea
Only a judicial decree triggers the chain; voluntary returns stop it.

What happens when a buyer resells an item, and then the second buyer returns it due to a defect? Can the first buyer in turn return it to his original seller? The Hanafi school distinguishes between returns ordered by a judge and returns by mutual consent. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its practical application in sale (buyu') transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key parties and sequence
There are three parties: original seller (S), first buyer (B1), and second buyer (B2). B1 buys from S, then sells to B2. B2 returns the item to B1 due to a defect. The question: can B1 return it to S?
2
Determine the basis of the return to B1
The ruling hinges on how B2's return to B1 occurred. If it was by judicial decree (qada) based on confession (iqrar), evidence (bayyinah), or refusal to swear (nukul), B1 can pass it back to S. If B2 returns it voluntarily (bi-ridahu), B1 cannot.
3
Apply the condition to the returned item's usability
Even when B1 can return, if the item is still usable despite the defect (e.g., as fodder or for the poor), B1 must keep it and can only claim compensation from S for the defect. If it is completely unusable, the original sale is void and B1 gets the full price.
This is from the commentary; the main text only covers the pass-back condition.
4
Identify the legal rationale
The rationale: a judicial decree makes the return legally binding on B1, so he can in turn enforce the same right against S. Voluntary return is a contract between B1 and B2 that does not affect S.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن باعَ مُشْتَرَيَهُ وَرُدَّ عليه بعيب بقضاء بإقرار، أو ببينة ، أو بنكول ، رَدَّ على بائعه، وإن رُدَّ برضاه لا. [2] قوله : وردّ عليه ... الخ ؛ تنقيحه : أنه إذا اشترى عيناً منقولاً فباعه بعد القبض ، ثم ردَّه المشتري الثاني على بائعه، وهو المشتري الأوّل ، فالمشتري الأوّل إن قبل الرد (۱) يعني لو ينتفع به مع فساده بأن يصلح للعلف أو لأكل بعض الفقراء لم يرده ؛ لتعذره بالكسر ؛ لأنه عيب حادث ولكن يرجع بنقصان العيب دفعاً للضرر بقدر الإمكان، وكل ثمنه في غير المنتفع به لبطلان البيع ؛ لأن المبيع ليس بمال. ينظر : شرح ابن ملك»(ق١٦٦ /أ).
And whoever sells his purchased item and it is returned to him due to a defect by a decree (of a judge) based on confession, or evidence, or refusal (to take oath), he returns it to his seller. But if it is returned by his (the buyer's) consent, he does not. [2] His statement: 'and it is returned to him...' its refinement: That if one buys a movable item and sells it after taking possession, then the second buyer returns it to his seller, who is the first buyer, then the first buyer if he accepts the return... (meaning: if it can be benefited from despite its defect, like being fit for fodder or for some poor people to eat, he does not return it because it is prevented by the break, for it is a new defect, but he claims the deficiency of the defect to remove harm as much as possible, and all of its price in what is not benefitted from due to the invalidity of the sale, because the sold item is not wealth. Refer to: Sharh Ibn Malik (folio 166a).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on a buyer who, after purchasing an item, sells it to a third party. If the third party then returns the item to the first buyer due to a defect (as decreed by a judge based on confession, evidence, or the defendant's refusal to swear), the first buyer can in turn return the item to his original seller. However, if the third party returns the item voluntarily (by mutual consent), the first buyer cannot pass that return to his own seller. The commentary clarifies the condition: if the item is still usable despite the defect, the first buyer cannot return it to his original seller because the break (his own action of reselling?) is a new defect, but he can claim the monetary difference for the defect. If the item is completely unusable, the sale to the first buyer is void and he can claim the full price.

Key Terms
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath in court, which is treated as an admission of the claim.
بقضاء (bi-qada'): By a judicial decree or judgment of a qadi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer (A) purchases a phone from seller (B), then sells it to C. C discovers a defect and refuses to swear in court, so the judge orders C to return the phone to A. Can A return the phone to B?
A) Yes, because the return was by judicial decree.
B) No, because A sold it voluntarily.
C) Only if C returned it with A's consent.
D) No, because the defect was not present at A's purchase.

Key Takeaway
A first buyer can only pass back a defect return to his seller if the return from his buyer was by judicial decree, not voluntary.

Buyer Breaks Defective Item: Usable vs. Useless Goods
Ruling on remedy for buyer who breaks a defective purchased item.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi remedy for a buyer who breaks a purchased item before discovering a defect, distinguishing between usable and useless goods, and apply the ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Breaking a defective item before knowing the defect does not forfeit all rights; the buyer claims deficiency if usable, full refund if useless.

Imagine buying a watermelon, cutting it open at home, and finding it rotten. If the watermelon is still edible (e.g., for animals), can you return it? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer based on whether the item retains any utility. This lesson unpacks that ruling, its proof by analogy, and the contrast with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal issue
What did the buyer do? He broke the item (e.g., egg, watermelon) before discovering a hidden defect. The legal question: what remedy does he have?
The break is a new defect caused by the buyer, which complicates return.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Does the broken item still have any use? If it can be used (e.g., as fodder), the sale remains valid, and the buyer claims only the reduction in value (nuqsān). If it is completely useless (not fit for humans or animals), the item was not wealth (mal) at sale, so the sale is void and the buyer gets a full refund.
The key condition is whether the item is 'muntafaʿu bihi' (beneficial).
3
Examine the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to a buyer who cuts a cloth before finding a defect. In that case, the buyer still has recourse to claim the defect by consensus. Similarly, breaking the item is an act in the buyer's own property (since ownership transferred), so it does not waive his right.
The analogy shows that a new act by the buyer does not imply acceptance of the defect.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the seller's permission to break implies consent to the defect, so the buyer may return the broken item. The Hanafi response: permission was given in the buyer's ownership, not the seller's, so it does not bind the seller.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Shafi'i allows return; Hanafi only allows deficiency claim for usable items.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The Hanafi position, supported by the analogy and detailed in the text, is the relied-upon view for fatwa. The buyer retains the item (or gets full refund if useless) and claims deficiency.
This ruling applies to many modern cases, like opening sealed products.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اشترى بيضاً، أو بطيخاً، أو قثاء، أو خياراً، أو جوزاً، فكسر، فوجده فاسداً فله نقصانه في المنتفع به، وكل ثمنه في غيره. ومَن باع مشتراه ورد عليه بعيب بقضاء بإقرار، أو ببينة، أو بنكول، رد على بائعه، وإن رد برضاه لا.

توضيحه: أنه إذا اشترى أحد شيئاً من هذه الأشياء المذكورة فكسره فوجده فاسداً، بأن كان مراً أو متناً، وهذا الكسر كان قبل العلم بالعيب؛ لأنه إن كان بعد العلم به فكان دليل الرضاء، فهذا بمكسور لا يخلو إما أن ينتفع به بأن يصلح لأكل بعض الناس أو الدواب، أو لا.
ففي الصورة الأولى: رجع بنقصانه دفعاً للضرر بقدر الإمكان، وليس له أن يرده؛ لأن الكسر عيب حادث، إلا أن يقبله البائع مكسوراً، أو يرد الثمن، وقال الشافعي: يرده؛ لأن الكسر وإن كان عيباً حادثاً لكنه بتسليط البائع، فصار كأنه فعله ورضي به. ولنا: أن التسليط على الكسر في ملك المشتري لا في ملك البائع؛ لأنه بالبيع لم يبق ملكه، فلم يكن التسليط إلا في ملك المشتري، وذلك هدر لعدم ولايته عليه، فصار كما إذا اشترى ثوباً فقطعه، ثم وجده معيباً، فله الرجوع اتفاقاً.
وفي الصورة الثانية: وهي ما إذا كان بحيث لم يصلح لأكل الناس ولا للعلف يرجع بالثمن كله؛ لأنه ليس بمال، فإن المال ما ينتفع به في الحال أو المآل، فإذا كان هذا الشيء بحيث لا ينتفع به أصلاً فلا يكون محلاً للبيع، فبطل البيع.
And if he buys eggs, or watermelon, or cucumber, or cucumber, or walnuts, and breaks it, and finds it spoiled, then he has its deficiency in what can be benefited from, and its entire price in other than that. And whoever sells his purchased item and it is returned to him due to a defect by a court ruling based on confession, or evidence, or refusal to take oath, he returns it to his seller, but if it is returned by his consent, no.
Clarification: if one buys any of these mentioned things and breaks it and finds it spoiled, being bitter or rotting, and this breaking was before knowledge of the defect; because if after knowledge then it is evidence of consent. So this broken item is either such that benefit can be taken from it (fit for eating by some people or animals) or not.
In the first case: he recovers the decrease to avoid harm as much as possible, and he does not have the right to return it because the break is a new defect, unless the seller accepts it broken or returns the price. Al-Shafi'i said: he returns it because the break, although a new defect, occurred by the seller's permission, so it is as if he did it and consented to it. Our proof: permission to break is in the buyer's ownership, not the seller's; because by sale the seller's ownership does not remain, so the permission was only in the buyer's ownership, and that is nullified due to lack of authority over it, so it is like if he bought a cloth and cut it, then found it defective, he has recourse unanimously.
In the second case: when it is not fit for human consumption nor fodder, he recovers the entire price because it is not wealth; wealth is that which is benefited from in the present or future. So if this thing is such that no benefit can be taken from it at all, then it is not subject to sale, so the sale is void.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the remedy for a buyer who breaks an item (like eggs, watermelon, etc.) before discovering a hidden defect. The author explains that if the buyer breaks it before knowing the defect, he has two scenarios: if the item can still be used (e.g., for fodder or some people), he can only claim the reduction in value (the difference between its actual value and the price paid) because returning it is not possible due to the new break. In the second scenario, if the item is completely useless (not fit for humans or animals), the sale is void (batil) because it was not considered wealth (mal) at the time of sale, so the buyer recovers the full price. A comparative view with Imam al-Shafi'i is mentioned: al-Shafi'i allows return even with the break because the seller permitted the break, as if he accepted the defect. The Hanafi counter-argument is that permission was given in the buyer's ownership, not the seller's, so it is like cutting a cloth; the buyer can still claim the defect because the cutting was a new act in his own property, not an acceptance of the defect. The analogy with cutting a cloth (agreed upon) supports this.

Key Terms
نقصانه (nuqsānahu): The decrease in value due to the defect, calculated as the difference between the price paid and the market value of the defective item.
المنتفع به (al-muntafaʿu bihi): The item that can still be used for some purpose despite the defect (e.g., as fodder).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer breaks an egg before knowing it is rotten, and the egg is fit only for animal feed, what is his remedy according to the Hanafi school?
A) He may return the egg and get a full refund.
B) He may claim the difference between the price and the value of the usable egg.
C) He has no remedy because he broke it.
D) He must keep the egg and cannot claim anything.

Key Takeaway
When a buyer breaks a purchased item before knowing a defect, the remedy depends on whether the item retains any utility: deficiency if usable, full refund if completely useless.

Return of Defective Item: Court-Ordered vs. Voluntary
Ruling on first buyer's right to return after sub-buyer's return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing court-ordered return (faskh) from voluntary return (new sale) to determine whether a first buyer may return a defective item to his original seller.

Core Idea
Court-ordered return rescinds the sale; voluntary return creates a new sale that blocks further return.

When a sub-buyer returns a defective item, the first buyer's ability to pass that return back to his own seller depends entirely on whether the return was compelled by a court or done voluntarily. This passage from the Hanafi school clarifies that distinction and provides important exceptions for currency and real estate. Understanding this ruling prevents wrongful chain returns and ensures consistency in sales contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the first buyer's receipt of a returned item from his sub-buyer. The passage rules that if the return was court-ordered, the first buyer can return it to his original seller; if voluntary, he cannot.
The key is the nature of the return: rescission vs. new sale.
2
Distinguish between court-ordered and voluntary return
Court-ordered return (radd bi qada) occurs when the sub-buyer proves a defect via confession, evidence, or refusal to take oath, and the judge compels the return. Voluntary return (radd bi rida) occurs when the first buyer accepts the item back without court involvement.
The method of proof does not matter for the ruling, only the presence or absence of a court order.
3
Analyze the legal consequences
A court-ordered return is treated as rescission (faskh) for all parties, as if the first buyer never sold the item. Thus, the first buyer can return it to his original seller. A voluntary return is treated as a new sale (bay' jadid) between the first and second buyer, so the first buyer cannot return it to his own seller.
The underlying principle: after possession, parties cannot unilaterally rescind; only a court can compel rescission.
4
Apply the exceptions
For currency (sarf, e.g., dinar for dirham), any return due to defect is rescission, whether court-ordered or voluntary, because currency is not specified. For real estate ('aqar), a voluntary return before possession is treated as a new sale (since real estate can be sold before possession), so the first buyer cannot return it to his seller.
Restriction to movable ('ayn) and non-real estate highlights the rule's boundaries.
5
Understand the rationale (dalil)
The proof is based on qiyas and istihsan: the distinction hinges on whether the return is compelled (court) or consensual (voluntary). A court order gives the return the effect of faskh, while voluntary acceptance after possession is akin to a new sale because parties cannot rescind after delivery without mutual consent or a court.
No direct Quran or hadith; the reasoning is analogical, fitting the Hanafi use of istihsan in muamalat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن باعَ مشتراه ورد عليه بعيب بقضاء بإقرار، أو ببينة، أو بنكول، رد على بائعه، وإن رد برضاه لا.

تنقيحه: أنه إذا اشترى عيناً منقولاً فباعه بعد القبض، ثم رده المشتري الثاني على بائعه، وهو المشتري الأول، فالمشتري الأول إن قبل الرد أي إن اشترى شيئاً، ثم باعه، فادعى المشتري الثاني عيباً على المشتري الأول، وأثبت ذلك بالبينة، أو بالنكول، أو بالإقرار، فقضى القاضي فرد على بائعه، كان له أن يخاصم البائع الأول بقضاء القاضي، بأن أقر بالعيب وامتنع من القبول، فرد عليه القاضي جبراً، أو أنكر العيب فأثبته بالبينة، أو بالإباء عن اليمين، أو بالبينة على إقرار البائع بالعيب مع إنكاره الإقرار به.
فإنه يرد عليه في الصور الأربعة، فللمشتري الأول أن يرده على بائعه؛ لأن الرد بالقضاء فسخ في حق الكل، فيكون كأنه لم يبعه.
وإن قبل المشتري الأول برضاه: أعني بغير قضاء القاضي، فليس له أن يرده على بائعه؛ لأنه بيع جديد؛ لأنه لا ولاية لهما على الرد بعد القبض. وإنما قيدنا بالعين احترازاً عن الصرف، فإنه يجعل فسخاً إذا رد بعيب، لا فرق بين القضاء والرضاء؛ لأنه لا يمكن أن يجعل بيعاً جديداً؛ لأن الدينار هاهنا لا يتعين في العقود، فإذا اشترى ديناراً بدراهم، ثم باع الدينار من آخر، ثم وجد المشتري الثاني بالدينار عيباً ورده المشتري بغير قضاء، فإنه يرده على بائعه لما ذكرنا.
ووجهه في «الكافي»: بأن المعيب ليس بمبيع، بل المبيع السليم، فيكون المبيع ملك البائع، فإذا رده على المشتري يرده على بائعه، أما هاهنا المبيعان الموجودان. كذا في «البحر».
وإنما قيدنا بغير العقار؛ لأنه لا يرده الأول على البائع، إلا إذا رد عليه بقضاء مطلقاً، سواء قبضه الثاني أو لا؛ إذ لو رده عليه قبل قبضه برضاه لا يرده على البائع؛ لأنه بمنزلة البيع الجديد فيه؛ إذ يجوز بيعه قبل قبضه فكأن المشتري الأول اشتراه من الثاني، فليس له الرد.
ولا يقال مثله في المنقول؛ لأنه لا يجوز بيعه قبل قبضه، فإذا رده الثاني على الأول قبله كان فسخاً لا بيعاً جديداً. كذا في حاشية الطحطاوي على الدر المختار.
And whoever sells his purchased item and it is returned to him due to a defect by a court ruling based on confession, or evidence, or refusal to take oath, he returns it to his seller, but if returned by his consent, no.
Refinement: if he buys a movable item and sells it after possession, then the second buyer returns it to his seller (the first buyer), the first buyer — if he accepts the return — i.e., if he buys something, then sells it, then the second buyer claims a defect against the first buyer and proves it by evidence, or refusal to take oath, or confession, and the judge rules and it is returned to his seller, he may litigate with the first seller by the judge's ruling: either he confessed to the defect and refused to accept, so the judge compelled the return, or he denied the defect and it was proven by evidence, or by refusal of oath, or by evidence of the seller's confession while he denies confession. So it is returned to him in these four cases, and the first buyer may return it to his seller because the return by court ruling is a rescission for everyone, as if he never sold it.
If the first buyer accepts it by his consent — i.e., without a judge's ruling — then he may not return it to his seller because it is a new sale; since they have no authority to rescind after possession. And we restricted it to a movable (ayn) to exclude currency (sarf); for currency, it is considered rescission if returned due to defect, no difference between court and consent; because it cannot be made a new sale; since a dinar here is not specified in contracts. So if he buys a dinar with dirhams, then sells the dinar to another, then the second buyer finds a defect in the dinar and returns it without a court ruling, he returns it to his seller for the reason we mentioned.
And its explanation in al-Kafi: the defective item is not the sold item; rather the sold item is the sound one, so the sold item belongs to the seller. So when he returns it to the buyer, he returns it to his seller. But here the sold items are existing. Similarly in al-Bahr.
And we restricted it to non-real estate; because the first buyer does not return it to the seller unless it was returned to him by a court ruling absolutely, whether the second took possession or not; because if the second returned it to him before possession by consent, he does not return it to the seller; because it is akin to a new sale in it; since it is permissible to sell it before possession, so it is as if the first buyer bought it from the second, so he has no right to return.
And it is not said similarly for movables; because it is not permissible to sell them before possession. So if the second returns it to the first before possession, it is rescission, not a new sale. Similarly in Hashiyat al-Tahtawi ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect when a sub-buyer (second buyer) returns a defective item to the first buyer. The key distinction is whether the return was by court order (bi qada') or voluntarily (bi rida). If the return is by court order (based on confession, evidence, or refusal to take oath), then the first buyer can return the item to his original seller, because a court-ordered rescission is considered a rescission (faskh) that applies to all parties, as if the original sale never happened. However, if the first buyer voluntarily accepts the return (without a court order), then that is considered a new sale (bay' jadid) between the first buyer and second buyer, and thus the first buyer cannot pass it back to his original seller. An exception is made for currency (sarf, exchange of dinars and dirhams): because currency is not specified (ghayr muta'ayyin) in contracts, a return of defective currency is always treated as rescission, not a new sale, regardless of whether there was a court order. Additionally, the ruling differs for real estate (aqar): for real estate, even a voluntary return before possession is treated as a new sale because real estate can be sold before possession. For movables, since selling before possession is invalid, a return before possession is rescission. The commentary from al-Bahr and al-Kafi explains the reasoning.

Key Terms
رد بقضاء (radd bi qaḍāʾ): Return of an item by court order after a claim of defect is proven.
رد برضاه (radd bi riḍāhu): Return of an item voluntarily by mutual consent without court involvement.
عين منقول (ʿayn manqūl): A movable, tangible object (e.g., cloth, food).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A first buyer (A) buys a car from B, sells it to C. C proves the car has a defect in a Hanafi court and the judge orders C to return the car to A. Can A return the car to B?
A) Yes, because the court-ordered return is a rescission.
B) No, because A had already sold the car.
C) Yes, but only if A also goes to court.
D) No, because the judge's order only applies to C.

Key Takeaway
A court-ordered return is rescission, a voluntary return is a new sale; the first buyer may only return to his seller in the former case.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Return of Defective Goods: Court Order vs. Mutual Consent
Main ruling and exceptions on sequential sale defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the first buyer's recourse after a second buyer returns a defective item, and identify the exceptions for currency exchange and real estate.

Core Idea
Court-ordered return is annulment; consensual return is a new sale—except for currency exchange.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses a common scenario in sequential sales: a person buys an item, sells it, and the second buyer discovers a defect. The key question is whether the first buyer can reclaim from his original seller. The Hanafi position distinguishes sharply between a return ordered by a judge (which retroactively annuls the sale) and a return by mutual consent (which is treated as a new sale). The text also provides important exceptions for currency exchange and real estate, which modify the default rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the legal scenario? The first buyer sells an item, the second buyer finds a defect and returns it. Determine whether the first buyer has recourse against his original seller.
The ruling depends on how the return happened: by court order or by mutual consent.
2
Distinguish the two types of return
Understand the distinction between al-radd bi-qadā' (return by judicial decree) and al-radd bi-riḍā' (return by mutual consent). The text states that only the former is considered annulment (faskh), allowing the first buyer to reclaim.
This is the core legal principle.
3
Identify the exceptions
Note the exceptions: currency exchange (ṣarf) and real estate (ʿaqār). For currency exchange, even consensual return is annulment because the items are not specific. For real estate, the first buyer can reclaim only if the return was by court order, regardless of possession.
The text also mentions movables but treats them differently due to the prohibition of selling before possession.
4
Follow the reasoning (talil)
The author explains the rationale: court-ordered return operates as annulment for all parties, so it is as if the first sale never happened. Consensual return is a new sale because the parties have no authority over return after possession.
This reasoning is consistent with Hanafi principles of faskh vs. new sale.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that only court-ordered return gives the first buyer the right to reclaim from his original seller, with the exceptions noted.
This is found in authoritative works like al-Hidayah, al-Kafi, al-Bahr, and al-Tahtawi.

Source Text
