أي إن اشترى شيئاً، ثُمَّ باعَهُ، فادعى المشتري الثاني عيباً على المشتري الأول، وأثبت ذلك بالبَيِّنَة ، أو بالنُّكُول ، أو بالإقرار، فقضى القاضي فرُدَّ على بائعه، كان له أنْ يُخاصم البائع الأَوَّلَ بقضاء القاضي، بأن أقر بالعيب وامتنع من القبول، فرد عليه القاضي جبراً، أو أنكر العيب فأثبته بالبينة، أو بالإباء عن اليمين، أو بالبينة على إقرار البائع بالعيب مع إنكاره الإقرار به. فإنه يردّ عليه في الصور الأربعة، فللمشتري الأول أن يرده على بائعه ; لأنَّ الرد بالقضاء فسخ في حق الكل، فيكون كأنه لم يبعه. وإن قبل المشتري الأول برضاه : أعني بغير قضاء القاضي، فليس له أن يرده على بائعه ; لأنه بيع جديد ; لأنه لا ولاية لهما على الرد بعد القبض. وإنما قيدنا بالعين احترازاً عن الصرف، فإنه يجعلُ فسخاً إذا ردَّ بعيب، لا فرق بين القضاء والرضاء ; لأنه لا يمكن أن يجعل بيعاً جديداً ; لأن الدينار هاهنا لا يتعين في العقود، فإذا اشترى ديناراً بدراهم، ثم باع الدينار من آخر، ثم وجد المشتري الثاني بالدينار عيباً وردّه المشتري بغير قضاء، فإنّه يردّه على بائعه لما ذكرنا. ووجهه في «الكافي» : بأن المعيب ليس بمبيع ، بل المبيع السليم، فيكون المبيع ملك البائع ، فإذا ردّه على المشتري يردّه على بائعه، أما هاهنا المبيعان الموجودان. كذا في «البحر» وإنما قيدنا بغير العقار ; لأنه لا يردّه الأول على البائع، إلا إذا رُدَّ عليه بقضاء مطلقاً، سواء قبضه الثاني أو لا ; إذ لو رده عليه قبل قبضه برضاه لا يردُّه على البائع ; لأنه بمنزلة البيع الجديد فيه ; إذ يجوز بيعه قبل قبضه فكأنّ المشتري الأول اشتراه من الثاني، فليس له الردّ. ولا يقال مثله في المنقول ; لأنه لا يجوز بيعه قبل قبضه، فإذا ردَّه الثاني على الأوَّل قبله كان فسخاً لا بيعاً جديداً. كذا في حاشية الطَّحْطَاوي على الدر المختار. قال في «الهداية»: معنى القضاء بالإقرار : أَنَّهُ أنكر الإقرار فأُثبت بالبينة. فإن قيل : المشتري الأَوَّلُ إذا أنكر إقرارَهُ بالعيب، فأثبت هذا بالبينة صارَ كأنه أقر عند القاضي، فإنَّ الثابت بالبينة كالثَّابِتِ عياناً، فينبغي أن لا يكون له ولاية الرد على البائع الأَوَّلِ سواءٌ أقرَّ عند القاضي، أو أنكر إقراره، فيثبتُ بالبَيِّنَة ; لأنَّ الإقرار حجَّةٌ قاصرةٌ، فَأَيُّ فائدة في قوله : معنى القضاء بالإقرار أنَّه أنكر الإقرار ؟ قلنا : نحن لم نَجْعَل الإقرار حجَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّية وإنما قيدنا بكون البيع بعد القبض ; لأنّ بيع المنقول قبل القبض لا يجوز كما صرحوا به. قوله : معنى القضاء... الخ ; وإنما احتاج إلى هذا التأويل ; لأنه إذا لم ينكر إقراره بعد الإقرار بالعيب لا يحتاج إلى القضاء، بل يردّه عليه بإقراره بالعيب، فإذا ردَّ عليه بإقراره بدون القضاء، لم يكن له أن يردَّه على بائعه. كما في «الكفاية» قوله : فإن قيل ... الخ ; حاصله : أن المشتري الثاني إذا رأى في مشتراه عيباً ورده إلى بائعه فلم يقبله، فتوجه إلى القضاء والمشتري الأوّل أنكر إقراره بالعيب في مشتراه عند بيعه، وأثبت المشتري الثاني ذلك الإقرار بالبينة، فكأن المشتري الأول أقر به في مجلس القاضي. فإنَّ من قاعدتهم أن الثابت بالبينة كالثابتِ عياناً في الحكم، فالمناسب أن لا يكون للمشتري الأول أن يرده على بائعه مطلقاً، سواء أقرَّ عند القاضي أو ثبت إقراره بالبينة بعد إنكاره إياه ; لأنّ الإقرار حجة، لكن لا بحيث يتعدّى أثرها إلى غير من هي له، فما فائدة التقييد في بيان معنى القضاء بالإقرار بقيدِ إنكار الإقرار وبثبوته بالبينة. قوله : نحن لم نجعل...الخ ; لا ندعي أن الإقرار حجةٌ يتعدى أثرها إلى غير من ولا ندعي أنَّ الردَّ بعينه على المشتري الأول هو على بائعه، ولا حاجة فيه إلى الخصومة، بل نقول : إنَّ المشتري الأوّل إذا ردّ عليه بقضاء القاضي ثبت له أن يُخاصم بائعه في رد مشتراه عليه بالعيب فإن... الخ.
That is, if he buys something, then sells it, and the second buyer claims a defect from the first buyer and proves it by evidence, or by refusal to take an oath, or by confession, and the judge rules and it is returned to its seller, he (the first buyer) may litigate with the first seller by the judge's ruling—whether by admitting the defect and refusing acceptance so the judge compels the return, or by denying the defect and proving it by evidence, or by refusal to swear, or by evidence of the seller's admission of the defect while he denies the admission. So it is returned in these four cases, and the first buyer may return it to his seller, because the return by judicial ruling is annulment regarding everyone, so it is as if he never sold it. But if the first buyer accepts it willingly—meaning without a judge's ruling—he may not return it to his seller, because it is a new sale, because they have no authority over return after possession. And we specified 'specific goods' to exclude currency exchange, for it is considered annulment when returned due to defect, with no difference between judicial ruling and mutual consent, because it cannot be considered a new sale, since the dinar here is not specified in contracts. So if he buys a dinar with dirhams, then sells the dinar to another, then the second buyer finds a defect in the dinar and returns it to the first buyer without a judicial ruling, he returns it to his seller for the reason we mentioned. And its justification in al-Kafi: that the defective item is not the sold item, but rather the sound item is the sold item, so the sold item belongs to the seller; when he returns it to the buyer, he returns it to his seller. But here the sold items are existing. Thus in al-Bahr. And we specified 'non-real estate' because the first buyer does not return it to the seller unless it is returned to him by a judicial ruling absolutely, whether the second buyer took possession or not; for if he returns it to him before taking possession willingly, he does not return it to the seller, because it is like a new sale, since selling before taking possession is permissible, so it is as if the first buyer bought it from the second, so he has no right to return. And the same is not said for movables, because selling before taking possession is not permissible; so when the second returns it to the first before possession, it is annulment, not a new sale. Thus in the margin of al-Tahtawi on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. He said in al-Hidaya: 'The meaning of judgment by confession is that he denied the confession and it was proven by evidence.' If it is said: The first buyer, if he denies his confession of the defect, and this is proven by evidence, it is as if he confessed before the judge; for what is proven by evidence is like what is proven by direct observation, so he should have no authority to return to the first seller whether he confessed before the judge or denied his confession and it was proven by evidence, because confession is a constricted proof, so what benefit is there in his saying: the meaning of judgment by confession is that he denied the confession? We said: We have not made the confession a transmissible proof; we only specified it being after possession because selling a movable before possession is not permissible, as they have stated. His statement: 'the meaning of judgment...' etc.; he needed this interpretation because if he does not deny his confession after admitting the defect, there is no need for judgment; rather, he returns it to him by his confession of the defect; so if he returns it by his confession without judgment, he may not return it to his seller. As in al-Kifaya. His statement: 'if it is said...' etc.; its summary: that the second buyer, when he sees a defect in his purchase and returns it to his seller but he does not accept it, he goes to the judge, and the first buyer denies his confession of the defect in his purchase at the time of sale, and the second buyer proves that confession by evidence; so it is as if the first buyer confessed it in the judge's court. For their principle is that what is proven by evidence is like what is proven by direct observation in legal effect; so it is fitting that the first buyer should not be able to return it to his seller absolutely, whether he confessed before the judge or his confession was proven by evidence after his denial, because confession is a proof, but not such that its effect extends to other than the one for whom it is made; so what is the benefit of restricting the explanation of meaning of judgment by confession with the condition of denying the confession and proving it by evidence? His statement: 'We have not made...' etc.; we do not claim that confession is a proof whose effect extends to other than its maker, nor do we claim that the return of the exact item to the first buyer is like returning it to his seller, and there is no need for litigation; rather, we say: when the first buyer has it returned to him by the judge's ruling, he is entitled to litigate with his seller regarding the return of his purchase upon him due to defect... etc.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses the scenario of sequential sales where a defect is discovered. The ruling: If a person buys an item, then sells it, and the second buyer finds a defect and legally proves it (by evidence, refusal to swear, or confession), and the judge orders the return of the item to the first buyer, then the first buyer has the right to return that item to his original seller, because the judicial return is considered an annulment of the sale retroactively. However, if the second buyer returns the item to the first buyer by mutual consent without a judge's ruling, the first buyer cannot return it to his original seller; it is treated as a new sale. An exception is made for currency exchange (e.g., dinars and dirhams), where even a consensual return is treated as annulment because these items are not specific in contracts. Another exception is for immovable property (real estate): the first buyer can only reclaim from his seller if the return was by court order, regardless of whether the second buyer had taken possession. For movables, the rule differs because selling before taking possession is invalid, so a return before possession is annulment. The text then discusses a nuance about 'judgment by confession' (qada' bil iqrar): if the first buyer denies his earlier confession of the defect, and the second buyer proves it by evidence, it is treated as if the first buyer confessed in court. A potential objection is raised: since evidence is equivalent to direct observation, the first buyer should have no recourse. The response clarifies that confession is a proof limited to the one who makes it; it does not extend to third parties. Thus, the restriction (denial and proof by evidence) is necessary because if the first buyer confessed without denial, the return would be consensual (without court order) and he would lose the right to reclaim from his seller. The overall principle is that the first buyer's right to reclaim from his seller hinges on the second buyer's return being by judicial decree, not on the nature of proof.

Key Terms
الرد بقضاء (al-radd bi-qadā'): Return of a defective item by order of a judge after legal proof.
الرد برضاء (al-radd bi-riḍā'): Return of a defective item by mutual consent of buyer and seller without judicial involvement.
الصرف (al-ṣarf): Currency exchange, i.e., sale of gold for silver or similar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling when the second buyer returns a defective item to the first buyer by mutual consent without a court order?
A) The first buyer can reclaim from his original seller because the defect existed.
B) The first buyer cannot reclaim from his original seller because the return is treated as a new sale.
C) The first buyer can reclaim only if the item is currency.
D) The first buyer can reclaim only if the item is real estate.

Key Takeaway
Only a court-ordered return of a defective item allows the first buyer to reclaim from his original seller; a consensual return is a new sale, with exceptions for currency exchange and movables before possession.

Judgment by Confession: When Denial Proves the Defect
Nuance of qada' bil iqrar in defect returns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on 'judgment by confession' (qada' bil iqrar) and evaluate the objection that evidence is equivalent to direct observation.

Core Idea
A denied confession proven by evidence is like a court confession, but the first buyer still has recourse because confession doesn't extend to third parties.

This passage delves into a subtle point within the broader discussion of defect returns: what happens when the first buyer denies having confessed the defect at the time of sale? The text from al-Hidayah introduces the concept of 'judgment by confession' and the commentator explains the reasoning behind the Hanafi position. A hypothetical objection is raised—since evidence is as good as direct observation, why should the first buyer have any recourse? The response clarifies the nature of confession as a limited proof (ḥujja qāṣira) and reaffirms the necessity of a court order to give the first buyer standing against his original seller.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of qada' bil iqrar
The second buyer proves the defect by showing that the first buyer had previously confessed the defect when selling. The first buyer now denies that confession. The second buyer brings evidence (bayyina) to prove the confession happened. How does this affect the first buyer's right to reclaim?
This is a subset of the four methods of proof mentioned earlier.
2
Identify the objection
The hypothetical objection: If the first buyer's confession is proven by evidence, it is as if he confessed in court (since the evidentiary standard is the same). Therefore, the return should be considered consensual? Wait, the objection is that if evidence makes it like an in-court confession, then the first buyer should lose his right to reclaim because the confession is a 'constricted proof' (ḥujja qāṣira) that doesn't extend to third parties? Actually, the objection is more nuanced: The objection states that if the confession is proven by evidence, it is as if he confessed in court, so the first buyer should have no authority to return to his seller in any case, whether he confessed in court or the confession was proven by evidence. The text says: 'if it is said...'
The objection questions the benefit of distinguishing between in-court confession and out-of-court confession proven by evidence.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response states that confession is a proof that does not extend beyond the one who makes it (ḥujja qāṣira). The first buyer's confession only binds him; it does not automatically give the second buyer a right against the original seller. However, the first buyer can still reclaim from his original seller because the court-ordered return (based on the proven confession) is an annulment that operates between the second buyer and first buyer, and that annulment gives the first buyer a fresh claim against his seller. The restriction (denial of confession) is important because if the first buyer had not denied it, the return would have been consensual (no court needed) and he would lose recourse.
The key is that the court order is the operative event, not the confession alone.
4
Trace the expansion from al-Hidayah
The commentator expands on al-Hidayah's statement: 'the meaning of judgment by confession is that he denied the confession and it was proven by evidence.' This expansion clarifies that the confession alone does not automatically constitute a court order; a judicial process is required to make the return a court-ordered one, which alone gives the first buyer recourse.
This is an 'expansion probe'—the commentator elaborates on an ambiguous point.
5
Summarize the practical consequence
The first buyer's right to reclaim depends on whether the return was effected by a judge or by private action. Even if the defect is proven by confession, if the first buyer denies and the second buyer proves the confession, a judge must order the return for it to count as qadā'.
This reinforces the central distinction of the larger passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي إن اشترى شيئاً، ثُمَّ باعَهُ، فادعى المشتري الثاني عيباً على المشتري الأول، وأثبت ذلك بالبَيِّنَة ، أو بالنُّكُول ، أو بالإقرار، فقضى القاضي فرُدَّ على بائعه، كان له أنْ يُخاصم البائع الأَوَّلَ بقضاء القاضي، بأن أقر بالعيب وامتنع من القبول، فرد عليه القاضي جبراً، أو أنكر العيب فأثبته بالبينة، أو بالإباء عن اليمين، أو بالبينة على إقرار البائع بالعيب مع إنكاره الإقرار به. فإنه يردّ عليه في الصور الأربعة، فللمشتري الأول أن يرده على بائعه ; لأنَّ الرد بالقضاء فسخ في حق الكل، فيكون كأنه لم يبعه. وإن قبل المشتري الأول برضاه : أعني بغير قضاء القاضي، فليس له أن يرده على بائعه ; لأنه بيع جديد ; لأنه لا ولاية لهما على الرد بعد القبض. وإنما قيدنا بالعين احترازاً عن الصرف، فإنه يجعلُ فسخاً إذا ردَّ بعيب، لا فرق بين القضاء والرضاء ; لأنه لا يمكن أن يجعل بيعاً جديداً ; لأن الدينار هاهنا لا يتعين في العقود، فإذا اشترى ديناراً بدراهم، ثم باع الدينار من آخر، ثم وجد المشتري الثاني بالدينار عيباً وردّه المشتري بغير قضاء، فإنّه يردّه على بائعه لما ذكرنا. ووجهه في «الكافي» : بأن المعيب ليس بمبيع ، بل المبيع السليم، فيكون المبيع ملك البائع ، فإذا ردّه على المشتري يردّه على بائعه، أما هاهنا المبيعان الموجودان. كذا في «البحر» وإنما قيدنا بغير العقار ; لأنه لا يردّه الأول على البائع، إلا إذا رُدَّ عليه بقضاء مطلقاً، سواء قبضه الثاني أو لا ; إذ لو رده عليه قبل قبضه برضاه لا يردُّه على البائع ; لأنه بمنزلة البيع الجديد فيه ; إذ يجوز بيعه قبل قبضه فكأنّ المشتري الأول اشتراه من الثاني، فليس له الردّ. ولا يقال مثله في المنقول ; لأنه لا يجوز بيعه قبل قبضه، فإذا ردَّه الثاني على الأوَّل قبله كان فسخاً لا بيعاً جديداً. كذا في حاشية الطَّحْطَاوي على الدر المختار. قال في «الهداية»: معنى القضاء بالإقرار : أَنَّهُ أنكر الإقرار فأُثبت بالبينة. فإن قيل : المشتري الأَوَّلُ إذا أنكر إقرارَهُ بالعيب، فأثبت هذا بالبينة صارَ كأنه أقر عند القاضي، فإنَّ الثابت بالبينة كالثَّابِتِ عياناً، فينبغي أن لا يكون له ولاية الرد على البائع الأَوَّلِ سواءٌ أقرَّ عند القاضي، أو أنكر إقراره، فيثبتُ بالبَيِّنَة ; لأنَّ الإقرار حجَّةٌ قاصرةٌ، فَأَيُّ فائدة في قوله : معنى القضاء بالإقرار أنَّه أنكر الإقرار ؟ قلنا : نحن لم نَجْعَل الإقرار حجَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّية وإنما قيدنا بكون البيع بعد القبض ; لأنّ بيع المنقول قبل القبض لا يجوز كما صرحوا به. قوله : معنى القضاء... الخ ; وإنما احتاج إلى هذا التأويل ; لأنه إذا لم ينكر إقراره بعد الإقرار بالعيب لا يحتاج إلى القضاء، بل يردّه عليه بإقراره بالعيب، فإذا ردَّ عليه بإقراره بدون القضاء، لم يكن له أن يردَّه على بائعه. كما في «الكفاية» قوله : فإن قيل ... الخ ; حاصله : أن المشتري الثاني إذا رأى في مشتراه عيباً ورده إلى بائعه فلم يقبله، فتوجه إلى القضاء والمشتري الأوّل أنكر إقراره بالعيب في مشتراه عند بيعه، وأثبت المشتري الثاني ذلك الإقرار بالبينة، فكأن المشتري الأول أقر به في مجلس القاضي. فإنَّ من قاعدتهم أن الثابت بالبينة كالثابتِ عياناً في الحكم، فالمناسب أن لا يكون للمشتري الأول أن يرده على بائعه مطلقاً، سواء أقرَّ عند القاضي أو ثبت إقراره بالبينة بعد إنكاره إياه ; لأنّ الإقرار حجة، لكن لا بحيث يتعدّى أثرها إلى غير من هي له، فما فائدة التقييد في بيان معنى القضاء بالإقرار بقيدِ إنكار الإقرار وبثبوته بالبينة. قوله : نحن لم نجعل...الخ ; لا ندعي أن الإقرار حجةٌ يتعدى أثرها إلى غير من ولا ندعي أنَّ الردَّ بعينه على المشتري الأول هو على بائعه، ولا حاجة فيه إلى الخصومة، بل نقول : إنَّ المشتري الأوّل إذا ردّ عليه بقضاء القاضي ثبت له أن يُخاصم بائعه في رد مشتراه عليه بالعيب فإن... الخ.
That is, if he buys something, then sells it, and the second buyer claims a defect from the first buyer and proves it by evidence, or by refusal to take an oath, or by confession, and the judge rules and it is returned to its seller, he (the first buyer) may litigate with the first seller by the judge's ruling—whether by admitting the defect and refusing acceptance so the judge compels the return, or by denying the defect and proving it by evidence, or by refusal to swear, or by evidence of the seller's admission of the defect while he denies the admission. So it is returned in these four cases, and the first buyer may return it to his seller, because the return by judicial ruling is annulment regarding everyone, so it is as if he never sold it. But if the first buyer accepts it willingly—meaning without a judge's ruling—he may not return it to his seller, because it is a new sale, because they have no authority over return after possession. And we specified 'specific goods' to exclude currency exchange, for it is considered annulment when returned due to defect, with no difference between judicial ruling and mutual consent, because it cannot be considered a new sale, since the dinar here is not specified in contracts. So if he buys a dinar with dirhams, then sells the dinar to another, then the second buyer finds a defect in the dinar and returns it to the first buyer without a judicial ruling, he returns it to his seller for the reason we mentioned. And its justification in al-Kafi: that the defective item is not the sold item, but rather the sound item is the sold item, so the sold item belongs to the seller; when he returns it to the buyer, he returns it to his seller. But here the sold items are existing. Thus in al-Bahr. And we specified 'non-real estate' because the first buyer does not return it to the seller unless it is returned to him by a judicial ruling absolutely, whether the second buyer took possession or not; for if he returns it to him before taking possession willingly, he does not return it to the seller, because it is like a new sale, since selling before taking possession is permissible, so it is as if the first buyer bought it from the second, so he has no right to return. And the same is not said for movables, because selling before taking possession is not permissible; so when the second returns it to the first before possession, it is annulment, not a new sale. Thus in the margin of al-Tahtawi on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. He said in al-Hidaya: 'The meaning of judgment by confession is that he denied the confession and it was proven by evidence.' If it is said: The first buyer, if he denies his confession of the defect, and this is proven by evidence, it is as if he confessed before the judge; for what is proven by evidence is like what is proven by direct observation, so he should have no authority to return to the first seller whether he confessed before the judge or denied his confession and it was proven by evidence, because confession is a constricted proof, so what benefit is there in his saying: the meaning of judgment by confession is that he denied the confession? We said: We have not made the confession a transmissible proof; we only specified it being after possession because selling a movable before possession is not permissible, as they have stated. His statement: 'the meaning of judgment...' etc.; he needed this interpretation because if he does not deny his confession after admitting the defect, there is no need for judgment; rather, he returns it to him by his confession of the defect; so if he returns it by his confession without judgment, he may not return it to his seller. As in al-Kifaya. His statement: 'if it is said...' etc.; its summary: that the second buyer, when he sees a defect in his purchase and returns it to his seller but he does not accept it, he goes to the judge, and the first buyer denies his confession of the defect in his purchase at the time of sale, and the second buyer proves that confession by evidence; so it is as if the first buyer confessed it in the judge's court. For their principle is that what is proven by evidence is like what is proven by direct observation in legal effect; so it is fitting that the first buyer should not be able to return it to his seller absolutely, whether he confessed before the judge or his confession was proven by evidence after his denial, because confession is a proof, but not such that its effect extends to other than the one for whom it is made; so what is the benefit of restricting the explanation of meaning of judgment by confession with the condition of denying the confession and proving it by evidence? His statement: 'We have not made...' etc.; we do not claim that confession is a proof whose effect extends to other than its maker, nor do we claim that the return of the exact item to the first buyer is like returning it to his seller, and there is no need for litigation; rather, we say: when the first buyer has it returned to him by the judge's ruling, he is entitled to litigate with his seller regarding the return of his purchase upon him due to defect... etc.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses the scenario of sequential sales where a defect is discovered. The ruling: If a person buys an item, then sells it, and the second buyer finds a defect and legally proves it (by evidence, refusal to swear, or confession), and the judge orders the return of the item to the first buyer, then the first buyer has the right to return that item to his original seller, because the judicial return is considered an annulment of the sale retroactively. However, if the second buyer returns the item to the first buyer by mutual consent without a judge's ruling, the first buyer cannot return it to his original seller; it is treated as a new sale. An exception is made for currency exchange (e.g., dinars and dirhams), where even a consensual return is treated as annulment because these items are not specific in contracts. Another exception is for immovable property (real estate): the first buyer can only reclaim from his seller if the return was by court order, regardless of whether the second buyer had taken possession. For movables, the rule differs because selling before taking possession is invalid, so a return before possession is annulment. The text then discusses a nuance about 'judgment by confession' (qada' bil iqrar): if the first buyer denies his earlier confession of the defect, and the second buyer proves it by evidence, it is treated as if the first buyer confessed in court. A potential objection is raised: since evidence is equivalent to direct observation, the first buyer should have no recourse. The response clarifies that confession is a proof limited to the one who makes it; it does not extend to third parties. Thus, the restriction (denial and proof by evidence) is necessary because if the first buyer confessed without denial, the return would be consensual (without court order) and he would lose the right to reclaim from his seller. The overall principle is that the first buyer's right to reclaim from his seller hinges on the second buyer's return being by judicial decree, not on the nature of proof.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Confession or admission of a fact (here, knowledge of a defect).
حجة قاصرة (ḥujja qāṣira): A constricted proof—evidence that is valid only against the one who produced it, not against others.
الثابت بالبينة كالثابت عياناً (al-thābit bil-bayyina ka al-thābit ʿiyānan): What is established by evidence is like what is established by direct observation in legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the essential element that gives the first buyer the right to reclaim from his original seller when the second buyer proves the defect by a denied confession?
A) The confession itself, because it is proven by evidence.
B) The court order (qada'), because the return is then annulment.
C) The fact that the second buyer took possession.
D) The first buyer's admission of the confession.

Key Takeaway
When a defect is proven through a denied confession, a court order is required for the return to be annulment; without it, the first buyer cannot reclaim from his seller.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Return After Confession of Defect Established by Evidence
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of proving confession by evidence on the right to return a defective item.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a first buyer may return a defective item to his seller after his confession is proven by evidence, and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf from that of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Evidence nullifies denial but does not create willing acceptance; the first buyer may still litigate with his seller.

When a second buyer proves the first buyer's confession of a defect by evidence, does the first buyer lose his right to return the item to his own seller? This passage from the commentaries on al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the first buyer may still litigate, while Muhammad ibn al-Hasan says he cannot. Understanding the distinction between confession in court (which implies willing acceptance) and proof of confession by evidence (which does not) is key to grasping the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
There are two sales: original seller → first buyer → second buyer. The first buyer confesses the defect but later denies it. The second buyer proves the confession by evidence and wants to return the item. The question: can the first buyer then return it to his own seller?
2
State the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They say the first buyer may litigate with his seller, provided he proves the defect existed with the seller. Their reasoning: evidence (bayyinah) binds him and nullifies his denial.
The evidence makes the denial irrelevant; the first buyer is bound by the confession.
3
State Muhammad's position
He says the first buyer cannot litigate because his previous denial contradicts his later claim; it is inconsistent.
Muhammad focuses on the logical inconsistency, disregarding the effect of evidence.
4
Analyze the distinction
The difference between confession in court (which implies willing acceptance) and proof by evidence (which does not) explains why the first buyer retains the right to litigate. Evidence does not make him willing; it only proves he made a statement.
Willing acceptance would bar return, but evidence of confession does not equal willing acceptance.

قال في «الهداية»: معنى القضاء بالإقرار : أَنَّهُ أنكر الإقرار فأُثبت بالبينة ) . فإن قيل : المشتري الأَوَّلُ إذا أنكر إقرارَهُ بالعيب، فأثبت هذا بالبينة صارَ كأنه أقر عند القاضي ، فإنَّ الثابت بالبينة كالثَّابِتِ عياناً ، فينبغي أن لا يكون له ولاية الرد على البائع الأَوَّلِ سواءٌ أقرَّ عند القاضي، أو أنكر إقراره، فيثبتُ بالبَيِّنَة ؛ لأنَّ الإقرار حجَّةٌ قاصرةٌ، فَأَيُّ فائدة في قوله : معنى القضاء بالإقرار أنَّه أنكر الإقرار ؟
(۲) قلنا : نحن لم نَجْعَل " الإقرار حجَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّية وإنما قيدنا بكون البيع بعد القبض ؛ لأنّ بيع المنقول قبل القبض لا يجوز كما صرحوا به. [١] قوله : معنى القضاء... الخ ؛ وإنما احتاج إلى هذا التأويل ؛ لأنه إذا لم ينكر إقراره بعد الإقرار بالعيب لا يحتاج إلى القضاء، بل يردّه عليه بإقراره بالعيب، فإذا ردَّ عليه بإقراره بدون القضاء، لم يكن له أن يردَّه على بائعه. كما في «الكفاية (٢) [٢]قوله : فإن قيل ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أن المشتري الثاني إذا رأى في مشتراه عيباً ورده إلى بائعه فلم يقبله ، فتوجه إلى القضاء والمشتري الأوّل أنكر إقراره بالعيب في مشتراه عند بيعه، وأثبت المشتري الثاني ذلك الإقرار بالبينة، فكأن المشتري الأول أقر به في مجلس القاضي. فإنَّ من قاعدتهم أن الثابت بالبينة كالثابتِ عياناً في الحكم، فالمناسب أن لا يكون للمشتري الأول أن يرده على بائعه مطلقاً، سواء أقرَّ عند القاضي أو ثبت إقراره بالبينة بعد إنكاره إياه ؛ لأنّ الإقرار حجة ، لكن لا بحيث يتعدّى أثرها إلى غير من هي له ، فما فائدة التقييد في بيان معنى القضاء بالإقرار بقيدِ إنكار الإقرار وبثبوته بالبينة. (۳) قوله : نحن لم نجعل...الخ ؛ لا ندعي أن الإقرار حجةٌ يتعدى أثرها إلى غير من ولا ندعي أنَّ الردَّ بعينه على المشتري الأول هو على بائعه، ولا حاجة فيه إلى الخصومة، بل نقول : إنَّ المشتري الأوّل إذا ردّ عليه بقضاء القاضي ثبت له أن يُخاصم بائعه في رد مشتراه عليه بالعيب فإن... الخ.
هي له،
ولم نَقُل : إِنَّ الرَّدَّ على المشتري الأَوَّلِ رَدُّ على بائعه، بل له أن يُخاصم بائعه، فإن المشتري الثاني إذا أَثْبَتَ أنَّ العيب كان في يد المشتري الأول ، ورَدَّ عليه ،
فالمشتري الأَوَّلُ إن أثبت أنَّ العيب كان في يَدِ بائعِهِ رَدَّهُ عليه، وإلا فلا. والفرق بين إقراره عند القاضي وبين إثبات إقراره بالبَيِّنَةِ أَنَّهُ إذا أقرَّ عند القاضي يكون طائعاً في أخذ المبيع، فصار كما إذا اشترى من المشتري الثاني ، فلا يكون له ولايةُ الرَّدِّ على البائع الأَوَّل
[١] قوله : ولم نقل....الخ ؛ لأنَّ الموجود بيعان ، فبفسخ الثاني لا يفسخ الأول بخلاف الوكيل بالبيع ؛ إذ رُدَّ عليه المبيع معيباً بالبينة أو بالنكول حيث يكون رداً على الموكل ؛ لأنَّ البيع هناك واحد، بل إذا رُدَّ المبيع على المشتري الأول ؛ لقضاء القاضي كان له أن يخاصم بائعه. فإن رد المشتري الثاني بإثبات عيب لا يحدث مثله عند المشتري الأوّل، لا حاجة للمشتري إن أراد رده على بائعه إلى إقامة بينة أخرى، بل البينة السابقة تكفي في رده عليه.
وإن كان رده بإثبات عيب يحدث مثله عند المشتري الأول كان له في رده على بائعه حاجة إلى إقامة بينة أخرى ؛ لإثبات كونه في يد بائعه أو إقراره ، وهذا هو قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف . وقال محمد ه : ليس للمشتري الأوّل أن يُخاصم بائعه ؛ لأنه أنكر عيب المبيع ،
فإذا أراد الردَّ على بائعه صارَ مُدّعياً بثبوت العيب، وذا يتناقض. ولهما : أنَّ المشتري الأوّل صار مكتباً بالبينة فجعل إنكاره بعدمه، فيصح دعواه.
(1) كذا في شروح «الهداية». [٢] قوله : وإلا فلا ؛ أي وإن لم يثبت المشتري الأوّل أنّ هذا العيب كان في يدِ بائعه حين اشتراه فلا يُرَدُّ عليه.
[٣] قوله : والفرق ...الخ ؛ قال يعقوب باشا) : «هذا الفرق مذكور في شروح «الهداية» لكن فيه كلام، وهو أنه لا يلزم من الإقرار أخذ المبيع طائعاً حتى يكون بيعاً
He said in al-Hidayah: The meaning of judgment based on confession is that he denied the confession and it was established by evidence. If it is said: The first buyer, when he denies his confession of the defect, and this is established by evidence, it becomes as if he confessed before the judge, for what is established by evidence is like what is established by eyewitness. So it should be that he has no authority to return (the item) to the original seller, whether he confessed before the judge or denied his confession and it was established by evidence, because the confession is a limited proof. What then is the benefit of his saying: 'The meaning of judgment based on confession is that he denied the confession'? (2) We say: We do not make the confession a transmissible proof, and we only restricted it to the sale being after possession, because the sale of movable property before possession is not permissible as they have stated. [1] His saying: 'The meaning of judgment...' etc.; he only needed this interpretation because if he does not deny his confession after confessing the defect, he does not need judgment, but rather returns it to him by his confession of the defect. So if he returns it to him by his confession without judgment, he cannot return it to his own seller. As in al-Kifayah. [2] His saying: 'If it is said...' etc.; its summary is that the second buyer, when he sees a defect in his purchase and returns it to his seller and he does not accept, he goes to court, while the first buyer denies his confession of the defect in his purchase at the time of his sale, and the second buyer proves that confession by evidence. It becomes as if the first buyer confessed to it in the judge's court. For their principle is that what is established by evidence is like what is established by eyewitness in legal effect. So it is appropriate that the first buyer should not be able to return it to his seller at all, whether he confessed before the judge or his confession was established by evidence after his denial, because the confession is a proof, but not such that its effect extends to someone other than the one for whom it is. So what is the benefit of restricting in explaining the meaning of judgment by confession the condition of denial of confession and its establishment by evidence? (3) His saying: 'We do not make...' etc.; We do not claim that the confession is a proof whose effect extends to others, nor do we claim that the return itself to the first buyer is a return to his seller, nor is there need for litigation in it. Rather we say: When the first buyer has it returned to him by the judge's judgment, he is established to have the right to litigate with his seller regarding the return of his purchase due to the defect. And we did not say that the return to the first buyer is a return to his seller. Rather he may litigate with his seller. For the second buyer, when he proves that the defect was in the possession of the first buyer and returns it to him, then the first buyer, if he proves that the defect was in the possession of his seller, he returns it to him, otherwise not. And the difference between his confession before the judge and the establishment of his confession by evidence is that when he confesses before the judge, he is willingly taking the sold item, so it becomes as if he bought it from the second buyer, and he has no authority to return it to the original seller. [1] His saying: 'And we did not say...' etc.; because there are two sales, so the rescission of the second does not rescind the first, unlike the agent for sale; for when the sold item is returned to him defective by evidence or by refusal (to take oath), it becomes a return to the principal, because the sale there is one. Rather when the sold item is returned to the first buyer by the judge's judgment, he may litigate with his seller. For if the second buyer returns it by proving a defect that does not normally occur in the possession of the first buyer, the first buyer does not need to produce other evidence if he wants to return it to his seller; rather the previous evidence suffices for his return to him. And if his return is by proving a defect that normally occurs in the possession of the first buyer, then he needs to produce other evidence for his return to his seller, to prove that it was in his seller's possession or his confession. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: The first buyer may not litigate with his seller because he denied the defect of the sold item; so if he wants to return it to his seller, he becomes a claimant asserting the existence of the defect, and this is contradictory. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): The first buyer is bound by the evidence, so his denial is rendered null, so his claim is valid. (1) This is in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: 'Otherwise not'; i.e., if the first buyer does not prove that this defect was in his seller's possession when he bought it, it is not returned to him. [3] His saying: 'And the difference...' etc.; Ya'qub Pasha said: 'This difference is mentioned in the commentaries of al-Hidayah, but there is discussion about it, namely that confession does not necessarily imply taking the sold item willingly so that it becomes a sale.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the first buyer's right to return a defective item to his own seller after a second buyer has proved his (the first buyer's) confession of the defect by evidence. The author of al-Hidayah defines 'judgment based on confession' as a situation where the first buyer denies his confession and it is proven by evidence. A question is raised: since proof by evidence is equivalent to eyewitness, why wouldn't the first buyer lose his right to return the item to his seller unconditionally, whether he confessed in court or his confession was proven by evidence? The answer given is that the confession is a limited proof (hujja qasira); it does not extend to third parties. The purpose of requiring the denial and proof is to establish that the first buyer did not voluntarily accept the defective item; if he confessed without denial, he would be deemed to have accepted it willingly and thus cannot return it to his own seller. The commentator explains the scenario: second buyer (M2) buys from first buyer (M1), finds a defect, and when M1 denies having confessed the defect during his own purchase, M2 proves the confession by evidence. The question is whether M1 can then return the item to his own seller (original seller). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that M1 may litigate with his seller, but he must prove either that the defect existed with the seller or that the seller confessed. Muhammad holds that M1 cannot litigate because his previous denial contradicts his claim. The majority respond that the evidence binds M1, effectively nullifying his denial, so his claim is valid. The commentary also distinguishes between M1's confession in court (which implies willing acceptance) and proof of confession by evidence (which does not). Additional notes clarify that if the defect is of a type that could have occurred during M1's possession, he needs separate evidence; otherwise, the prior evidence suffices. This ruling follows the principle that the rescission of a second sale does not automatically rescind the first, unlike the case of an agent where the return is directly to the principal because there is only one sale.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession by a party (buyer) that the item had a defect at the time of his purchase.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence (witness testimony) that establishes the confession of the first buyer.
ولاية الرد (wilayat al-radd): The authority or right of a buyer to return a defective item to his seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the effect of proving the first buyer's confession by evidence on his right to return the item to his own seller?
A) He loses the right entirely because evidence is like eyewitness.
B) He may still return it if he proves the defect existed with his seller.
C) He may return it without any further proof.
D) He cannot return it because his previous denial is contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The right to return a defective item after confession is proven by evidence depends on the nature of the proof: testimony does not imply willingness to accept the defect.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
The First Buyer's Right to Sue His Seller After a Chain of Defect Returns
Intra-Hanafi dispute with explanatory commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the first buyer's right to sue his original seller after a subsequent buyer returns a defective item, identify the conditions under which that right is preserved or lost, and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf from Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
A second buyer's return does not automatically free the first buyer; he must prove the defect was with his seller.

When a defective item is sold through a chain, the return of the item by the last buyer triggers complex rights. This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute: after the second buyer returns a defective item to the first buyer, can the first buyer in turn sue his own seller? The answer depends on how the defect was proven—by admission or by evidence—and on which Hanafi authority you follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Read the base text: 'We did not say that the return upon the first buyer is a return upon his seller; rather, he has the right to sue his seller.' Note that the first sale is not automatically rescinded.
2
Understand the key distinction
The passage contrasts admission (iqrar) before a judge with proof by evidence (bayyinah). Admission makes the first buyer a 'willing taker' and bars suit; evidence preserves his right to sue.
This distinction is critical to the Hanafi position.
3
Compare the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow suit if the first buyer brings new evidence that the defect existed with his seller. Imam Muhammad forbids suit because the first buyer's prior denial contradicts his claim.
Both are legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Analyze the qiyas with the agent case
The text analogizes to an agent for sale: if the agent returns a defective item, it is a return on the principal because the sale is one. But here there are two sales, so the first sale is distinct.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Example: A sells to B, B sells to C. C returns the item to B because of a defect. B can sue A only if B proves the defect was present when A sold it. If B admitted the defect in court, he cannot sue A.
This shows the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

وقد قيل : هذه المسألة فيما إذا ادّعى المشتري الثاني على المشتري الأَوَّلِ أَنَّ العيب كان في يَدِ البائع الأَوَّل، فحينئذ للمشتري الأَوَّل أَنْ يُخاصم على البائع
الأَوَّلِ ، أَمَّا إذا ادعى أنّ العيب في يدِ المشتري الأوَّلِ فليس له أن يُخَاصِمَ بائعه. أقول : فيه نظر؛ لأنَّه إذا ادّعى أنَّ العيب في يد البائع الأول، وأقام عليه البينة، وقضى على المشتري الأَوَّل ، فهذا القضاء ليس قضاء على البائع الأول ، وهذه البينة لم تقم على البائع الأوَّلِ ولا على نائبه ؛ لأنَّ ما يدعى" على الغائب ليس سببا لما يدعى على الحاضر.
جديداً في حق الثالث، الذي هو البائع الأوّل، ولا يكون له ولاية الرد لعدم الرضا، وإن كان الردّ بالرضاء فلا كما لا يخفى إلا أن يدعي أن الإقرار عند القاضي أخذ المبيع طائعاً في الحال ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر علمُهُ بقضاء القاضي سنة، فليتأمل». انتهى. [ 1 ] قوله : وقد قيل ... الخ ; حاصله : أنَّ موضعَ الخلاف دعوى وجود العيب عند البائع الأول إذا أقام البينة على أنَّ العيب كان عند المشتري الأول، ليس للمشتري الأول أن يخاصم بائعه اتفاقاً فإنّه لم يجعل كاذباً في إقراره ؛ لكونه سليماً عند البائع. [٢) قوله : أقول ... الخ ; حاصله : أنه لَمَّا كان البيّنة والقضاء غيره واردين على البائع الأوّل بل على الثاني، فمال الدعوى الأولى إلى الثانية، فكما لا مجال في الثانية للمخاصمة؛ لفقدان المكذب في سلامته عند البائع الأول، فكذا في الأولى لهذه العلة بعينها ; لرجوعها إليها ، فالفرق بينهما بتجويزا المخاصمة وعدمه تحكم. (۳) قوله : لأن ما يدعي...الخ ; تعليل لقوله : ولا على نائبه، يعني أن القضاء على الغائب وإقامة البيّنة لا يصحان إلا بحضرة نائبه ، وهو على ثلاثة أنواع :
۱. حقيقي ; وهو من يكون بأمره وإنابته، وهو الوكيل.
٢. وشرعي : وهو الوصي الذي نَصبه القاضي. ٣. وحكمي : وهو أن يكون بنيابة الحاضر عن الغائب حكماً ; بأن يكون ما يدعي على الغائب سبباً لما يدعي على الحاضر على كل حال ، وهو بحيث لا ينفك. ففي هذه الحالة ينصب الحاضر خصماً عن الغائب، ويقضى عليهما جميعاً.
But we did not say: that the return upon the first buyer is a return upon his seller; rather, he has the right to sue his seller. For if the second buyer proves that the defect was in the possession of the first buyer and returns it to him, then the first buyer—if he proves that the defect was in the possession of his seller—returns it to him, otherwise not. And the difference between his admission before the judge and the proof of his admission by evidence is that if he admits before the judge, he is taking the merchandise willingly, so it becomes as if he bought from the second buyer, and thus he has no authority to return to the first seller. [1] His statement: 'And we did not say...etc.' because there are two sales; the rescission of the second does not rescind the first, unlike the agent for sale, for if the merchandise is returned to him defective by evidence or by refusal, it is a return upon the principal because the sale there is one. Rather, if the merchandise is returned to the first buyer by the judge's decree, he has the right to sue his seller. If the second buyer returns by proving a defect that does not normally occur in the possession of the first buyer, the first buyer, if he wants to return to his seller, does not need to produce other evidence; rather, the prior evidence suffices for his return upon him. But if his return is by proving a defect that normally occurs in the possession of the first buyer, then in his return upon his seller he needs to produce other evidence to prove that it was in the possession of his seller or his admission. This is the statement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: The first buyer may not sue his seller because he denied the defect of the merchandise; if he wants to return to his seller, he becomes a claimant of the existence of the defect, and this is contradictory. Their [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's] argument: The first buyer has been bound by the evidence, so his denial is considered as nonexistent, thus his claim is valid. (1) Likewise in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'otherwise not' means if the first buyer does not prove that this defect was in the possession of his seller when he bought it, then it is not returned upon him. [3] His statement: 'And the difference...etc.' Ya'qub Pasha said: 'This difference is mentioned in the commentaries of al-Hidayah, but there is discussion about it: that from admission, taking the merchandise willingly does not necessarily follow so that it becomes a new sale with respect to the third party, who is the first seller, and he would have no authority to return due to lack of consent. But if the return is with consent, then it is not, as is not hidden, unless one claims that admission before the judge means taking the merchandise willingly in the immediate sense because apparently he knows the judge's decree from the previous year. So let it be considered.' End. [1] His statement: 'And it has been said...etc.' Its summary: the locus of disagreement is the claim of the existence of the defect with the first seller. If he brings evidence that the defect was with the first buyer, the first buyer cannot sue his seller by agreement because he is not considered a liar in his admission, since it was sound with the seller. [2] His statement: 'I say...etc.' Its summary: since the evidence and the decree were not directed at the first seller but at the second, the first claim devolves to the second. Just as there is no room for suing in the second due to the absence of contradiction of his soundness with the first seller, likewise in the first for the same reason because it reverts to it. So the difference between them by permitting suing or not is arbitrary. (3) His statement: 'because what is claimed...etc.' is an explanation for his statement 'nor against his deputy', meaning that the decree against an absentee and presenting evidence are not valid except in the presence of his deputy, which is of three types: 1. Real: one who acts by his command and delegation, i.e., the agent. 2. Legal: the executor appointed by the judge. 3. Judgmental: that the present person acts as a deputy for the absentee judgmentally, by what is claimed against the absentee being a cause for what is claimed against the present in any case, in a way that is inseparable. In this case, the present is appointed as a litigant for the absentee, and judgment is passed against both.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the right of the first buyer (who purchased a defective item and later resold it) to return the item to his own seller after a subsequent buyer returns it to him. The commentary by the author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains the base text from Sharh al-Wiqaya.

The base ruling states that the second buyer's return does not automatically nullify the first sale; the first buyer can only return to his seller if he proves the defect existed in his seller's possession. The key distinction is between the first buyer admitting the defect before a judge (which makes him a willing taker, negating his right to return to his seller) versus the defect being proven by evidence (which makes him a reluctant taker, preserving his right).

Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the first buyer may sue his seller if he brings new evidence that the defect existed with the seller, even if he previously denied it. Their reasoning: the first buyer was compelled by the evidence, so his denial is treated as null, allowing him to claim. Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that the first buyer's denial of the defect contradicts his later claim, so he cannot sue. The author notes that this is the position in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.

The passage then critiques a proposed distinction by some (perhaps from al-Hidayah commentaries) that the first buyer's right to sue depends on whether the second buyer claimed the defect existed with the first buyer or with the first seller. The commentator (the author of 'Umda) finds this arbitrary, as the evidence and judgment were against the second buyer, not the first seller, so the first buyer should have no claim either way. Finally, a gloss explains the types of deputies (real, legal, judgmental) relevant to litigation against an absentee.

Key Terms
رد (radd): The return of a defective item by the buyer to the seller, exercising the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb).
إقرار (iqrar): An admission by the first buyer that the defect existed when he purchased the item, made before a judge.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically witness testimony, that proves the defect existed in the previous seller's possession.
مخاصمة (mukhasamah): The legal right to sue or litigate against the seller for the return of the price due to a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the case where the first buyer can sue his seller from the case where he cannot?
A) Whether the first buyer admits the defect before a judge or proves it by evidence.
B) Whether the second buyer returns the item willingly or unwillingly.
C) Whether the original seller is present or absent.
D) Whether the defect is major or minor.

Key Takeaway
In a chain sale, a first buyer can sue his seller only if he proves the defect existed with that seller; a prior admission bars suit, but evidence overrides denial.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
First Buyer's Right to Sue After Second Buyer Proves Defect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the first buyer's right to sue after a second buyer's proof of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the first buyer's right to sue his original seller when a second buyer proves a defect, and apply the concept of deemed representation (niyabah hukmiyyah) to determine the validity of litigation against an absent party.

Core Idea
The first buyer's right to sue is not restricted by the second buyer's claim direction; the distinction is arbitrary.

In Hanafi sales law, when a second buyer proves a defect in goods that were previously sold twice, the first buyer may have a claim against his original seller—even if he had initially admitted there was no defect. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether that right depends on where the second buyer claims the defect originated. The commentator rejects a purported distinction as arbitrary, grounding his analysis in the concept of legal representation (niyabah). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate procedural rights linked to substantive evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the core ruling and the dispute
Identify the main ruling: when the first buyer denies his admission and the second buyer proves the defect, the first buyer's taking is deemed rescission, so he can litigate with his seller. Then note the disputed point: whether this right depends on the second buyer's claim direction.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; the opposing view is attributed to 'qil' (some scholars).
2
Identify the two scenarios distinguished by qil
Under the qil view: (1) if the second buyer claims the defect was at the original seller, the first buyer can sue; (2) if the second buyer claims the defect was at the first buyer, the first buyer cannot sue. Express each scenario clearly.
The qil view makes the right contingent on the second buyer's allegation.
3
Analyze the commentator's rebuttal using the three types of representation
The commentator argues that the evidence and judgment are only against the first buyer, not the original seller. Even if the second buyer claims the defect was at the original seller, the claim against the absent party is not a cause for the claim against the present party. He then explains the three types of niyabah (real, legal, deemed) and shows none apply here, so the distinction is arbitrary.
Focus on the deemed representation (niyabah hukmiyyah): it requires a necessary and inseparable causal link, which is absent in transitory defects.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position and the practical consequence
The commentator's view is that the first buyer can sue in both scenarios because the defect must have originated from the original seller, and the first buyer's admission is nullified by proof. The mufta bihi is the permissive view. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the first buyer can recover from his seller or is limited to suing the second buyer.
The tarjih authority is the commentator himself, who labels the qil distinction as arbitrary (tahakkum).

Source Text

أَمَّا إذا أنكر إقراره بالعيب، فثبت بالبينة، لم يكن طائعاً في الأخذ، فيكون أخذه بحكم الفسخ كأَنَّه لم يبع، فيكون له المخاصمة مع بائعه. [۱] وقد قيل : هذه المسألة فيما إذا ادّعى المشتري الثاني على المشتري الأَوَّلِ أَنَّ العيب كان في يَدِ البائع الأَوَّل، فحينئذ للمشتري الأَوَّل أَنْ يُخاصم على البائع الأَوَّلِ ، أَمَّا إذا ادعى أنّ العيب في يدِ المشتري الأوَّلِ فليس له أن يُخَاصِمَ بائعه. أقول : فيه نظر؛ لأنَّه إذا ادّعى أنَّ العيب في يد البائع الأول، وأقام عليه البينة، وقضى على المشتري الأَوَّل ، فهذا القضاء ليس قضاء على البائع الأول ، وهذه البينة لم تقم على البائع الأوَّلِ ولا على نائبه ؛ لأنَّ ما يدعى" على الغائب ليس سببا لما يدعى على الحاضر. جديداً في حق الثالث، الذي هو البائع الأوّل، ولا يكون له ولاية الرد لعدم الرضا، وإن كان الردّ بالرضاء فلا كما لا يخفى إلا أن يدعي أن الإقرار عند القاضي أخذ المبيع طائعاً في الحال ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر علمُهُ بقضاء القاضي سنة، فليتأمل». انتهى. [ 1 ] قوله : وقد قيل ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنَّ موضعَ الخلاف دعوى وجود العيب عند البائع الأول إذا أقام البينة على أنَّ العيب كان عند المشتري الأول، ليس للمشتري الأول أن يخاصم بائعه اتفاقاً فإنّه لم يجعل كاذباً في إقراره ؛ لكونه سليماً عند البائع. [٢) قوله : أقول ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه لَمَّا كان البيّنة والقضاء غيره واردين على البائع الأوّل بل على الثاني، فمال الدعوى الأولى إلى الثانية، فكما لا مجال في الثانية للمخاصمة؛ لفقدان المكذب في سلامته عند البائع الأول، فكذا في الأولى لهذه العلة بعينها ؛ لرجوعها إليها ، فالفرق بينهما بتجويزا المخاصمة وعدمه تحكم. (۳) قوله : لأن ما يدعي...الخ ؛ تعليل لقوله : ولا على نائبه، يعني أن القضاء على الغائب وإقامة البيّنة لا يصحان إلا بحضرة نائبه ، وهو على ثلاثة أنواع : ۱. حقيقي ؛ وهو من يكون بأمره وإنابته، وهو الوكيل. ٢. وشرعي : وهو الوصي الذي نَصبه القاضي. ٣. وحكمي : وهو أن يكون بنيابة الحاضر عن الغائب حكماً ؛ بأن يكون ما يدعي على الغائب سبباً لما يدعي على الحاضر على كل حال ، وهو بحيث لا ينفك. ففي هذه الحالة ينصب الحاضر خصماً عن الغائب، ويقضى عليهما جميعاً. كما إذا ادعى على رجل أنه كفيل عن فلان بما يجب لـه عـلـيـه وأقام المدعي عـلـيـه بالكفالة، وأنكر الحق ، فأقام المدعي البينة عليه أنه وجب له على فلان ألف درهم، فإنّه يقضي بها في حق الكفيل الحاضر، وفي حق الغائب جميعاً، حتى لو حضر الغائبُ وأنكر لا يلتفت إلى إنكاره ، وكلٌّ من هذه الأنواع منتفي ها هنا. القاضي. أما الأول : فلعدم كون المشتري الأوّل وكيلاً من البائع، ولا وصيّاً من جانب وأما الثاني، فلأنَّ العيب الذي ادعى المشتري الثاني على البائع الأوَّلِ الغائب لا يكون سبباً لازماً لما ادعاه على المشتري الأول الحاضر ؛ لأنَّ العيب المذكور قد يتحقق عند البائع الأول ولا يتحقق عند المشتري الأوّل كما في المعائب المتزائلة، وقد يكون متحققاً عندهما معاً بحيث يكون الأوّل سبباً للثاني، كما في المستمرة مثل الأصبع الزائدة، ولزوم السببية شرط للنيابة الحكميّة. كذا في «ذخيرة العقبى»)، وغيرها.
As for when he denies his admission of the defect, and it is proven by evidence, he was not willing in taking, so his taking is by way of rescission, as if he did not sell, so he has the right to litigate with his seller. [1] And it has been said: This issue is when the second buyer claims against the first buyer that the defect was in the hand of the original seller; then the first buyer may litigate against the original seller. But if he claims that the defect was in the hand of the first buyer, then he may not litigate against his seller. I say: There is consideration in this, because if he claims that the defect was in the hand of the original seller, and he produces evidence against him, and judgment is given against the first buyer, this judgment is not a judgment against the original seller, and this evidence is not established against the original seller nor against his representative; because what is claimed against the absent party is not a cause for what is claimed against the present party. New in the right of the third, who is the original seller, and he has no authority of return due to lack of consent, and if the return is by consent, then it is not, as is not hidden, unless he claims that the admission before the judge took the sold item willingly at the moment; because the apparent is his knowledge of the judge's ruling a sunnah, so let it be considered. End. [1] His saying: 'And it has been said... etc.': Its summary: The point of disagreement is the claim of existence of the defect at the original seller. If he produces evidence that the defect was at the first buyer, the first buyer may not litigate against his seller by agreement, because he has not been made a liar in his admission due to its being sound at the seller. [2] His saying: 'I say... etc.': Its summary: When the evidence and judgment are not directed at the original seller but at the second, the first claim is transferred to the second. Just as there is no scope for litigation in the second due to the absence of a falsifier of his soundness at the original seller, so too in the first for this very reason, because it reverts to it. So the distinction between them by permitting litigation and not is arbitrary. (3) His saying: 'Because what is claimed... etc.': Explanation of his saying: 'nor against his representative'. That is, judgment against the absent and establishment of evidence are not valid except in the presence of his representative, and it is of three types: 1. Real: who is by his command and deputization, i.e., the agent. 2. Legal: the executor appointed by the judge. 3. Deemed: that the present person represents the absent by legal fiction; such that what is claimed against the absent is a cause for what is claimed against the present in all respects, and it is inseparable. In this case, the present is appointed as a litigant on behalf of the absent, and judgment is given against both. As when one claims against a man that he is a guarantor for so-and-so for what is due to him, and the claimant produces evidence of the guarantee, and he denies the right, then the claimant produces evidence against him that a thousand dirhams is due to him from so-and-so; then judgment is given thereby against the present guarantor and against the absent both, such that if the absent appears and denies, his denial is not heeded. And all of these types are absent here. The judge. As for the first: due to the absence of the first buyer being an agent from the seller, nor an executor from the side of the judge. As for the second: because the defect that the second buyer claimed against the absent original seller is not a necessary cause for what he claimed against the present first buyer; because the mentioned defect may be present at the original seller and not present at the first buyer, as in transitory defects, and it may be present at both together such that the first is a cause for the second, as in continuous defects like an extra finger, and the necessity of causation is a condition for deemed representation. Thus in 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba' and others.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the right of a first buyer to sue his seller when a second buyer proves a defect. The initial ruling states that if the first buyer admitted no defect but the second buyer proves it with evidence, the first buyer's taking is deemed as rescission, and he can sue his seller. A divergent opinion (قيل) restricts this to when the second buyer claims the defect was with the original seller; otherwise, the first buyer cannot sue. The commentator (أقول) rejects this distinction, arguing that the evidence and judgment only affect the first buyer, not the original seller, as the defect claim against an absent party does not automatically implicate the present one. The commentary then elaborates on the three types of legal representation (نيابة): real (agent), legal (executor), and deemed (where the claim against the absent is inseparably linked to the present). An example of kafalah illustrates deemed representation. The analysis concludes that none of these types apply, so the first buyer's right to sue is not contingent on the second buyer's claim direction. The relied-upon view is that the first buyer can sue in both scenarios.

Key Terms
النيابة (al-niyābah): Representation in legal proceedings; the authority to act on behalf of another, categorized as real (ḥaqīqī), legal (sharʿī), or deemed (ḥukmī).
النيابة الحكمية (al-niyābah al-ḥukmiyyah): Deemed representation where the present litigant represents an absent party because the claim against the absent is inseparably linked to the claim against the present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator (أقول), what is the ruling on the first buyer's right to sue his seller when the second buyer proves the defect?
A) He can only sue if the second buyer claims the defect was with the original seller.
B) He can sue regardless of where the second buyer claims the defect existed.
C) He cannot sue because his admission of no defect is final.
D) He must first return the goods to the second buyer before suing.

Key Takeaway
The first buyer's prior admission of no defect does not bar his right to sue his original seller when a subsequent buyer proves the defect, because the proof nullifies the admission, regardless of the subsequent buyer's claim direction.

Procedure for Buyer Claiming Defect After Possession
Details the legal process for a buyer's defect claim after possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the step-by-step Hanafi procedure when a buyer claims a defect after taking possession, and identify the roles of evidence and oaths.

Core Idea
A buyer claiming a defect must prove it existed at the seller; if no proof, the seller's oath resolves.

When a buyer takes possession of goods and later claims a defect, the law balances both parties' rights. The buyer is not forced to pay immediately, but must follow a specific procedure to prove the defect existed at the time of sale. This lesson unpacks each step, from evidence requirements to the seller’s oath, ensuring clarity in dispute resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial situation
Recognize that the buyer has taken possession and now claims a defect. The judge does not force payment until the claim is resolved.
The buyer's claim is taken seriously; he may be truthful.
2
Determine the buyer’s burden of proof
The buyer must first produce evidence that the defect exists in his possession. If he cannot, he loses the right to return.
The defect must be present now; if it disappeared, no return.
3
Prove the defect existed at the seller
Even if the defect is present, the buyer must prove it existed while the goods were with the seller; otherwise, it may have occurred in the buyer’s custody.
If both are proven, the contract is rescinded.
4
Seller’s oath when buyer lacks evidence
If the buyer has no evidence, the seller is asked to swear that the defect did not exist at the time of sale. If he swears, he is cleared; if he refuses, the defect is deemed present now.
Refusal (nukūl) counts as admission.
5
Second oath regarding the buyer’s possession
After the first oath or establishment, the seller must also swear that the defect did not occur in the buyer’s possession. If he swears, he is free; if he refuses, the judge rescinds the contract.
This protects against defects arising after sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قبض مُشْتَرَيه وادَّعَى عيباً (۱) قوله : فإن قبض...الخ ؛ توضيح المسألة : أنه إذا اشترى شيئاً وقبضه، ثم ادعى عيباً فيه لم يجبر المشتري من جانب القاضي على دفع ثمنه إلى البائع ؛ لاحتمال كونه صادقاً في دعواه. ولكنَّ المشتري يقيمُ البينة لإثباتِ العيب، بأنه وجده بالمبيع عنده ؛ لأنه إذا لم يوجد العيب عنده ليس له أن يردّه بالعيب وإن كان عند البائع ؛ لاحتمال أنه زال، فإذا أقام البينة أنه وجده عنده : يحتاج إلى أن يبرهن أنَّ هذا العيب كان عند البائع لاحتمال أنه حدث عنده فلا يستحق الرد عليه. فإذا ثبت أنه كان فيه عند البائع فسخ العقد بينهما ؛ لثبوته في الحالين عنده وعند البائع ، أو يَحْلِفُ بائعه إذا لم يكن للمشتري بينة على وجودِ العيب عنده ، وقيامه في الحال على قولهما ؛ لأنه لو أقرَّ به لزمه ، فإذا أنكره يَحْلِف، فإن حلف برئ، وإن نكل ثبت قيام العيب للحال، ثمَّ يَحْلِفُ ثانياً على أن هذا العيب لم يكن فيه عنده ، حلف برئ وإن نكل فسخ القاضي العقد بينهما.
If his buyer takes possession and claims a defect: (1) His saying: 'If he takes possession... etc.': Clarification of the issue: If he buys something and takes possession, then claims a defect in it, the buyer is not compelled by the judge to pay its price to the seller, due to the possibility that he is truthful in his claim. But the buyer produces evidence to prove the defect, that he found it in the sold item while in his possession; because if the defect is not found in his possession, he has no right to return it for the defect even if it was at the seller, due to the possibility that it disappeared. So if he produces evidence that he found it in his possession, he needs to prove that this defect was at the seller, due to the possibility that it occurred in his possession, so he does not deserve to return it. So if it is proven that it was in it at the seller, the contract between them is rescinded, due to its being established in both states: in his possession and at the seller. Or his seller takes an oath if the buyer has no evidence of the defect's existence in his possession and its current existence according to their statement; because if he admitted it, it would be binding on him. So if he denies it, he takes an oath; if he swears, he is cleared; and if he refuses, the existence of the defect at present is established. Then he takes a second oath that this defect was not in it while in his possession; if he swears, he is cleared; and if he refuses, the judge rescinds the contract between them.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage explains the procedure when a buyer takes possession of goods and later claims a defect. The buyer is not forced to pay the price immediately because his claim might be true. He must first produce evidence that the defect is present in his possession. If he does, he must also prove that the defect existed at the time of the seller's possession, because it might have arisen while in his own custody. If he proves both, the contract is rescinded. If he has no evidence, the seller is asked to take an oath denying the defect's existence at the time of sale. If the seller swears, he is absolved; if he refuses, the defect is deemed present now. Then a second oath is required of the seller that the defect did not occur in the buyer's possession; if he swears, he is free; if he refuses, the judge rescinds the contract. This process balances the rights of both parties and prevents fraudulent claims.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence or proof, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females in financial matters, used to establish the defect's existence.
حلف (ḥalafa): To take an oath; in this context, the seller takes an oath that the defect did not exist at the time of sale or that it did not occur in the buyer's possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer claims a defect after taking possession but has no evidence that the defect currently exists, what is the outcome?
A) The buyer automatically gets a refund.
B) The seller must pay compensation.
C) The buyer loses the right to return the item because the defect may have disappeared.
D) The judge immediately rescinds the contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the buyer must prove the defect was at the seller, or the seller's oath prevails.

Buyer Withholding Payment upon Claim of Defect
Explaining ruling on buyer's right to withhold payment upon claiming a defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the buyer's right to withhold payment when claiming a defect, and explain the procedural steps involving evidence and the seller's oath.

Core Idea
The buyer is not forced to pay until the seller swears or the buyer proves the defect existed at sale.

When a buyer takes possession of goods and later claims a defect, a common question arises: must he still pay the price immediately? The Hanafi school provides a clear answer: the buyer is not compelled to pay until the claim is resolved. This lesson unpacks the procedure—how evidence and oaths determine the outcome—and highlights a subtle intra-Hanafi difference regarding the buyer's own oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
Recognize that after taking possession, the buyer alleges a defect in the item. The seller demands payment, but the buyer refuses based on the defect claim.
This is a contractual dispute over the obligation to pay.
2
State the Basic Ruling
The judge does not compel the buyer to pay the price immediately because the buyer may be truthful in his claim. This is a right to withhold, not an obligation.
The ruling applies regardless of the defect's actual existence at this stage.
3
Understand the Proof Requirements
The buyer must first prove that he found the defect in the item with him (i.e., the defect exists now). If he cannot, he loses the right to return the item even if the defect existed earlier. If he proves it, he must further prove that the defect existed at the time of sale (i.e., at the seller's).
The burden is on the buyer to establish both the defect's current presence and its origin.
4
Trace the Oath Procedure
If the buyer has no evidence, the seller is sworn that the defect did not exist at the time of sale. If the seller swears, he is free and the buyer must pay. If the seller refuses (nukul), the defect is considered present, the buyer can return the item, and he is not compelled to pay.
The seller's oath is key; the buyer is generally not sworn himself according to the relied-upon view.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Variance
There is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: some say the buyer should also be sworn (that the defect existed at the seller's), but the correct view (al-ashah) is that the buyer is not sworn because he does not become a litigant until the defect is established with him.
This variance does not affect the core ruling on withholding payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قبض مُشْتَرَيه وادَّعَى عيباً كما إذا ادعى على رجل أنه كفيل عن فلان بما يجب له عليه وأقام المدعي عليه بالكفالة، وأنكر الحق، فأقام المدعي البينة عليه أنه وجب له على فلان ألف درهم، فإنه يقضي بها في حق الكفيل الحاضر، وفي حق الغائب جميعاً، حتى لو حضر الغائب وأنكر لا يلتفت إلى إنكاره، وكل من هذه الأنواع منتفي ها هنا. القاضي. أما الأول: فلعدم كون المشتري الأول وكيلاً من البائع، ولا وصياً من جانب. وأما الثاني، فلأن العيب الذي ادعى المشتري الثاني على البائع الأول الغائب لا يكون سبباً لازماً لما ادعاه على المشتري الأول الحاضر؛ لأن العيب المذكور قد يتحقق عند البائع الأول ولا يتحقق عند المشتري من خصم، ولا يصير خصماً فيه إلا بعد قيام العيب عنده، ولا يلزم من ترتب البينة ترتب اليمين، كما في الحدود، وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل فارجع إلى البحر وشروح الهداية. قوله: لم يجبر؛ لأن المشتري أنكر وجوب دفع الثمن، حيث أنكر تعيين حقه بدعوى العيب، ودفع الثمن أولى ليتعين حقه بإزاء تعيين المبيع؛ لأن حق المشتري في المسلم لا في المعيب، ووجوب دفع الثمن إنما هو ليتعين حق البائع بإزاء تعيين المبيع، فحيث أنكر تعين
