Seller's Oath When Buyer Lacks Evidence of Defect
Ruling on seller's oath with hadith proof and intra-Hanafi disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the seller's oath when the buyer lacks evidence, explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and evaluate the response to the circularity objection.

Core Idea
The seller must swear when the buyer lacks evidence, but Abu Hanifa's circularity objection challenges this.

When a buyer claims a defect existed before purchase but has no witnesses, how does Islamic law proceed? This passage examines the seller's oath as the default when evidence is absent. It presents the hadith 'Evidence is on the claimant, oath on the denier' as the primary proof, then navigates a crucial internal Hanafi dispute: does requiring the seller's oath create a circular dependency?

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The seller's oath when the buyer has no evidence of a defect. The ruling according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that the oath is wajib on the seller.
The ruling is not that the seller automatically loses; rather, the burden shifts to the seller to swear.
2
Locate the dalil
What is the proof? The hadith: 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر' (Evidence is on the claimant, oath on the denier). This is a hadith ahad, zanni al-thubut, but its meaning (dalala) is considered qati regarding the general principle.
The hadith is the core evidence; the school applies it directly.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Who disagrees and why? Abu Hanifa is reported to have a view that the seller does not swear, due to circular reasoning (dawr): the buyer must first be an opponent to require the oath, but he can only become an opponent by proving the defect, which requires the oath. This is a logical objection.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; it does not conflict with the hadith.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How is the circularity objection resolved? Al-'Inayah distinguishes evidence from oath: evidence can prove someone is an opponent without a prior lawsuit (as in hudud cases), whereas the oath requires an existing opposition. Therefore, no circularity arises when the buyer uses evidence to establish his standing.
This response solidifies the relied-upon position of the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند عدم بَيِّنَةِ المشتري على العيب عنده يَحْلِفُ البائع عندهما أَنَّهُ ما يعلمُ أَنَّهُ أَيقَ عنده، واختلفوا على قول أبي حنيفة له. قد ذكر أن المشتري أقام بَيْنَة أَوَّلاً أَنَّهُ أَيقَ عنده، فإن لم يكن له بينةٌ يَحْلِفُ بائعُهُ عندهما بأنَّك ما تعلَمُ أَنَّه بَيِّنَةٌ أيـق عند المشتري؛ لقوله: «البَيِّنَةُ على المدعي، واليمين على مَن أنكر»، فكل شيء يثبت بالبَيِّنَةِ فعند العجز عنها يتوجه اليمين على المنكر. العيب يوهم تعلقه بالشَّرطين، وهما: البيع والتسليم لا بأحدهما فقط، فإذا كان هذا العيب في العبد وقت التسليم لا وقت البيع لم يحنث، فيدفعُ الرد بهذا التأويل فيتضرر به المشتري. والأصح أنَّ البائع لا يَير في يمينه إلا إذا لم يكن العيب موجوداً في البيع والتسليم أصلاً؛ لأنه ينفي العيب عند البيع وعند التسليم، فيحنث إذا لم يكن متعيباً في أحدهما. واختلف المشايخ على قول أبي حنيفة له، ووجه عدم الاستحلاف أنَّ اليمين لا يتوجه إلا على الخصم، ولا يصير خصماً إلا بعد قيام العيب عنده، فلا يُمكنُ إثبات هذا بالحلف؛ لأنَّه دور. وقال في «العناية: لا نسلّم أنَّ كلَّ ما يترتب عليه البيئة يترتب عليه التحليف، فإنَّ دعوى الوكالة يترتب عليها البينة دون التحليف، والبيئة لا تستلزم الدعوى، فضلاً عن صحتها، بل تقوم على ما لا دعوى فيه أصلاً، كما في الحدود، بخلاف التحليف. والفرق أنَّ التحليف شُرعَ لقطع الخصومة، فكان مقتضياً سابقية الخصم، ولا يكون المشتري هاهنا خصماً إلا بعد إثباتِ قيام العيب في يده، ولم يثبت، كما تقوم، وأما البينة فمشروعة لإثبات كونه خصماً.
And when the buyer has no evidence of the defect in his possession, the seller swears according to the two [companions] that he does not know that it [the defect] occurred in his possession, and they differed regarding the statement of Abu Hanifa about him. It has been mentioned that the buyer first produced evidence that it occurred in his possession. If he has no evidence, his seller swears according to the two that you do not know that it is a proven [defect] in the buyer's possession, because of his saying: 'Evidence is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the denier.' So everything established by evidence, when unable [to produce it], the oath is directed to the denier. The defect is understood to be attached to the two conditions: the sale and delivery, not one of them alone. So if this defect was in the slave at the time of delivery not at the time of sale, he does not break his oath, thus he repels the return by this interpretation, and the buyer is harmed by it. The correct view is that the seller does not fulfill his oath unless the defect was not present at the sale and delivery at all, because he denies the defect at the sale and at delivery, so he breaks it if it was not defective in either of them. And the mashayikh differed regarding the statement of Abu Hanifa about him. The reason for not requiring the oath is that the oath is only directed to the opponent, and one only becomes an opponent after the proof of the defect in his possession, so this cannot be established by the oath because it is circular reasoning. And it is said in al-'Inayah: We do not accept that everything on which evidence is based is based on the oath, because the claim of agency is based on evidence but not oath, and evidence does not necessitate a lawsuit, let alone its validity, rather it is established on something that does not involve a lawsuit at all, as in hudud, unlike the oath. The difference is that the oath was legislated to cut off litigation, so it requires the prior existence of an opponent, and the buyer here is not an opponent except after proving the existence of the defect in his hand, and it has not been proven as it arises. As for evidence, it is legislated to prove that he is an opponent.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah discusses the buyer's claim of a defect (like the slave running away) that existed before purchase. The hadith 'Evidence is on the claimant, oath on the denier' is the proof. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if the buyer has no evidence, the seller must swear an oath denying knowledge of the defect at his place. But there is a dispute regarding Abu Hanifa: some say the seller does not swear because of circular reasoning (dawr) — the buyer must first prove the defect to become an opponent, but he can only prove it via the oath which requires him to be an opponent. Al-'Inayah resolves this by distinguishing evidence from oath: evidence can prove someone is an opponent without a prior lawsuit, but oath requires an existing opposition. The correct view is that the oath covers both time of sale and delivery; if the defect existed at either, the seller's oath is broken. However, on Abu Hanifa's view, the seller does not take an oath, which is preferred by some mashayikh.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically two male witnesses or one male and two females, sufficient to prove a claim in court.
يمين (yamin): An oath taken by the defendant (the seller) denying the claim, used when the plaintiff lacks evidence.
دور (dawr): Circular reasoning or circular dependency; here, the buyer's becoming a litigant depends on proving the defect, which depends on the oath, which depends on being a litigant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason given by Al-'Inayah to reject the objection of circularity (dawr)?
A) Because the oath is always required in buyer-seller disputes.
B) Because evidence can establish someone as a litigant without requiring a prior lawsuit.
C) Because the defect is already proven by the hadith.
D) Because Abu Hanifa agreed with the Sahibayn on this point.

Key Takeaway
The seller's oath is wajib when the buyer lacks evidence, per the Sahibayn, despite Abu Hanifa's circularity objection, which is resolved by distinguishing evidence from oath.

Dispute on Administering Oath to Seller for Defect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on istihlaf in option of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the buyer cannot request an oath from the seller regarding a defect, and identify the circular reasoning (dawr) used as the basis.

Core Idea
The buyer cannot force the seller to swear about a defect because it would create circularity: the seller is not a defendant until the defect is proven, but proving it requires an oath.

This passage deals with a dispute among Hanafi scholars regarding whether a buyer who discovers a defect in a purchased item can demand that the seller take an oath that he was unaware of the defect. Some later scholars (mashayikh) allowed it, but the more correct view prohibits it. The key argument is the logical flaw of circularity (dawr): the oath can only be directed at a defendant (khasm), but the seller only becomes a defendant after the defect is proven in his possession, which would require the oath itself. The passage also distinguishes between evidence (bayyinah) and oaths, explaining why bayyinah can establish defendant status but oaths cannot.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the more correct view (mufta bihi): the buyer does NOT have the right to request an oath from the seller.
This is the view adopted by the majority of later Hanafi scholars (mashayikh).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Some mashayikh, based on a literal interpretation of Abu Hanifa's statement, allowed the buyer to request an oath.
Their reasoning analogizes to other cases where oaths are used to establish claims.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They follow Abu Hanifa's apparent statement and analogize to other cases where oaths are administered.
No explicit proof is given in the text for the opposing view beyond this.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The response is based on circularity (dawr): the oath requires the seller to be a defendant, but he only becomes a defendant after the defect is proven, which would need the oath. Thus, the oath cannot be the means of proof.
The passage elaborates that bayyinah can be used to establish defendant status, but the oath cannot because it imposes harm (darar) on a non-defendant.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: the buyer cannot force an oath; he must rely on other evidence (bayyinah) to prove the defect existed prior to sale.
This ruling affects litigation procedure in Islamic courts.

كتاب البيع خيار العيب
واختلف المشايخ " على قول أبي حنيفة له ، ووجه عدم الاستحلاف إنَّ اليمين" لا يتوجه إلا على الخصم، ولا يصير" خصماً إلا بعـ بعد قيام العيب عنده ، فلا يُمكنُ إثبات هذا بالحلف ؛ لأنَّه دور اليمين على المدعى عليه». وأخرجه الدَّارَ قُطْنِي (۱) أيضاً عن مسلم بن خالد، عن ابن جريح، عن عطاء، عن أبي هريرة : إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «البينة على مَن ادّعى، واليمين على من أنكر إلا في القسامة»، وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل فارجع إلى «نصب الراية»، وغيره من التخاريج.
[١] قوله : واختلف المشايخ ...الخ ؛ فقيل : يحلف، وقيل: لا يحلف، وهو الأصح، وتقريرُهُ مَرَّ سابقاً انظر إليه.
[٢] قوله : إنَّ اليمين...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ اليمين إنما يتوجه على الخصم، والخصم إنما يصير خصماً بعد قيام العيب عنده ، وثبوته حينئذ لا يكون إلا باليمين، فيلزمُ الدَّورُ فلا يثبت الاستحلاف.
[٣] قوله : ولا يصير ... الخ ؛ قال في «العناية : لا نسلّم أنَّ كلَّ ما يترتب عليه البيئة يترتب عليه التحليف، فإنَّ دعوى الوكالة يترتب عليها البينة دون التحليف ، والبيئة لا تستلزم الدعوى، فضلاً عن صحتها، بل تقوم على ما لا دعوى فيه أصلاً، كما في الحدود، بخلاف التحليف. والفرق أنَّ التحليف شُرعَ لقطع الخصومة، فكان مقتضياً سابقية الخصم، ولا يكون المشتري هاهنا خصماً إلا بعد إثباتِ قيام العيب في يده، ولم يثبت، كما تقوم، وأما البينة فمشروعة لإثبات كونه خصماً». انتهى.
[٤] قوله : لأنه دور ؛ لأنَّ تحقق اليمين في هذه الصورة موقوف على كونه خصماً، وكونه خصماً موقوف على تحقق العيب عنده ، وتحقق العيب عنده موقوف على اليمين بشرط التكول فيكون دوراً ، فإنَّ الدَّورَ هو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف على ذلك الشيء، أما بمرتبته كما يتوقف على آب ويتوقف ب على آ أو بمرتبتين، كما يتوقف على ب وب على ج وج على آ أو بمراتب كما يتوقف آ على ب وب على جَ وجَ على دَ ودَ على آ.
أمَّا البَيِّنَةُ فقد تقام ليصير خصماً، لكن لا يَحْلِفُ ليصير خصماً. والفرق أن وجوب الحلفِ ضَرَرٌ ، فإذا لم يكن خصماً فلا وجه لإلزام الضَّرَرِ عليه بخلاف إقامةِ البَيِّنَةِ إذ المدَّعي مختار في إقامةِ البيِّنة، فهي أهون من الزامَ الضَّرَرِ عليه ، فجعل إقامة البيِّنة طريقاً لإثباتِ كونِهِ خصماً لا التحليف.
The book of sales: Option of defect. The scholars (mashayikh) differed regarding the statement of Abu Hanifa [that the buyer has the right to request an oath]. The basis for not administering an oath [to the seller] is that the oath is only directed at the defendant, and one does not become a defendant except after the existence of the defect is established in his possession. So it is not possible to prove this by swearing an oath because it is a circularity. The oath is upon the defendant. And al-Daraqutni also narrated it via Muslim ibn Khalid, from Ibn Jurayj, from 'Ata', from Abu Hurayra that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The burden of proof is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the denier except in cases of qasama.' If you wish further detail, refer to Nasb al-Raya and other works of takhrij.

[Commentary]: He said: 'The scholars differed...' Some said he swears, some said he does not swear, and this [latter] is the most correct. Its explanation has passed earlier; refer to it.

He said: 'Because the oath...' Its summary: The oath is only directed at the defendant, and one becomes a defendant only after the defect is established in his possession, and its establishment at that time cannot be except by oath, so circularity occurs, and thus administering an oath is not proved.

He said: 'And he does not become...' In al-'Inayah: We do not agree that everything on which bayyinah is based is also a basis for requiring an oath. For example, the claim of agency is based on bayyinah without oath. Bayyinah does not necessarily entail a claim, let alone its validity. Rather, it is established in matters where there is no claim at all, such as in hudud, unlike the oath. The difference is that the oath is prescribed to cut off litigation, so it requires the prior existence of a defendant. Here, the buyer is not a defendant until the existence of the defect in his possession is proven, and it is not proven [otherwise]. As for bayyinah, it is prescribed to prove that one is a defendant.

He said: 'Because it is circular' because the verification of the oath in this scenario is dependent on him being a defendant, and him being a defendant is dependent on the verification of the defect in his possession, and the verification of the defect in his possession is dependent on the oath (with the condition of taking it), so it is a circularity. Circularity is the dependence of a thing on that which depends on that thing, either in one step (as A depends on B and B depends on A) or in two steps (as A depends on B, B depends on C, and C depends on A) or in multiple steps.

As for bayyinah, it may be produced to become a defendant, but he does NOT swear to become a defendant. The difference is that the obligation of the oath is a harm, so if he is not a defendant, there is no justification for imposing harm upon him, unlike the production of bayyinah, since the claimant has a choice in producing bayyinah. Thus, it is easier than imposing harm upon him. So the production of bayyinah is made a means to prove that he is a defendant, not the oath.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a buyer who discovers a defect in a purchased item can force the seller to take an oath that he was unaware of the defect or that he sold it with disclosure. The text begins by stating that the mashayikh (later scholars) differed regarding Abu Hanifa's position. Some say the buyer may request an oath, others say no; the latter is considered the more correct view. The reasoning against the oath is circular: the oath can only be directed at a defendant (khasm), but the seller only becomes a defendant once the defect is proven in his possession, yet proving the defect requires the oath. To break this circularity, the passage distinguishes between bayyinah (proof) and yamin (oath). Bayyinah can be used even without a prior defendant status—it can establish that one is a defendant. But the oath requires a pre-existing defendant because it is a compulsory imposition of harm (darar). The buyer cannot be forced into harm if he is not yet a defendant. The passage also cites the famous hadith: 'The burden of proof is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the denier except in qasama.' This hadith supports the general principle. The commentary from al-'Inayah elaborates that bayyinah and tahleef are not equivalent: e.g., in a claim of agency, bayyinah is required but no oath. Also, bayyinah may be used in hudud where there is no claim. Thus, the lack of a defendant does not prevent bayyinah, but it does prevent requiring an oath.

Key Terms
استحلاف (istihlaf): Requesting an oath from the opposing party; in this context, the buyer demanding that the seller swear about the defect.
خصم (khasm): Defendant or opponent in litigation; the person against whom a claim is made and who can be subjected to an oath.
دور (dawr): Circular reasoning: A depends on B, B depends on C, and C depends on A.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do some scholars argue that the buyer cannot request an oath from the seller when a defect appears?
A) Because the buyer cannot be trusted.
B) Because the seller is not a defendant until the defect is proven, but proof requires an oath, creating circularity.
C) Because the hadith prohibits oaths in sales.
D) Because the buyer already paid the price.

Key Takeaway
The buyer cannot force the seller to swear about a defect because the oath presupposes a defendant, and the seller only becomes a defendant once the defect is proven—a circularity that prevents the oath from being a means of proof.

Dispute on Quantity of Defective Goods: The Buyer's Word Is Taken
Ruling on disputed quantity after possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the buyer's statement is accepted regarding the quantity of defective goods received after possession, and explain the analogy to usurpation (ghasp).

Core Idea
The recipient's word prevails on the quantity received — the qabid is believed.

This passage addresses a dispute that arises after the exchange of possession in a sale involving a defective item. The seller claims he sold two items (one defective), while the buyer insists he only bought one. The Hanafi school gives weight to the buyer's statement because he is the one who physically received the goods. The commentary explains the reasoning and draws an instructive analogy to the law of usurpation (ghasp), where the wrongful possessor's word is likewise taken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The seller claims after possession that he sold the defective item along with another item; the buyer admits only this one item. The dispute is about the quantity received (maqbud).
The setting is after taqabud (exchange of possession).
2
State the ruling
The buyer's word is accepted with his oath. The procedural rule favors the recipient of the goods.
This is a procedural ruling, not a substantive one.
3
Understand the rationale
The seller's claim reduces the refund if the item is returned, so he has an incentive to overstate the number of items. The dispute is about what was actually received, and the buyer (qabid) has direct knowledge.
The commentary in al-'Inaya explains the seller's motive.
4
Analyze the analogy to ghasp
In ghasp, if the usurper (ghasib) and the victim differ on the number of items taken, the usurper's word is taken because he is the qabid. This establishes a general principle: the possessor is believed regarding the possessed quantity.
The analogy is from al-Binaya.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may place the burden of proof on the seller to prove he sold two items. The Hanafi position is that the buyer's word is taken.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال البائع بعد التقابض : بعتك هذا المعيب مع آخر، فقال المشتري : بل هذا وحده، فالقول له ، وكذا إذا اتفقا في قدر المبيع، واختلفا في المقبوض
قال في «العناية: لا نسلّم أنَّ كلَّ ما يترتب عليه البيئة يترتب عليه التحليف، فإنَّ دعوى الوكالة يترتب عليها البينة دون التحليف ، والبيئة لا تستلزم الدعوى، فضلاً عن صحتها، بل تقوم على ما لا دعوى فيه أصلاً، كما في الحدود، بخلاف التحليف. والفرق أنَّ التحليف شُرعَ لقطع الخصومة، فكان مقتضياً سابقية الخصم، ولا يكون المشتري هاهنا خصماً إلا بعد إثباتِ قيام العيب في يده، ولم يثبت، كما تقوم، وأما البينة فمشروعة لإثبات كونه خصماً». انتهى.
[١] قوله : فقـد تـقـام...الخ ؛ وبهذا يندفع الدور في البينة : أي إقامة البينة لا تتوقف على الخصم، بل تقام ليصير خصماً.
[٢] قوله : البائع ؛ فائدة دعوى البائع أنه جرّ بنفع تنقيص الثَّمن على تقدير الردّ، ولهذا قال بعد التقابض
[٣]قوله : بعد التقابض ؛ أي بعد قبض المشتري المبيع، وقبض البائع الثمن.
[٤]قوله : كما في الغصب ؛ فإنّه إذا اختلف الغاصب والمغصوب منه ، فقـال المغصوب منه : غصبت مني غلامين، وقال الغاصب غصبتُ غلاماً واحداً، فالقولُ قولُ الغاصب ؛ لأنه القابض. كذا في «البناية».
And if the seller says after the exchange of possession: 'I sold you this defective item along with another,' and the buyer says: 'Rather, this one alone,' then the word is his (the buyer's). Likewise, if they agree on the quantity of the sale but differ on what was received [in quantity]. [Commentary]: He said: 'The benefit of the seller's claim is that it leads to a reduction of the price in the event of return, and that is why [the scenario is] after exchange of possession.' He said: 'After exchange of possession' means after the buyer took possession of the sold item and the seller took possession of the price. He said: 'As in ghasp: if the usurper and the usurped-from differ, the usurped-from says: You usurped two slaves from me, and the usurper says: I usurped one slave, then the word is the usurper's because he is the one in possession (al-qabid). So in al-Binaya.'
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a dispute after possession has been exchanged (ba'd al-taqabud). The seller claims that he sold the defective item along with another item (so that the price covers both), but the buyer says he only purchased this one item. The ruling is that the buyer's statement is accepted (with his oath), because the dispute is about the quantity received (maqbud). The commentary clarifies that the seller's motivation is to reduce the refund amount if the item is returned—by claiming there was another item, the price would be higher. The analogy is made to ghasp (usurpation): if the victim says you took two items and the wrongdoer says one, the word of the wrongdoer (the one in possession) is taken. The underlying principle is that the statement of the person who has physical possession (qabid) of the goods is given greater weight when there is disagreement about the number of items received. This is because the qabid is the one who actually holds the goods and has better knowledge of what he received.

Key Terms
قابض (qabid): The person who has taken possession; here, the buyer who received the goods.
مقبوض (maqbud): That which is taken possession of or received; the items actually delivered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute after possession over the number of defective goods sold, whose word does the Hanafi school accept?
A) The seller's, because he is the owner.
B) The buyer's, because he is the recipient (qabid).
C) Neither, the case is dismissed.
D) The judge decides based on external evidence.

Key Takeaway
After possession, the Hanafi school accepts the buyer's word regarding the quantity of defective goods received, analogously to the principle in ghasp that the possessor is believed.

Dispute After Exchange: Buyer's Word on Defect Sold Alone or with Another
Ruling on buyer's word when seller claims defect was sold with another item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the buyer's statement is taken in a dispute over the amount received after exchange and apply the analogy (qiyas) to the case of ghasp.

Core Idea
In disputes over the quantity received, the receiver's statement is taken with oath.

Imagine you buy a defective item and later the seller claims he sold it with another item you never received. Whose word should the court accept? The Hanafi school has a clear rule: the buyer, as the receiver, gets the benefit of the doubt. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, rooted in a powerful analogy to the law of ghasp (usurpation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The seller says after exchange that the defective item was sold together with another item; the buyer insists it was sold alone. This is a dispute over what was received (quantity), not over the defect itself.
Dispute occurs after both parties have exchanged possession (taqabud).
2
State the ruling
The buyer's statement is taken with an oath. This means the court accepts the buyer's claim unless the seller provides evidence.
The buyer is the receiver (qabid) in this context.
3
Trace the legal analogy (qiyas)
The ruling is derived by analogy to ghasp (usurpation): when an usurper and owner dispute the number of items taken, the usurper's word is taken because he is in possession. Here, the buyer is in possession, so his word is taken.
The effective cause (illa) is being the receiver in a dispute over the quantity received.
4
Understand the distinction on evidence vs. oath
A person may present evidence (bayyinah) to become a litigant, but cannot be forced to take an oath to become a litigant. The commentary explains that an oath is a burden (darar) that should not be imposed on a non-litigant.
This clarifies procedural rules of litigation.
5
Apply to a similar case
If the parties agree on the item sold but disagree on what was actually received, the same rule applies: the receiver's statement is taken.
Consistency in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال البائع بعد التقابض : بعتك هذا المعيب مع آخر، فقال المشتري : بل هذا وحده، فالقول له ، وكذا إذا اتفقا في قدر المبيع، واختلفا في المقبوض أما البينة فقد تقام ليصير خصماً، لكن لا يحلف ليصير خصماً. والفرق أن وجوب الحلف ضرر ، فإذا لم يكن خصماً فلا وجه لإلزام الضرر عليه بخلاف إقامة البينة إذ المدعي مختار في إقامة البينة، فهي أهون من الزام الضرر عليه ، فجعل إقامة البينة طريقاً لإثبات كونه خصماً لا التحليف. (ولو قال البائع بعد التقابض: بعتك هذا المعيب مع آخر، فقال المشتري: بل هذا وحده، فالقول له: أي إذا ظهر في المبيع بعد التقابض عيب فيرده المشتري ويطلب الثمن، فيقول البائع : هذا الثمن مقابل بهذا الشيء مع شيء آخر، ويقول المشتري : بل هو مقابل بهذا الشيء وحده، فالقول له مع اليمين ؛ لأن الاختلاف وقع في مقدار المقبوض ، فالقول للقابض ، كما في الغصب (وكذا إذا اتفقا في قدر المبيع، واختلفا في المقبوض) كذا في البناية.
And if after exchange the seller says: I sold you this defective item with another, and the buyer says: Rather this alone, then the statement is his (the buyer's). Similarly if they agree on the amount of the sold item and disagree on the received item. As for evidence, it may be presented to become a litigant, but he does not take an oath to become a litigant. The difference is that the obligation of an oath is harm, so if he is not a litigant there is no way to impose harm upon him, unlike presenting evidence because the claimant has a choice in presenting evidence; it is lighter than imposing harm upon him. So presenting evidence is made a means to establish being a litigant, not taking an oath. (And if the seller after exchange says: I sold you this defective item with another, and the buyer says: Rather this alone, then the statement is his: meaning if after exchange a defect appears in the sold item and the buyer returns it and demands the price, and the seller says: This price is in exchange for this thing along with something else, and the buyer says: Rather it is in exchange for this thing alone, then the statement is his with an oath, because the dispute occurs over the amount of the received item, so the statement is of the receiver, as in ghasp (usurpation). (And similarly if they agree on the amount of the sold item and disagree on the received item). As in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with rational proof from the commentary of the Wiqayah. The matn states that if a seller claims after exchange that he sold a defective item together with another item, but the buyer says the defective item was sold alone, the buyer's word is taken. This is derived from the principle that in disputes over the amount of what was received (qabd), the receiver's statement prevails, because he is the one who actually took possession. The commentary then addresses a subtle point: why can a person become a litigant by presenting evidence but not by taking an oath? The reason is that taking an oath is a burden (darar) and should not be imposed on someone who is not yet a litigant, whereas presenting evidence is a choice and less burdensome. The ruling is analogized to the case of ghasp (usurpation): if an usurper and the owner dispute the number of items usurped, the usurper's word is taken because he is the receiver. Thus, in sale disputes after exchange, the buyer (as receiver) is preferred.

Key Terms
التقابض (al-taqabud): The mutual exchange of possession: the buyer takes the goods and the seller takes the price.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession; the act of receiving an item in a transaction.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by a party in court to support their claim.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically witness testimony, presented in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute after exchange, the seller says he sold the defective item with another, but the buyer says it was sold alone. Why is the buyer's word taken?
A) Because the buyer has the right to choose the item.
B) Because the buyer is the receiver and the dispute is about the amount received.
C) Because the seller is considered untrustworthy.
D) Because the contract is not valid until both agree.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over the amount received after exchange, the receiver's statement is taken with an oath, analogized from the case of ghasp.

Return of Defective Goods in a Bundle Purchase
Ruling on returning one of two items bought together when one is defective

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning a defective item in a bundle purchase and explain the difference when one or both items are received, using the proof from khiyar al-`ayb.

Core Idea
After possession of both items, the defective one may be returned alone; before full possession, both must go together.

When a buyer purchases multiple items in a single transaction (safqah), the option of defect (khiyar al-`ayb) operates differently depending on whether all items have been received. This passage from al-Hidayah explains the rule and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf, Zufar, and the established position. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the concept of tafriq al-safqah and the significance of possession in finalizing the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and defect scenario
Recognise that the case involves two items (e.g., two slaves) bought in a single contract. The buyer takes possession of either one or both, then discovers a defect in one of them.
The ruling hinges on whether both items have been received.
2
Explain the ruling for partial vs. full possession
According to the matn (base text), if only one item is received, the buyer must keep both or return both; he cannot keep one and return one. If both are received, he may return only the defective item.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that if the received item is defective, it can be returned alone even if only one was received. Zufar holds that even after both are received, both must be returned. Understand each imam's reasoning.
Abu Yusuf argues completion of the transaction for the received item; Zufar analogises to splitting before possession and to khiyar al-ru'yah.
4
Apply the mufta bihi position to a new scenario
Given a new fact pattern (e.g., two books bought, one received and found defective, or both received), determine the correct outcome using the relied-upon Hanafi rule.
Ensure you distinguish partial from full possession.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى عبدين صفقة، وقبض أحدَهُما ووجد به أو بالآخر عيباً أخذهما أو ردهما، ولو قبضَهُمَا رَدَّ المعيب خاصةً أي اتفقا في أنَّ المبيع شيئان، واختلفا في المقبوض ، فقال المشتري : قبضتُ أحدهما فقط ، وقال البائع : بل قبضتهما فالقول للمشتري على ما مر. (ولو اشترى عبدين صفقة ، وقبض أحدَهُما ووجد به أو بالآخر عيباً أخذهما أو ردَّهُما ، ولو قبضَهُمَا رَدَّ المعيب خاصةً) قوله : على ما مر ؛ من أنَّ في الاختلاف في مقدار المقبوض القول قول القابض، بل هاهنا أولى؛ لأنَّ كون المبيع شيئين إمارة ظاهرة على أنَّ المقبوض كذلك ؛ لأنَّ العقد عليهما سبب مطلق لقبضهما. كذا في العناية. قوله : عبدين ... الخ ؛ قيد العبدين اتفاقي ؛ إذ الحكم في الثوبين كذلك. كذا في المعدن. قوله : وجد به أو بالآخر؛ وعن أبي يوسف : إذا وجد بالمقبوض عيباً رده خاصةً ؛ لأنَّ الصفقة فيه تمت لتناهيها فيه، فبالنظر إليه لا يلزم تفريق الصفقة. قوله : ولو قبضهما ؛ حاصله : إنّ المشتري إذا اشترى عبدين وقبضهما، ثمَّ وجد بأحدهما عيباً يردّه خاصةً خلافاً لزفر ؛ فإنّ عنده يردهما جميعاً ؛ لأن فيه تفريق الصفقة، ولا يخلو عن ضرر ; لأنّ من عادات التجار ضم الجيد إلى الرديء الرواج الرديء فإذا جاز ردُّ أحدهما دون الآخر يفوت غرض البائع، وفيه ضرر. فأشبه هذا التفريق الذي هو بعد القبض بالتفريقِ الذي قبل القبض، وفيه الردّ لأحدهما غير جائز فكذا هاهنا وبخيار الرؤية والشَّرط، فإنَّ الصفقة لا تتم فيهما بعد القبض، فكذا هاهنا أيضاً. ولنا : ردُّ أحدهما تفريق الصفقة بعد تمامها، وهو جائز؛ لأنَّ الصفقة في خيارِ العيب تتم بالقبض، وفي خيار الرؤية والشَّرط لا تتم به ; لأنَّ تمامها لا يكون إلا بتمام الرضاء، وهو لم يوجد ، وقد مرَّ سابقاً. وقيل : هذا ; أي ردُّ المعيب خاصةً في شيئين إفراد أحدهما بالانتفاع كالعبدين.
And if he buys two slaves in one transaction and takes possession of one of them and finds a defect in it or in the other, he takes both or returns both. And if he takes possession of both, he returns the defective one specifically. That is, they agree that the sold items are two, and they disagree on what was received: the buyer says 'I took possession of only one', and the seller says 'You took possession of both.' The statement is for the buyer according to what has passed. (If he buys two slaves in one transaction and takes possession of one and finds a defect in it or the other, he takes both or returns both; and if he takes possession of both, he returns the defective one specifically.) His statement: 'according to what has passed' means that in a dispute over the amount of the received, the statement is that of the receiver; and here it is more appropriate because the fact that the sold items are two is a clear indication that the received is likewise, since the contract on them is an absolute cause for taking possession of both. As in al-`Inayah. His statement: 'two slaves' is an incidental specification; the ruling applies similarly to two garments. In al-Ma`din. His statement: 'finding a defect in it or the other' — from Abu Yusuf is that if he finds a defect in the received item, he returns it specifically because the transaction is completed with regard to it due to its finalization, so there is no requirement to split the transaction. His statement: 'and if he takes possession of both' — its summary is that if the buyer buys two slaves and takes possession of both, then finds a defect in one of them, he returns it specifically, contrary to Zufar, because according to him he returns both due to splitting the transaction, and it is not free from harm because it is the custom of merchants to combine good with bad to promote the bad, so if it is permissible to return one without the other, the seller's purpose is defeated and there is harm. He analogized this splitting after possession to splitting before possession where returning one is not permissible, and similarly in option of inspection and option of condition, the transaction is not finalized after possession, so here too. And our proof is that returning one is splitting the transaction after its completion, and that is permissible because the transaction in option of defect is finalized by possession, while in option of inspection and option of condition it is not finalized thereby because finalization only occurs with complete satisfaction, which has not occurred. It is said: this, i.e., returning the defective one specifically in two items, is an assignment of one for benefit, like two slaves.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the return of defective items purchased in a single transaction (صفقة). The matn states: if a person buys two items (e.g., two slaves) in one contract, and receives only one, then if either item is found defective, he must either keep both or return both — he cannot keep one and return one. However, if he received both items, then he may return only the defective one. The commentary clarifies that this ruling is based on the principle that the receiver's word is taken in disputes over what was received, and the context of two items makes it more likely that both were received. It then reports two positions: Abu Yusuf holds that if the defective item was the one received, it can be returned alone even if only one was received, because the transaction is complete regarding that specific item. Zufar holds that even after receiving both, the buyer must return both if one is defective, comparing it to splitting the transaction before possession or to option of inspection. The relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is the matn's ruling: after possession of both, only the defective one is returned, because the transaction is complete by possession in khiyar al-`ayb (option of defect), unlike khiyar al-ru'yah where possession does not finalize it.

Key Terms
صفقة (safqah): A single transaction involving multiple items; a bundle purchase.
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the transaction by returning part of the items while keeping others.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-`ayb): The option to return a defective item purchased; right to rescind the contract for a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases two garments in one transaction and receives both. He later finds a defect in one. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what can he do?
A) He must return both garments.
B) He may return only the defective garment.
C) He must keep both and cannot return any.
D) He may return both or keep both, but cannot return only one.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows returning only the defective item in a bundle purchase only after possession of all items; before full possession, the buyer must keep or return the entire bundle.

Return of Defective Item in a Single Contract for Two Items
Ruling with proof for khiyar al-'ayb in multi-item transactions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning a defective item when two goods are purchased in one contract, apply the condition of independent usability, and evaluate Zufar's counterargument based on khiyar al-ru'ya.

Core Idea
After possession, the sale is complete; returning only the defective item is permissible without harming the transaction.

When a buyer purchases two items together and finds a defect after taking possession, can he return only the defective one? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, the intra-madhhab dispute with Zufar, and the key distinction between khiyar al-'ayb and khiyar al-ru'ya. The condition that each item is independently usable is central.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and dispute
Read the opening sentence: if the buyer takes possession of both, he returns only the defective one. Note that this is the majority Hanafi view, opposed by Zufar.
The ruling applies to any two individually usable items, not just slaves.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The passage states 'لنا' (for us): the sale in khiyar al-'ayb is completed by possession (qabd). Therefore, returning one item after completion does not harm the seller—his purpose is fulfilled.
Compare with khiyar al-ru'ya where the sale is not complete by possession alone.
3
Analyze Zufar's counterargument
Zufar argues that returning only one splits the transaction and harms the seller, as merchants bundle good with bad. He draws an analogy to splitting before possession and to khiyar al-ru'ya.
Identify why the analogy fails according to the Hanafis.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the majority Hanafi view, buyer keeps the sound item and returns only the defective. Under Zufar, buyer must return both.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى عبدين صفقة، وقبض أحدَهُما ووجد به أو بالآخر عيباً أخذهما أو ردهما، ولو قبضَهُمَا رَدَّ المعيب خاصةً. أي اتفقا في أنَّ المبيع شيئان، واختلفا في المقبوض، فقال المشتري: قبضتُ أحدهما فقط، وقال البائع: بل قبضتهما فالقول للمشتري على ما مرَّ. قوله: على ما مرَّ؛ من أنَّ في الاختلاف في مقدار المقبوض القول قول القابض، بل هاهنا أولى؛ لأنَّ كون المبيع شيئين إمارة ظاهرة على أنَّ المقبوض كذلك؛ لأنَّ العقدَ عليهما سبب مطلق لقبضهما. كذا في «العناية». قوله: عبدين... الخ؛ قيد العبدين اتفاقي؛ إذ الحكم في الثوبين كذلك. كذا في «المعدن». قوله: وجد به أو بالآخر؛ وعن أبي يوسف: إذا وجد بالمقبوض عيباً رده خاصةً؛ لأنَّ الصفقة فيه تمت لتناهيها فيه، فبالنظر إليه لا يلزم تفريق الصفقة. قوله: ولو قبضهما؛ حاصله: إنّ المشتري إذا اشترى عبدين وقبضهما، ثمَّ وجد بأحدهما عيباً يردّه خاصةً خلافاً لزفر؛ فإنّ عنده يردهما جميعاً؛ لأن فيه تفريق الصفقة، ولا يخلو عن ضرر؛ لأنّ من عادات التجار ضم الجيد إلى الرديء الرواج الرديء فإذا جاز ردُّ أحدهما دون الآخر يفوت غرض البائع، وفيه ضرر. فأشبه هذا التفريق الذي هو بعد القبض بالتفريقِ الذي قبل القبض، وفيه الردّ لأحدهما غير جائز فكذا هاهنا وبخيار الرؤية والشَّرط، فإنَّ الصفقة لا تتم فيهما بعد القبض، فكذا هاهنا أيضاً. ولنا: ردُّ أحدهما تفريق الصفقة بعد تمامها، وهو جائز؛ لأنَّ الصفقة في خيارِ العيب تتم بالقبض، وفي خيار الرؤية والشَّرط لا تتم به؛ لأنَّ تمامها لا يكون إلا بتمام الرضاء، وهو لم يوجد، وقد مرَّ سابقاً. وقيل: هذا؛ أي ردُّ المعيب خاصةً في شيئين إفراد أحدهما بالانتفاع كالعبدين.
And if he bought two slaves in one transaction, and took possession of one of them and found a defect in it or in the other, he may take both or return both. But if he took possession of both, he returns only the defective one specifically. Meaning: They agree that the sold items are two, but they differed regarding what was taken possession. The buyer said: 'I took possession of only one,' and the seller said: 'Rather, you took possession of both.' The statement is the buyer's, as previously mentioned. His statement: 'as previously mentioned': from that in a dispute over the amount of the taken possession, the statement is that of the taker, indeed here it is more appropriate because the fact that the sold items are two is a clear indication that what was taken is likewise; because the contract on them is an absolute cause for taking possession of them. As in al-'Inayah. His statement: 'two slaves, etc.': the qualification of two slaves is incidental; indeed the ruling for two garments is likewise. As in al-Ma'din. His statement: 'found a defect in it or in the other': and from Abu Yusuf: if he found a defect in what was taken, he returns it specifically; because the transaction is completed in it due to its finality in it, so with respect to it, splitting the transaction is not necessitated. His statement: 'but if he took possession of both': its summary: Indeed the buyer, if he bought two slaves and took possession of both, then found a defect in one of them, he returns it specifically, contrary to Zufar; because according to him, he returns both, because it involves splitting the transaction and is not free of harm; because among the customs of merchants is combining good with bad to promote the bad. If returning one without the other is permitted, the seller's purpose is lost and there is harm. So this splitting after possession resembles splitting before possession, where returning one is not permissible, and likewise here, and in khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart, indeed the transaction is not completed by possession, so likewise here. And for us: returning one is splitting the transaction after its completion, and it is permissible; because the transaction in khiyar al-'ayb is completed by possession, while in khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart it is not completed thereby; because its completion is only by complete consent, which did not exist, and it has passed earlier. And it was said: this, i.e., returning the defective one specifically in two things is where each is individually usable like the two slaves.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of sales regarding khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) when multiple items are sold in one contract. The base rule states: if a buyer buys two slaves in one transaction and takes possession of one, then finds a defect in the taken one or the other, he has the option to keep both or return both. If he takes possession of both and finds a defect in one, he may return only the defective one. The commentary explains that if the buyer and seller dispute over how many were taken, the buyer's word is accepted, as is the general rule. The qualification of 'two slaves' is not exclusive; the same applies to two garments. Abu Yusuf holds that if the defect is in the received item, the buyer can return only that one because the sale is complete regarding it. Zufar opposes the main ruling, arguing that returning only one splits the transaction and harms the seller. Zufar analogizes to khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart where the sale is not complete until consent. The Hanafi position (لنا) is that the sale in khiyar al-'ayb is completed by possession (qabd), so after possession the transaction is final; thus returning one does not harm because the sale is already complete. In contrast, in khiyar al-ru'ya and al-shart, the sale is not complete by possession alone—it requires full consent—so splitting before completion is not allowed. This ruling applies when each item is individually usable (إفراد أحدهما بالانتفاع), like slaves or garments.

Key Terms
صفقة (safqah): A single transaction or contract involving multiple items sold together.
إفراد أحدهما بالانتفاع (ifrād aḥadihimā bil-intifāʿ): Each item can be used independently and separately from the other.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must return both items.
B) He may return only the defective item, provided each item is individually usable.
C) He cannot return anything because the sale is final.
D) He must return the defective item only if the seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-'ayb, once possession is taken, the sale is complete; therefore, returning only the defective item from a multi-item transaction is permissible if the items are independently usable.

Return of Defective Items: Homogeneous Goods and Non-Individual Items
Explains the rulings for weight/measure goods and non-individual items with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for returning a defective item when goods are sold by weight/measure or when items are not individually usable, explain the reasoning based on unity of transaction, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the condition of qabd.

Core Idea
For goods sold by measure/weight, you must return all or keep all; for a pair that is useless separately, return both or keep both.

This lesson extends the khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) to two specific contexts: homogeneous goods sold by weight or measure, and items that are not individually usable. In both cases, partial return is prohibited because the transaction is considered a single unit. The passage also discusses an intra-Hanafi difference on whether this ruling applies before or after possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two distinct cases: (a) goods sold by weight/measure (e.g., wheat, sugar), and (b) items that are not individually usable (e.g., a pair of socks, door leaves). Look for the key phrases: 'مكيلي أو وزني' and 'لا ينفع إفراد أحدهما بالانتفاع'.
These are separate rulings under khiyar al-'ayb.
2
State the ruling for weight/measure goods
If a defect is found in part of the goods after possession, the buyer must either return the entire quantity or keep it all. He cannot return only the defective portion. Note the condition 'قبض' (possession) mentioned by some authorities.
This applies to homogeneous goods where the price is per unit.
3
State the ruling for non-individual items
For items like a pair of socks, if one is defective, both must be returned or kept. This is because neither is useful without the other; the transaction is considered a single unit.
The mashāyikh extended this to a pair of oxen accustomed to each other.
4
Examine the reasoning
The text provides two lines of reasoning: (a) for non-individual items, splitting the transaction before completion is prohibited (the transaction is not completed without the pair), (b) an analogy: possession is similar to the contract, so splitting possession is like splitting the contract, which is invalid.
The prohibition of splitting is derived from general principles, not a specific text.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on qabd
Some scholars (e.g., al-Kāfī) hold that the ruling applies regardless of whether possession occurred, while others (al-Hidāyah) state it applies only after possession. The author notes that al-Mukhtaṣar and al-Kanz omit the condition, implying the latter view is mufta bihi.
This ikhtilaf is relevant to the practical application of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكيلى أو وزني قُبِضَ إن وجد ببعضهِ عيباً رُدَّ كلّه أو أَخَدَه. أما إذا لم تكن إفراد أحدهما بالانتفاع: كزوجي الخف، ومصراعي الباب، فإنّه يردهما أو يمسكهما، حتى قال مشايخنا: إذا اشترى زوجي ثور فقبضهما فوجد بأحدهما عيباً، وقد ألف أحدهما الآخرِ بحيث لا يعمل بدونه لا يملك ردَّ المعيب خاصة. كذا في «الكفاية» و«مجمع الأنهر». قوله: لأن الصفقة ... الخ؛ حاصله: إنَّ الصفقة لا تتم إلا بالآخر، فيكون ردّ أحدهما بدون الآخرِ تفريقاً قبل التمام، وهو منهي، فلا يجوز ردّ أحدهما، بل إن شاء أخذهما جميعاً، وإن شاء ردَّهما جميعاً. قوله: لا يجوز؛ لأنَّ القبض مشابه بالعقدِ من حيث أنَّ القبض يُثبت ملك التصرّف واليد، كما أنَّ العقد يثبت ملك الرقبة، والغرض من مِلكِ الرَّقبةِ مِلكُ التصرف واليد، فالتفريق في القبض كالتفريق في العقد، فلو قال البائع: بعت منك هذين العبدين، فقال المشتري: قبلت أحدهما، لم يجز، فكذا هاهنا لا يجوز. قوله: يجوز؛ لأنَّ النهي ليس بواردٍ في تفريق الصفقة بعد تمامها، وليس فيه ضرر للبائع ولا للمشتري. قوله: وكيلي...الخ؛ صورته: اشترى عشرة أقفزة من الحنطة، أو عشرة أمناء من السكر، فوجد ببعضها عيباً، فهو بالخيار إن شاءَ ردَّ كلَّه أو أخذ كلَّه. قوله: قبض؛ واعلم أنَّ المصنف ذكر هذا القيد كما ذكره صاحب «الهداية»، وقد صرَّحَ في «الكافي» بأنه سواءً كان قبل القبض أو بعده، وبالنظر إلى هذا التصريح لم يذكر هذا القيد في «المختصر» و«الكنز».
And for that which is sold by measure or weight, if it is taken possession of and a defect is found in part of it, the whole is returned or he takes it. As for when each item is not individually usable: such as a pair of socks and the two leaves of a door, then he returns both or keeps both, such that our mashāyikh said: if he bought a pair of oxen and took possession of them and found a defect in one, and one had become accustomed to the other such that it cannot work without it, he does not have the right to return only the defective one. As in al-Kifāyah and Majma' al-Anhur. His statement: 'because the transaction...' etc.: Its summary: Indeed the transaction is not completed except with the other, so returning one without the other is a split before completion, and it is prohibited, so it is not permissible to return one; rather, if he wishes he takes both, and if he wishes he returns both. His statement: 'it is not permissible'; because possession is similar to the contract in that possession establishes ownership of disposal and physical control, just as the contract establishes ownership of the corpus, and the purpose of ownership of the corpus is ownership of disposal and physical control, so split in possession is like split in the contract. If the seller said: 'I sold you these two slaves,' and the buyer said: 'I accepted one,' it is not valid; so likewise here it is not permissible. His statement: 'it is permissible'; because the prohibition does not apply to splitting the transaction after its completion, and there is no harm to the seller or buyer. His statement: 'and for that which is by measure or weight...' etc.; its form: he bought ten qafīz of wheat, or ten mann of sugar, and found a defect in some of it, he has the option: if he wishes he returns all or takes all. His statement: 'taken possession of'; and know that the author mentioned this condition as the author of al-Hidāyah mentioned it, and he stated in al-Kāfī that it is the same whether before or after possession, and with reference to this statement he did not mention this condition in al-Mukhtaṣar and al-Kanz.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage addresses two additional scenarios in khiyar al-'ayb. First, when goods are sold by weight (مكيال) or measure (میزان), such as wheat or sugar: if the buyer takes possession and finds a defect in part of the goods, he must either return the entire quantity or keep it all; he cannot return only the defective portion because the goods are homogenous and the price is calculated per unit, but the transaction is for a single commodity. Second, when items are not individually usable (لا إفراد بالانتفاع), like a pair of socks or the two leaves of a door—neither is useful without the other. In such cases, if a defect is found in one after possession, the buyer must return both or keep both; he cannot return only the defective one because separating them would render the remaining item useless. The mashāyikh extended this to a pair of oxen that have become accustomed to each other. The commentary discusses the reasoning: for non-usable items, the transaction is not completed without the pair, so returning only one is a split before completion, which is prohibited. It also notes that possession is analogous to the contract: just as you cannot accept only one of two slaves in the contract, you cannot take possession of only one after the contract; the same prohibition applies to returning only one before completion. However, after possession and completion, splitting is allowed if there is no harm. Regarding the condition of possession (قبض) in the text of the matn, there is a difference: al-Hidayah mentions it, but al-Kāfī states it is the same whether before or after possession; accordingly, al-Mukhtaṣar and al-Kanz omit the condition.

Key Terms
إفراد أحدهما بالانتفاع (ifrād aḥadihimā bil-intifāʿ): Each item can be used independently without needing the other.
زوجي الخف (zawjay al-khuff): A pair of socks or shoes; each is useless without the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases ten qafīz of wheat (sold by measure). After taking possession, he finds a defect in three qafīz. According to the Hanafi ruling, what are his options?
A) He may return only the defective three qafīz.
B) He may keep all or return all.
C) He must keep all and cannot return any.
D) He may return the defective three and keep the rest.

Key Takeaway
When goods are sold by weight/measure or are not individually usable, the transaction is considered a single unit, so partial return of a defective part is prohibited; the buyer must either keep all or return all.

Indivisibility of Makil/Mawzun in Defect and Istihqaq
Commentary on rulings for defective or claimed parts of weighed/measured goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a buyer of makil/mawzun must return or keep the entire lot when a defect is found, and distinguish this from the ruling on istihqaq and from other goods like cloth.

Core Idea
A weighed or measured item is legally indivisible: a defect in part binds the whole; third-party claim does not permit return of the remainder.

This lesson examines two related rulings in the Hanafi school regarding items sold by weight or measure. First, when a defect is found in part of such goods after possession, the buyer cannot return only the defective portion—he must take or leave the entire lot. Second, if a third party establishes ownership of part, the buyer cannot return the remainder because partiality is not a defect in makil/mawzun. We will explore the reasoning, the analogy to contracts, and the minority opinion about separate containers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item type
Determine whether the sold item is makil (sold by volume), mawzun (sold by weight), or other (like cloth or slaves). This classification triggers different rules.
Makil/mawzun are considered legally indivisible because their value arises from aggregation.
2
Check possession status
The ruling differs before and after possession. Before possession, splitting the transaction is prohibited for all items. After possession, only makil/mawzun remain indivisible.
The commentary notes that al-Kafi says possession is irrelevant; the rule applies equally before and after.
3
Apply the defect ruling
If a defect is found in part of makil/mawzun after possession, the buyer must return the whole or keep the whole. He cannot return only the defective part.
The reason: the transaction is only completed by possession; splitting before completion is prohibited. After completion, makil/mawzun are treated as a single unit.
4
Apply the istihqaq ruling
If a third party claims part of the sold makil/mawzun, the buyer does not have the right to return the remaining part. This contrasts with a cloth, where partiality is a defect.
For cloth, splitting is a defect that harms value/usage. For makil/mawzun, partnership does not harm because the whole is conceptually one.
5
Evaluate the container dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (Abu Ja'far) held that if the goods are in separate containers, each container is independent, so the buyer can return only the defective container. The majority (Imam Muhammad, al-Sarakhsi) reject this.
The mufta bihi is no distinction; the buyer must return all or keep all regardless of containers.

The gist of al-Nihayah for the support of al-Ri'ayah: And for something sold by measure or weight that has been taken into possession, if a defect is found in part of it, the whole is returned or the whole is taken [2] because the transaction is only completed by possession, so before possession splitting the transaction is not permissible, and after possession it is permissible. (And for something sold by measure or weight that has been taken into possession) if a defect is found in part of it, the whole is returned or he takes the whole. But if the separation of one of the two does not allow benefit [individually] — like a pair of socks, or the two leaves of a door — then he returns both or keeps both. Even our scholars said: If someone buys a pair of oxen and takes possession of them, then finds a defect in one of them, and each has become accustomed to the other such that it does not work without it, he does not have the right to return only the defective one. This is in al-Kifayah and Majma' al-Anhur. [1] His statement: 'because the transaction...' etc.; its summary: the transaction is only completed by the other, so returning one without the other is splitting before completion, which is prohibited, so it is not permissible to return one, but if he wishes he takes both, and if he wishes he returns both. (2) His statement: 'not permissible'; because possession is similar to the contract in that possession establishes ownership of disposal and physical control, just as the contract establishes ownership of the corpus, and the purpose of owning the corpus is ownership of disposal and control, so splitting in possession is like splitting in the contract. So if the seller said: 'I sold you these two slaves,' and the buyer said: 'I accepted one of them,' it is not valid, so likewise here it is not permissible. [3] His statement: 'is permissible'; because the prohibition does not apply to splitting the transaction after its completion, and there is no harm to the seller or buyer. [4] His statement: 'and for something sold by measure or weight...' etc.; its scenario: He bought ten qafeez of wheat, or ten maunds of sugar, and found a defect in part of it; he has the option: if he wishes he returns all, or he takes all. [0] His statement: 'possession'; and know that the author (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned this condition, as the author of al-Hidayah mentioned it, and in al-Kafi he explicitly stated that it is the same whether before or after possession, and considering this explicit statement he did not mention this condition in al-Mukhtasar and al-Kanz. (1) al-Kifayah (6:30-31). (2) Majma' al-Anhur (2:50). (3) al-Hidayah (3:41). (4) Kanz al-Daqa'iq (p.100).

Book of Sale, Option of Defect: And if part of it is claimed [by a third party], he does not return the rest, unlike a cloth. [1] 107 Because if it is of the same type, it is like a single thing. It is said: this is if it is in one container, so that if it is in two containers, it is equivalent to two slaves, so he returns the container that has the defect. [4] (And if part of it is claimed, he does not return the rest, unlike a cloth (3) because splitting does not harm it (4) (1) And it is said in Majma' al-Anhur: 'It would be better if he left it.' But Allamah al-Ayni (2) said regarding the benefit of this condition: 'If it is before possession, the ruling among us does not differ between [items sold by] measure/weight and others in that it is not permissible to split the transaction by returning only the defective item. But if after possession, it is permissible to split the transaction for other than measure/weight items, but for them it is not permissible.' End. [1] His statement: 'it is like a single thing'; because monetary value and assessment in items sold by measure and weight is based on combination and aggregation; since a single grain is not considered valuable such that its sale is impermissible, and when the monetary value in them is based on combination and aggregation, the whole becomes like a single thing in terms of sale; hence it is called by a single name such as 'kurr' and the like, and seeing part of it is like seeing all of it. :: [2] His statement: 'it is said this...' etc.; said in al-Kifayah (3): 'Fa'qih Abu Ja'far used to give fatwa according to it and claimed it was a narration from our companions, and they said: there is no difference between whether it is in one container or containers; he does not have the right to return part due to defect. The unrestricted statement of Muhammad in the book indicates it, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi used to say it.' End. [3] His statement: 'unlike a cloth'; if the sold item is a cloth and the buyer has taken possession, then part of it is claimed, the buyer has the option to return the remainder; because splitting in a cloth is a defect; since it harms its monetary value and benefit, as they explicitly stated. (4) His statement: 'because splitting does not harm it'; and partnership in [items sold by] measure and weight is not considered a defect, and this is the answer to the objection: if the sold item by measure/weight is like a single thing, then he should have the right to return the remainder when part is claimed, as in the case of a single cloth or single slave.
(1) Majma' al-Anhur (2:50). (2) In al-Binayah (6:360). (3) al-Kifayah (6:32-33).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on rulings concerning items sold by weight or measure (makil and mawzun) when a defect is found in part of them, or when part is claimed by a third party. The passage type is ruling_with_proof, as it provides reasoning and analogies. The author first states the main ruling: if a buyer finds a defect in part of a weighed/measured item after taking possession, he must either return the whole or keep the whole; he cannot return only the defective part. The reason given is that the sale transaction is only completed by possession, and before completion, splitting the transaction is prohibited. An analogy is made to a contract: if a seller offers two slaves and the buyer accepts only one, the sale is invalid; similarly, rejecting part of the goods before possession is like splitting the contract. After possession, splitting is generally allowed, but for weighed/measured items, it remains prohibited because they are considered a single unit—their value arises from aggregation (a single grain has no market value). The passage also discusses exceptions: if the items are paired (like a pair of oxen accustomed to each other), the buyer cannot separate them. A differing opinion is mentioned regarding containers: some scholars say that if the weighed/measured item is in two containers, it is treated as two separate items, so the buyer can return only the defective container. However, the relied-upon view is that no distinction between containers exists. The second part of the passage addresses istihqaq (a third party claiming ownership of part). For a cloth, if part is claimed, the buyer may return the remainder because partiality is a defect. For weighed/measured items, partiality (being shared with someone else) is not a defect, so the buyer cannot return the remainder; this is because partnership in such items does not harm their value or usage.

Key Terms
المكيل (al-makil): Items sold by volume measure, such as grains or liquids
الموزون (al-mawzun): Items sold by weight, such as sugar or metals
الصفقة (al-safqah): The transaction or bargain struck in a sale
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession or physical delivery of the sold item
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A third party's legal claim to ownership of part of the sold item
التبعضي (al-tab'id): Splitting or dividing the sold item into parts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases 10 kg of rice (makil) and after taking possession finds a defect in part of it. According to the Hanafi school, what are his options?
A) He may return only the defective portion.
B) He must return the entire 10 kg or keep it all.
C) He may keep the defective portion and return the rest.
D) He must return the entire lot immediately without option.

Key Takeaway
For items sold by measure or weight, the Hanafi school treats the entire purchase as a single unit: a defect in part binds the whole, and a third-party claim on part does not allow return of the remainder.

When Part of Sold Goods is Reclaimed: The Hanafi Rule on Returning the Remainder
Primary ruling with explanation on returning remainder when part is reclaimed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer's obligation when part of a sold item of the same genus is reclaimed, and distinguish between measured/weighed goods and goods like cloth.

Core Idea
For measured/weighed goods, partition is no defect; the buyer must keep the remainder.

In this passage from the Book of Sale, we examine a key scenario: a buyer purchases a quantity of goods of a single genus (e.g., grain). After taking possession, a third party proves ownership of a portion. What happens to the rest? The Hanafi school draws a sharp line between goods whose value lies in aggregation (measurable or weighable items) and those where cutting reduces practical utility (like cloth). Let's unpack the ruling, its rationale, and the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the partial reclamation (istihqāq) of a sold item of a single genus after the buyer has taken possession. The ruling: the buyer does not return the remaining part; he must keep it and claim a price reduction.
This is the default rule for measured (makīl) or weighed (mawzūn) goods.
2
Understand the rationale
The text explains that such goods derive their value from aggregation. A single grain of rice has no independent market value; the worth is in the whole. Therefore, the entire lot is like one thing. Partition does not harm the value of the remaining portion.
Seeing part is like seeing all: 'رؤية بعضه كرؤية كله'.
3
Contrast with cloth
If the sold item is a cloth (thawb) and part is reclaimed, the buyer may return the remainder. Cutting cloth into pieces reduces its utility and market price; partition is a defect.
This exception shows that the rule hinges on whether partition harms value.
4
Note the container nuance
If the goods are in two separate containers, each container is treated as a distinct item (like two slaves). Thus, the buyer returns only the container with the defective part.
There is an intra-Hanafi variant: some early scholars (Abu Ja'far) held no difference between one or two containers, but the muftā bihi follows Imam Muhammad's view.
5
Distinguish before vs. after possession
The commentator al-‘Aynī clarifies: before possession, splitting the contract is not allowed for any type of good. After possession, for non-measured/weighed goods, splitting is allowed; for measured/weighed, it is not.
This reinforces that the special status of measured/weighed goods is only after delivery.

( ولو استحق بعضه لم يَرُدُّ باقيه بخلاف الثَّوب لأنَّهُ لا يضرُّهُ التبعيض (1) (وقال في «مجمع الأنهر ) : «لو تركه لكان أولى». لكن قال العلامة العيني في فائدة هذا القيد : «إنّه إذا كان قبل القبض لا يتفاوتُ الحكم عندنا بين المكيل والموزون وغيرهما في أنه لا يجوز تفريق الصفقة برد المعيب خاصة، وأما إذا كان بعد القبض فيجوز تفريق الصفقة فيما عدا المكيل والموزون ، وأما فيهما فلا يجوز». انتهى. قوله : فهو كشيء واحد ؛ لأنَّ المالية والتقوم في المكيلات والموزونات باعتبارِ الاجتماع والانضمام ؛ إذ الحبَّةُ الواحدة ليست بمتقومةٍ حتى لا يجوز بيعها، وإذا كانت المالية فيها باعتبار الاجتماع والانضمام صارَ الكل في حق البيع كشيء واحد ؛ ولهذا يسمى باسم واحد وهو الكرّ ونحوه، وكان رؤية بعضه كرؤية كله. قوله : وقيل هذا...الخ ؛ قال في «الكفاية» : «وكان الفقيه أبو جعفر له يفتي به ويزعم أنه رواية عن أصحابنا له ، وقالوا : لا فرق بين ما إذا كان في وعاءٍ واحد أو أوعية ليس له أن يردّ البعض بالعيب ، وإطلاق محمد له في الكتاب يدل عليه، وبه كان يقول شمس الأئمة السَّرَخْسِي ». انتهى. قوله : بخلاف الثوب ؛ فإنّه إذا كان المبيع ثوباً وقد قبضه المشتري ، ثم استحق بعضه، فللمشتري الخيار في ردّ ما بقي ؛ لأنَّ التبعيض في الثوب عيب ؛ لأنه يضرُّ في ماليته والانتفاع به كما صرحوا به. قوله : لأنه لا يضره التبعيض ؛ والشركة في الكيلي والوزني لا تعد عيباً، وهذا هو الجواب عن أن يقال: إذا كان المكيلُ والموزون كشيء واحدٍ يجب أن يكون له ردُّ الباقي إذا استحق بعضه، كما في الثوب الواحد والعبد الواحد.
Book of Sale: Option of Defect. And if part of it is reclaimed, he does not return the remainder, contrary to a cloth. Because if it is of one genus, it is like one thing. And it is said: this is if it is in one container, so much so that if it were in two containers, it is like two slaves, so he returns the container that has the defective one. And if part is reclaimed, he does not return the remainder, contrary to the cloth, because partition does not harm it. ... He said in Majma' al-Anhur: 'If he left it, it would be better.' But al-Allama al-Ayni said regarding the benefit of this condition: 'If it is before possession, the ruling does not differ with us between measured/weighed and others in that it is not permissible to split the deal by returning only the defective. As for after possession, splitting the deal is permissible except for measured/weighed. And in them it is not permissible.' ... He said: it is like one thing because valuation and value in measured and weighed items are based on aggregation and combination, for a single grain is not a valuable item so its sale is not permissible. And when value is based on aggregation, the whole becomes like one thing in terms of sale; hence it is called by one name like 'karr' and the like, and seeing part of it is like seeing all of it. ... He said regarding 'it is said': ... The faqih Abu Ja'far used to give fatwa by it and claimed it as a narration from our companions. And they said: there is no difference between whether it is in one container or multiple containers; he does not have the right to return part due to defect. And Muhammad's absolute statement in the book indicates it, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi used to say it. ... He said: contrary to the cloth; for if the sold item is a cloth and the buyer has taken possession, then part is reclaimed, the buyer has the option to return what remains because partition in a cloth is a defect; it harms its value and benefit, as they have stated. He said: because partition does not harm him; and partnership in measured and weighed items is not considered a defect. And this is the answer to the objection: if the measured/weighed is like one thing, it should be permissible for him to return the remainder when part is reclaimed, as in the single cloth and single slave.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Sale (كتاب البيع) addresses the Option of Defect (خيار العيب) concerning istihqaq (reclamation of part of the sold item). The central ruling: if a sold item of a single genus (e.g., grain, oil, weight-based goods) is partly reclaimed by a third party after possession, the buyer cannot return the remaining part; he must accept it with a reduction in price proportional to the reclaimed portion. This is because such goods derive their value from aggregation—a single grain has no distinct market value. Hence, the entire lot is treated as a single entity in sale. Therefore, partition does not constitute a defect (عيب) in these goods. In contrast, if the sold item is a cloth (ثوب) and part is reclaimed, the buyer may return the remainder because cutting cloth into separate pieces harms its value and utility. The passage further notes a difference of opinion: some (including Abu Ja'far and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi) hold that even if the goods are in two containers, the same rule applies—the buyer cannot return a part. However, the majority view (supported by Imam Muhammad's absolute statement in the book) is that if the goods are in two separate containers, they are treated like two separate items (like two slaves), and the buyer returns only the container with the defective portion. The commentary also mentions an important distinction regarding timing: before possession, the buyer has the option to rescind the entire contract if part is reclaimed, regardless of the type of good (measured, weighed, or otherwise). After possession, the rule applies differently: for goods other than measured/weighed, the buyer can split the deal and return only the defective part; but for measured/weighed goods, splitting is not allowed. This is a clear exposition of the Hanafi position on how to handle partial reclamation in sale transactions.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqāq): Reclamation of part of the sold item by a third party who proves ownership, causing the contract to be voided proportionally.
تبعضي (tab‘īḍ): Partition or splitting of the sold item into parts, which may be harmful (e.g., cloth) or harmless (e.g., grain).
مكيل (makīl): Goods sold by measure (volume), such as grains, oil, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when part of a measured/weighed good (e.g., grain) is reclaimed after the buyer has taken possession, what is the ruling on the remaining part?
A) The buyer must return the entire lot.
B) The buyer may choose to return the remainder or keep it with a price reduction.
C) The buyer must keep the remainder and is not allowed to return it.
D) The buyer may keep the remainder only if the defect is hidden.

Key Takeaway
For measured/weighed goods of a single genus, partial reclamation does not give the buyer the right to return the remainder; the goods are treated as a single unit whose value is in the aggregate.
