Timing of Reclamation: Before vs After Possession
Detailed ruling on buyer's option depending on timing of reclamation and defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that determines the buyer's right to rescind when part of the sold item is reclaimed, distinguishing between reclamation before and after possession and whether it causes a defect.

Core Idea
The buyer's right to return the remainder after partial reclamation depends on timing and defect creation.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on the buyer's options when a third party reclaims part of a purchased item. The key factor is whether the buyer has taken possession (qabd) and whether the remaining part is now defective. Understanding this distinction prevents common mistakes in sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify timing of reclamation
Determine whether the reclamation occurred before or after the buyer took possession (qabd) of the entire sold item.
Possession can be actual or constructive.
2
Apply pre-possession rule
If reclamation occurs before full possession, the buyer has a full option to either accept the remainder with a price reduction or rescind the entire contract, regardless of defect.
3
Apply after-possession rule – check for defect
If reclamation occurs after full possession, examine whether the partial reclamation causes a defect ('ayb) in the remaining part. This depends on whether the item is indivisible (e.g., house, land) or divisible (e.g., separate cloths, grain).
Indivisible items: partition inherently causes defect.
4
Determine buyer's option based on defect
If reclamation causes a defect (indivisible item), the buyer has the option to return or keep the remainder. If no defect (divisible items), the buyer is bound to keep the remainder without option.
5
Handle mixed possession cases
If the buyer has taken possession of part and not another, and reclamation occurs, the ruling follows the pre-possession rule: the buyer has full option because the contract was not fully completed.
This covers scenarios where some items are delivered and some are not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستحقاق لا يمنع تمامَ الصَّفقة ؛ لأنَّ تمامها برضا العاقدين، وهذا بعد القبض أما لو استحق البعض قبل القبض، فللمشتري حق الفسخ في الباقي. قوله : والاستحقاق...الخ ؛ هذا جواب سؤال مقدّر ، تقريره : أن يقال : انتفاء الخيار في رد ما بقي يستلزم تفريق الصفقة قبل التّمام ؛ لأنَّ تمامها بالرضاء، والمستحق لم يكن راضياً، وتوجيه الجواب : أنَّ الاستحقاق لا يمنعُ تمامها ؛ لأنه برضاء العاقد لا المالك ؛ لأن العقد حق العاقد، فتمامُهُ يستدعي تمامَ رضائه، وبالاستحقاق لا ينتفي ذلك. قوله : وهذا بعد القبض ؛ تفصيلُهُ ما في «المنح» نقلاً عن «العمادية»: من أنه إذا استحق بعض المبيع فإنه ينظرُ إن استحق قبل القبض بطل البيع في مقدار المستحق، والمشتري بالخيار في الباقي إن شاءَ رَضِيَ بحصة من الثمن وإن شاء ردَّ سواء كان استحقاق ما استحق يورث العيب في الباقي أو لا يورث ؛ لأنَّ الصفقة بالاستحقاق تفرّقت على المشتري قبل التمام وكذلك إذا كان الاستحقاق بعد قبض البعض دون البعض، واستحق المقبوض أو غير المقبوض ، فالجواب على ما ذكرنا أنه مخيّر لتفريق الصفقة قبل التمام، ولو قبض الكل ثم استحق بعضه، فإنَّ المبيع في مقدار المستحق باطل. ثم ينظرُ إن كان استحقاق ما استحق يورث عيبا في الباقي كما إذا كان المعقود عليه واحداً مما في تبعيضه ضرر كالدَّارِ والأرض والكرم والعبد ونحوها، فالمشتري بالخيار في الباقي إن شاءَ رضي بحصته من الثَّمن، وإن شاء ردّ. وكذلك إذا كان المعقود عليه شيئين، وهما في الحكم كشيء واحد فاستحق أحدهما فله الخيار في الباقي. وإن كان استحقاق ما استحق لا يورث عيباً في الباقي، كما إذا كان المعقود عليه ثوبين أو عبدين فاستحق أحدهما ، أو صُبرة حنطة، أو حَمْلَة وَزْنِي، فاستحق بعضه، فإنّه لا ضرر في تبعيضه فيلزمُ الباقي المشتري بحصته من الثمن وليس له الخيار.
And reclamation does not prevent the completion of the contract, because its completion is by the consent of the two contracting parties, and this is after possession. But if part is reclaimed before possession, the buyer has the right of rescission regarding the remainder. He said: 'And reclamation... etc.' This is an answer to a presupposed question. Its formulation: it could be said that the absence of the option to return what remains necessitates splitting the contract before completion, because its completion is by consent, and the claimant was not consenting. The answer: reclamation does not prevent its completion because it is by the consent of the contracting party, not the owner; for the contract is the right of the contracting party, so its completion necessitates the completion of his consent, and this is not negated by reclamation. He said: 'And this is after possession.' Its detail is in al-Manh, quoting al-Imadiyya: If part of the sold item is reclaimed, it is considered: if reclaimed before possession, the sale is void to the extent of the reclaimed portion, and the buyer has the option regarding the remainder: if he wishes, he accepts the portion of the price, and if he wishes, he returns it, whether or not the reclamation causes a defect in the remainder, because the contract was split for the buyer before completion. Similarly, if reclamation occurs after part possession and part non-possession, and the possessed or unpossessed is reclaimed, the ruling is as mentioned: he has the option to split the contract before completion. And if he takes possession of the whole then part is reclaimed, then the sale is void to the extent of the reclaimed portion. Then it is considered: if the reclamation causes a defect in the remainder, as when the subject matter is a single thing whose partition is harmful, like a house, land, vineyard, slave, and the like, then the buyer has the option regarding the remainder: if he wishes, he accepts his share of the price, and if he wishes, he returns it. Similarly, if the subject matter is two things that are in ruling like one thing, and one is reclaimed, he has the option regarding the remainder. And if the reclamation does not cause a defect in the remainder, as when the subject matter is two cloths or two slaves, and one is reclaimed, or a heap of wheat, or a weight load, and part of it is reclaimed, then there is no harm in its partition, so the remainder is binding on the buyer with his share of the price, and he has no option.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage addresses the critical distinction in Hanafi law between reclamation (istihqāq) occurring before or after the buyer takes possession (qabd). The commentary answers a potential objection: if the buyer cannot return the remainder after reclaim, does that not force a split of the contract without the claimant's consent? The Hanafi response: consent for the contract's completion is that of the contracting parties (buyer and seller), not the claimant. Reclamation does not invalidate their mutual consent. Then the detailed ruling from al-Manh: 1) If reclamation occurs before possession: the sale is void pro rata, and the buyer has a full option to either accept the remainder with a price reduction or rescind the entire contract. This applies regardless of whether the remaining part is defective. 2) If reclamation occurs after the buyer has taken full possession: then the sale is void only to the extent of the reclaimed portion. The buyer's right to return the remainder depends on whether the reclamation causes a defect in that remainder. If the subject matter is a single item where partition is harmful (house, land, slave, etc.), the buyer has the option to return or keep. If the subject matter is two distinct items (e.g., two cloths, two slaves) or a bulk of measured/weighed goods, and partition does not cause harm, then the buyer must keep the remainder and cannot return it. 3) If part possession occurs (some delivered, some not), the ruling follows the pre-possession rule: the buyer has the option to rescind because the contract was split before completion. This is a comprehensive exposition of the Hanafi rules on partial reclamation and its effect on the buyer's rights.

Key Terms
قبض (qabḍ): Physical or constructive possession of the sold item by the buyer. The timing of qabḍ determines legal effects of reclamation.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the contract by the buyer's choice, returning the item and receiving full refund.
عيب (‘ayb): A defect that reduces the value or utility of the item. Whether reclamation causes a 'ayb determines the buyer's option after possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases two separate cloths and takes possession of both. Later, one cloth is reclaimed by its true owner. According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the buyer's right regarding the remaining cloth?
A) The buyer must return the remaining cloth as well.
B) The buyer may keep the remaining cloth with a price reduction.
C) The buyer has the option to either keep or return the remaining cloth.
D) The buyer must keep the remaining cloth and has no option.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's right to rescind after partial reclamation hinges on whether the reclamation occurs before full possession (always an option) or after (only if the remainder becomes defective).

Effect of Third-Party Claim on Sale and Buyer's Option
Ruling on when claim of part of item affects buyer's right to cancel remainder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on partial istihqaq to determine whether the buyer has the option to cancel the remaining part of the sale.

Core Idea
The buyer's option to cancel after a partial claim hinges on claim timing and whether the remainder is harmed by the split.

When a third party claims ownership of part of a sold item, the validity of the sale and the buyer's rights depend on key factors. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position on how such a claim affects the transaction, distinguishing between claims before and after possession, and between divisible and indivisible goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timing of the claim
Determine whether the third-party claim (istihqaq) occurs before the buyer has taken possession (qabd) of the entire item or after full possession.
Possession is physical or constructive control.
2
Apply the rule for claim before full possession
If the claim occurs before possession of all items, the sale is void for the claimed part, and the buyer has the option to cancel the remainder—regardless of defect.
This is because the transaction was split before completion.
3
Apply the rule for claim after full possession
If the claim occurs after full possession, check whether the claimed part causes a defect (ḍarar) in the remainder. If it does (e.g., a house, land, slave), the buyer may cancel the remainder. If it does not (e.g., separate garments, wheat heap), the buyer must keep the remainder and pay a proportional share of the price.
The option exists only when splitting damages the utility of the remainder.

والاستحقاق لا يمنع تمامَ الصَّفقة؛ لأنَّ تمامها برضا العاقدين، وهذا بعد القبض. أما لو استحق البعض قبل القبض، فللمشتري حق الفسخ في الباقي. قوله : والاستحقاق...الخ ؛ هذا جواب سؤال مقدّر ، تقريره : أن يقال : انتفاء الخيار في رد ما بقي يستلزم تفريق الصفقة قبل التّمام ؛ لأنَّ تمامها بالرضاء، والمستحق لم يكن راضياً، وتوجيه الجواب : أنَّ الاستحقاق لا يمنعُ تمامها ؛ لأنه برضاء العاقد لا المالك ؛ لأن العقد حق العاقد، فتمامُهُ يستدعي تمامَ رضائه، وبالاستحقاق لا ينتفي ذلك. «الفصول» قوله : وهذا بعد القبض ؛ تفصيلُهُ ما في «المنح» نقلاً عن العمادية»: من أنه إذا استحق بعض المبيع فإنه ينظرُ إن استحق قبل القبض بطل البيع في مقدار المستحق، والمشتري بالخيار في الباقي إن شاءَ رَضِيَ بحصة من الثمن وإن شاء ردَّ سواء كان استحقاق ما استحق يورث العيب في الباقي أو لا يورث ؛ لأنَّ الصفقة بالاستحقاق تفرّقت على المشتري قبل التمام. وكذلك إذا كان الاستحقاق بعد قبض البعض دون البعض، واستحق المقبوض أو غير المقبوض ، فالجواب على ما ذكرنا أنه مخيّر لتفريق الصفقة قبل التمام، ولو قبض الكل ثم استحق بعضه، فإنَّ المبيع في مقدار المستحق باطل. ثم ينظرُ إن كان استحقاق ما استحق يورث عيبا في الباقي كما إذا كان المعقود عليه واحداً مما في تبعيضه ضرر كالدَّارِ والأرض والكرم والعبد ونحوها، فالمشتري بالخيار في الباقي إن شاءَ رضي بحصته من الثَّمن، وإن شاء ردّ. وكذلك إذا كان المعقود عليه شيئين، وهما في الحكم كشيء واحد فاستحق أحدهما فله الخيار في الباقي. وإن كان استحقاق ما استحق لا يورث عيباً في الباقي، كما إذا كان المعقود عليه ثوبين أو عبدين فاستحق أحدهما ، أو صُبرة حنطة، أو حَمْلَة وَزْنِي، فاستحق بعضه، فإنّه لا ضرر في تبعيضه فيلزمُ الباقي المشتري بحصته من الثمن وليس له الخيار.
The claim of ownership does not prevent the completion of the transaction because its completion is based on the satisfaction of the two contracting parties, and this is after possession. As for if a part is claimed before possession, the buyer has the right of rescission regarding the remainder. His statement: 'And the claim...etc.'; this is an answer to an implied question. Its explanation: It could be said: The absence of the option to return what remains entails splitting the transaction before completion because its completion is by satisfaction, and the claimant was not satisfied. The direction of the answer: That the claim does not prevent its completion because it is by the satisfaction of the contracting party, not the owner, because the contract is the right of the contracting party, so its completion requires the completion of his satisfaction, and the claim does not negate that. 'Al-Fusul'. His statement: 'And this is after possession'; its detail is as in 'Al-Minh' quoting from 'Al-Imadiyyah': That if a part of the sold item is claimed, consider: if it is claimed before possession, the sale is void to the extent of the claimed portion, and the buyer has the option regarding the remainder: if he wishes he is content with a share of the price, and if he wishes he returns, whether the claim of what was claimed causes a defect in the remainder or not, because the transaction by the claim is split for the buyer before completion. Similarly, if the claim is after possession of some but not all, and the claimed portion is either possessed or unpossessed, then the answer is as we mentioned: he has the choice due to the splitting of the transaction before completion. And if he took possession of the whole, then a part is claimed, then the sale is void to the extent of the claimed. Then it is considered: if the claim of what was claimed causes a defect in the remainder, as if the contracted item is one thing in whose division there is harm, such as a house, land, vineyard, slave, and the like, then the buyer has the option regarding the remainder: if he wishes he is content with its share of the price, and if he wishes he returns. Similarly, if the contracted item is two things, and they are in ruling like one thing, and one of them is claimed, then he has the option regarding the remainder. And if the claim of what was claimed does not cause a defect in the remainder, as if the contracted item is two garments or two slaves and one is claimed, or a heap of wheat, or a weight load, and part is claimed, then there is no harm in its division, so the remainder is binding on the buyer with its share of the price and he has no option.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the effect of a third-party claim (istihqaq) on a sale, specifically when part of the sold item is claimed. The passage first establishes the principle that a claim does not prevent the completion of the transaction after possession, because completion is based on the consent of the contracting parties. However, if the claim occurs before possession, the buyer has the option to rescind the entire remaining part. The commentary then elaborates with detailed scenarios: if the claim is before full possession, the buyer is always given the option to cancel the rest regardless of defect, because the transaction was split before completion. If the claim occurs after full possession, the ruling depends on whether the claimed portion causes a defect in the remaining property. If the item is indivisible without harm (e.g., a house, land), the buyer may cancel the rest. If the items are separable without harm (e.g., two separate garments), the buyer must keep the remainder at a proportional price and has no option. This is based on the principle that the sale is valid for the unclaimed part, and the buyer's right of rescission is only for the claimed part, but if splitting causes harm, he is given the option to protect against loss.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A third party's claim of ownership over part or all of the sold item.
الصفقة (al-safqah): The transaction or contract of sale in its entirety.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option to rescind or confirm the sale.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the sold item.
التبعية (al-tab'iyyah): Division or splitting of the transaction into parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Whether the buyer has taken physical possession

Key Takeaway
The buyer's right to cancel the rest of a sale after a partial claim depends critically on when the claim occurs and whether the split harms the value of the remainder.

Actions That Constitute Consent to a Defect in Sale
Explanation that treating a defect and riding for necessity do not imply consent, but riding for one's need does.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actions that constitute consent (rada) to a defect and those that do not, according to the Hanafi school, and apply the criteria of knowledge, necessity, and alternative.

Core Idea
Treating a known defect or riding for your own need is consent; necessary riding to return or preserve is not.

In this lesson, we examine the exact legal boundaries of when a buyer's use of a defective item forfeits the right to return it. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between actions that imply acceptance and those performed out of necessity. The key is the buyer's knowledge and intent. This ruling safeguards fairness: a buyer who uses the item for personal benefit after discovering the defect is deemed to have accepted it, but one who acts out of necessity retains the right to return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State clearly that treating a defect after knowledge and riding for one's own need is consent, while riding for return or other necessities is not.
2
Distinguish between need and necessity
Understand that 'need' (hajah) refers to personal benefit, while 'necessity' (like returning, watering, buying fodder without alternative) preserves the right to reject.
The presence of an alternative (badd) turns necessary riding into consent.
3
Understand the condition of knowledge
Treatment only constitutes consent if the buyer is aware of the defect. Without knowledge, treatment does not imply acceptance.
This prevents waiver of rights by mistake.
4
Apply to hypothetical scenarios
Practice with cases: riding for a leisure trip vs. riding to the vet; treating a visible vs. hidden defect.
Use the criteria of knowledge, benefit, and alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومداواة المعيب وركوبُهُ في حاجتِهِ رضاً ، ولو ركب لرده أو لسقيهِ أو لشراء عَلَفِه ولا بد له منه فلا. قوله : قبل التمام؛ لأنَّ تمام الصفقة قد يحتاجُ إلى رضاء العاقدين، وقبض المبيع، وانتفاء أحدهما يوجب عدم تمامها كذا في «البناية». قوله : فالتبعيض يضرّه فله ... الخ؛ قيل : إنه حدث بالاستحقاق عيب جديد في المشترى ، ومثله يمنع الرد بالعيب. وأجيب: بأنه ليس بحادث في يد المشتري ، بل كان في يد البائع وقت البيع حيث ظهر الاستحقاق فلا يكون مانعاً. قوله : ومداواة المعيب؛ بأن كان المبيعُ المعيب عبداً مثلا، فداواه، ولو قال : والمداواة لكان أخصر وأشمل ما إذا كان العيب دواءً فداوى به نفسه أو غيره بعد اطلاعه على عيب فيه. وركونُهُ في حاجته؛ احتراز عن ركويه لسقيه رضاءً؛ لأنه دليل استبقائه وإمساكه قال في «البحر» نقلاً عن «الوَلْوَالجيّة»: «المداواة إنما تكون رضاً بعيب داواه، أما إذا داوى المبيع من عيب قد بَرِءَ منه البائع، وبه عيب آخر فإنه لا يمتنع ردّه». انتهى) قوله : ولو ركب...الخ؛ ولا فرق في الركوب بين أن يكون له منه بداً أو لا؛ لأنَّ في الركوب ضبط الدابة، وهو أحفظ لها من حدوث عيب آخر. كذا في «العناية». قوله : ولا بد له منه؛ وهذا قيد لازم وذلك إما لصعوبتها أو لعجزه، أو لكون العلف في عدل واحد، أما إذا كان له بد منه فهو رضاً. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : فلا؛ أي لا يكون بهذه الأشياء رضاً بالعيب، أما الركوب للردّ فلانه
Treating the defective item and riding it for one's need is consent. But if he rode it to return it or to water it or to buy its fodder, and he had no alternative to it, then it is not [consent]. His statement: 'before completion' because the completion of the transaction may need the satisfaction of the two contracting parties and possession of the sold item, and the absence of either requires its non-completion. This is from 'Al-Binayah'. His statement: 'so division harms him, so he has...etc.'; it was said: A new defect occurred by the claim in the purchased item, and the like prevents return due to defect. And it was answered: That it did not occur in the hand of the buyer, but was in the hand of the seller at the time of the sale where the claim appeared, so it is not a preventer. His statement: 'And treating the defective item' such as if the defective sold item was a slave, and he treated him. And if he said: 'and treatment' it would be more concise and comprehensive for the case if the defect was a medicine and he treated himself or others after his knowledge of a defect in it. 'And riding it for his need' is a precaution against riding it for watering it as consent, because it is evidence of retaining it and holding it. He said in 'Al-Bahr' quoting from 'Al-Walwajiyyah': 'Treatment is only consent for a defect he treated. However, if he treated the sold item from a defect from which the seller had recovered, and it has another defect, then its return is not prevented.' End. His statement: 'And if he rode...etc.'; there is no difference in riding between whether he had an alternative to it or not, because in riding there is controlling the animal, and it is more protective for it from the occurrence of another defect. This is from 'Al-Inayah'. His statement: 'And he has no alternative to it' is a necessary condition, either due to its difficulty or his inability, or because the fodder is in one package. But if he had an alternative to it, then it is consent. This is from 'Al-Hidayah'. His statement: 'so it is not' means that by these things it is not consent to the defect, but the riding for return...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage explains what actions constitute rada' (consent) that prevents the buyer from returning a defective item under khiyar al-'ayb. The key ruling: treating the defect (madawaa) and riding the animal for one's own need are considered consent, barring return. However, if the buyer treats the defect but without knowledge of the defect, or rides the animal out of necessity (e.g., to return it, to water it, to buy fodder), those actions do not constitute consent. The commentary clarifies that treatment is consent only if done after knowledge of the specific defect. Riding for necessity is not consent because it is required for preservation. The passage also discusses the condition that if the buyer had an alternative to riding for necessity (e.g., another means), then riding becomes consent. This helps preserve fairness: actions indicating acceptance preclude return, but necessary actions taken under duress do not.

Key Terms
المداواة (al-mudawaah): Treating a defect in the sold item, such as applying medicine to a sick slave.
الرضا (al-rida): Consent or acceptance that waives the right to return a defective item.
العيب (al-'ayb): A defect in the sold item that gives the buyer the option of return.
الركوب (al-rukub): Riding an animal that is sold, which may indicate consent or necessity depending on context.
البد (al-badd): Alternative or option; if the buyer has an alternative to an action, that action may indicate consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following actions by the buyer is considered consent (rada) to a defect, thereby preventing return?
A) Riding the defective animal to return it to the seller
B) Treating the defect with medicine without knowledge of the defect
C) Riding the defective animal for the buyer's own errand
D) Watering the animal because it was thirsty

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, consent to a defect is established by the buyer's voluntary use for personal benefit after knowledge, but not by necessary actions taken under duress.

Actions Constituting Acceptance of Defect (رضا بالعيب)
Clarifies which actions indicate acceptance of a defect in a sale (khiyar al-‘ayb).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish actions that constitute implicit acceptance of a defect from those that do not, and state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
An action indicating intent to keep the defect forfeits return; necessity or return-directed actions do not.

This passage from al-Wiqaya clarifies a common practical issue in sales (buyu’): which actions by the buyer, after discovering a defect, are considered implicit acceptance (rida) and thus eliminate the right to return the item? The author distinguishes between using the item for personal need (which indicates acceptance) and using it out of necessity or specifically to return it (which does not). The commentary from al-`Inaya and al-Hidaya provides the underlying rationale based on the buyer’s intention and the purpose of the action. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of khiyar al-‘ayb (option of defect) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions discussed
The passage mentions four actions: (1) mudawat – treating the defect, (2) rukub for personal need, (3) rukub for the purpose of return, and (4) rukub for watering/feeding when necessary.
Mudawat and riding for need are categorized as acceptance; the other two are not.
2
Determine which actions constitute acceptance
According to the matn, mudawat and riding for need are acceptance. Riding for return or for watering/feeding without alternative does not constitute acceptance.
3
Analyze the rationale
The commentary explains that riding for return is a cause of return, so it does not indicate acceptance. Riding for necessity (watering/feeding when no alternative exists) is indispensable and thus does not imply a choice to keep the item.
The underlying principle: actions are judged by their purpose and necessity.
4
Apply the rule to other similar actions
Any action that evidences an intent to keep (e.g., using the item for personal benefit) is acceptance. Any action necessitated by the item’s survival or aimed at returning it is not acceptance.
Necessity is defined as having no practical alternative (e.g., no other way to water the animal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومداواة المعيب وركوبُهُ في حاجتِهِ رضاً ، ولو ركب لرده أو لسقيهِ أو لشراء عَلَفِه ولا بد له منه فلا. قوله : قبل التمام ؛ لأنَّ تمام الصفقة قد يحتاجُ إلى رضاء العاقدين، وقبض المبيع، وانتفاء أحدهما يوجب عدم تمامها كذا في «البناية». قوله : فالتبعيض يضرّه فله ... الخ ؛ قيل : إنه حدث بالاستحقاق عيب جديد في المشترى ، ومثله يمنع الرد بالعيب. وأجيب: بأنه ليس بحادث في يد المشتري ، بل كان في يد البائع وقت البيع حيث ظهر الاستحقاق فلا يكون مانعاً. قوله : ومداواة المعيب ؛ بأن كان المبيعُ المعيب عبداً مثلا، فداواه، ولو قال : والمداواة لكان أخصر وأشمل ما إذا كان العيب دواءً فداوى به نفسه أو غيره بعد اطلاعه على عيب فيه. وركونُهُ في حاجته ؛ احتراز عن ركويه لسقيه رضاءً ؛ لأنه دليل استبقائه وإمساكه قال في «البحر» نقلاً عن «الوَلْوَالجيّة»: «المداواة إنما تكون رضاً بعيب داواه، أما إذا داوى المبيع من عيب قد بَرِءَ منه البائع، وبه عيب آخر فإنه لا يمتنع ردّه». انتهى) قوله : ولو ركب...الخ ؛ ولا فرق في الركوب بين أن يكون له منه بداً أو لا ؛ لأنَّ في الركوب ضبط الدابة، وهو أحفظ لها من حدوث عيب آخر. كذا في «العناية». قوله : ولا بد له منه ؛ وهذا قيد ،لازم وذلك إما لصعوبتها أو لعجزه، أو لكون العلف في عدل واحد، أما إذا كان له بد منه فهو رضاً. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : فلا ؛ أي لا يكون بهذه الأشياء رضاً بالعيب، أما الركوب للردّ فلانه سبب الرد، فيكون مفضياً إليه ومقرّراً ، وأما الركوب لسقيه أو شراء علفه فللاحتياج إليه.
And treating the defective [item] and riding it for his need is acceptance. And if he rode it for returning it or for watering it or for buying fodder for it, and he has no alternative to it, then it is not [acceptance]. He said: 'before completion' because the completion of the transaction may require the consent of the two contracting parties and the delivery of the sold item, and the absence of either necessitates its incompleteness, as in 'al-Binaya'. He said: 'so division harms him, so he has the option...' It is said that a new defect occurred in the purchased item due to the claim, and the like prevents return due to defect. And it was answered: that it did not occur in the hand of the buyer, but was in the hand of the seller at the time of sale when the claim appeared, so it is not a preventer. He said: 'and treating the defective' such as if the defective sold item was a slave, and he treated him. And if he said 'and the treatment' it would be more concise and more inclusive of the case where the defect is a medicine and he treated himself or others with it after being aware of a defect in it. And 'riding it in his need' is a precaution against his riding for watering as acceptance, because it is evidence of retaining and keeping it. He said in 'al-Bahr' quoting 'al-Walwajiyya': 'Treatment is only acceptance of the defect for which he treated it. However, if he treated the sold item for a defect from which the seller had cured it, and it has another defect, then he is not prevented from returning it.' End. He said: 'and if he rode...' and there is no difference in riding between whether he has an alternative to it or not, because in riding there is control of the animal, and it is more protective against the occurrence of another defect, as in 'al-Inaya'. He said: 'and he has no alternative to it' and this is a necessary condition, either due to its difficulty or his inability, or because the fodder is in a single bag; but if he has an alternative, it is acceptance, as in 'al-Hidaya'. He said: 'then no' i.e., these actions do not constitute acceptance of the defect. As for riding for return, it is because it is a cause of return, so it leads to it and confirms it. As for riding for watering or buying fodder, it is due to necessity.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn on what constitutes implicit acceptance (رضا) of a defect in a purchased item. The author, al-Wiqaya, states that treating the defect (e.g., medicating a sick slave) and riding an animal for one's own need are actions that indicate acceptance, thus forfeiting the right to return the item due to that defect. However, if the buyer rides the animal specifically for the purpose of returning it, or for watering it, or for buying fodder when he has no alternative, then these actions do not constitute acceptance. The commentary from al-`Inaya and others explains the rationale: riding for return is directly connected to exercising the right of return, not to keeping the item; riding for necessity (watering, feeding) is indispensable for the animal's survival and thus does not imply a choice to keep it. The key distinction is the buyer's intention and necessity.

Key Terms
رضا (riḍā): Implicit acceptance of the defect by the buyer's actions, forfeiting the right to return.
مداواة (mudāwāt): Treatment of the defect, such as medicating a sick slave.
ركوب (rukūb): Riding the animal; considered acceptance if for personal need, but not if for return or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, which of the following actions does NOT constitute acceptance (riḍā) of a defect in a purchased animal?
A) Riding it for a personal trip.
B) Treating its illness with medicine.
C) Riding it to return it to the seller.
D) Feeding it hay.

Key Takeaway
The decisive factor in determining acceptance is the buyer's intention and whether the action is for personal benefit or out of necessity for the item's preservation or return.

Dispute on Slave with Pre-Existing Capital Crime: Full Refund or Partial Compensation?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on remedy for slave with pre-existing capital crime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on the remedy for a slave who is executed due to a pre-existing capital crime, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa treats pre-existing capital crime as istihqaq (full refund); the Sahibayn treat it as a defect (partial compensation).

In this lesson, we examine a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the purchase of a slave who is later found to have committed a capital crime before the sale, leading to his amputation or execution. The central question is: should the buyer recover the full price or only the difference in value? The matn presents Abu Hanifa's view, while the Sahibayn disagree. We will explore the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the dispute
A slave is bought, then his hand is cut or he is executed due to a pre-existing crime. The issue: what compensation can the buyer claim?
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
He allows return of the slave and full price in both cases, analogizing to istihqaq (a claim that voids the sale).
For killing, no physical return, but full price is taken.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
They value the slave in two states and allow only the difference, treating it as a hidden defect (ʻayb).
4
Analyze the reasoning
Understand why istihqaq leads to full refund (the seller never had full ownership) versus defect leads to partial compensation (the sale is valid but defective).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: full price vs. price difference. Also note the additional nuance: the buyer may keep the slave and claim half the price (analogous to istihqaq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قطع يده بعد قبضه، أو قتل بسبب كان عند بائعه ردَّه ، وأَخَذَ ثَمَنَه الرَّدُّ في صورة القطع ، أما في القتل فلا رَدّ ، بل أخذ الثَّمَنَ عند أبي حنيفة . لأنَّ هذا بمنزلة الاستحقاق عنده. قوله : ولو قطع ... الخ ؛ صورتُه : اشترى عبداً وكان قد سرق عند البائع ، والمشتري لم يعلم به لا وقت الشراء ولا وقت القبض ، وبعد القبض قطعت يده عند المشتري ، فله أن يرده أو يأخذ الثمن كله ، وهذا عند الإمام . وعندهما يقوم ذلك العبد حال كونه سارقاً وغير سارق، فيرجعُ المشتري لفضل ما بينهما، هذا في القطع. وأما في القتل فصورته : إن اشترى عبداً حال كونه مباحَ الدَّم لقود أو ردة أو قطع طريق، والمشتري لا يعلم ذلك الحال، فقتل عنده ، يرجع المشتري على البائع بكل الثمن عند الإمام الله. وقالا : يقوم ذلك العبد حال كونه مباح الدم ومعصوم الدم فيرجع بفضل ما بينهما. قوله : ردَّه وأخذ ثمنه ؛ ظاهر كلام المصنف له أنه ليس بمخير بين إمساكه والرجوع بنصف الثَّمن، وليس كذلك، بل مخير ، فله إمساكه وأخذ نصف الثّمن ؛ لأنه بمنزلة الاستحقاقِ لا العيب، حتى لو مات بعد القطع حتف أنفه رجع بنصف الثّمن كالاستحقاق. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». قوله : الرد ؛ لما كان قوله : ردّه وأخذ ثمنه ؛ جزاءً لقوله : لو قطع بعد قبضه أو قتل بسبب كان عند بائعه والجزاء يترتب على الشرط ، فالمتبادرُ أنَّ هذا الحكم يترتَّبُ على هاتين الصورتين، والأمر ليس كذلك ، فالشارح الله احتاج إلى التفصيل والبيان. قوله : لأن هذا ؛ أي استحقاق القطع أو كونه مباح الدم بمنزلة الاستحقاق عنده ، فإذا ظهر مستحق يرجعُ بكل الثمن، فكذلك هاهنا.
And if his hand is severed after taking possession, or he is killed due to a cause that existed with his seller, he may return him and take his price. The return is in the case of cutting, but as for killing, there is no return, rather he takes the price according to Abu Hanifa, because this is like a claim [istihqaq] in his view. He said: 'and if he cuts...' etc.; its scenario: he bought a slave who had stolen while with the seller, and the buyer did not know of it at the time of purchase or possession, and after possession his hand was cut off while with the buyer; so he may return him or take the full price, and this is according to the Imam. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], that slave is valued as a thief and as not a thief, and the buyer recovers the difference between the two. This is regarding cutting. As for killing, its scenario: he bought a slave while he was legally subject to execution for retaliation, apostasy, or highway robbery, and the buyer did not know that condition; if he is killed, the buyer recovers the full price from the seller according to the Imam. They said: that slave is valued as legally permissible to kill and as inviolable, and he recovers the difference between the two. He said: 'return him and take his price'; the apparent wording of the author implies that he is not given a choice between keeping him and claiming half the price, but it is not so; rather he has a choice, so he may keep him and take half the price, because it is like a claim, not a defect; even if he dies a natural death after the cutting, he recovers half the price like a claim. He said: 'because this' i.e., the entitlement to cutting or being legally subject to execution is like a claim in his view; so if a claimant appears, he recovers the full price; similarly here.

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a slave who, after purchase, is discovered to have a pre-existing cause for amputation or execution (e.g., theft or capital crime). The matn states that if the slave's hand is cut off or he is killed due to a cause that existed with the seller, the buyer may return him and take the full price. The commentary clarifies that according to Abu Hanifa, the buyer can return the slave and recover the full price in both cases, treat this as akin to a claim (istihqaq) where the subject matter was never validly owned. However, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the slave is valued in two states: as defective (thief or liable to execution) and as sound; the buyer recovers only the difference. The commentary further explains that the buyer is not forced to return; he may keep the slave and recover half the price, analogizing it to istihqaq. The core difference is whether the defect is treated as a hidden defect giving right to partial compensation or as a complete failure of the sale contract.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): A claim or entitlement that results in the sale being void ab initio, entitling the buyer to full refund.
مباح الدم (mubāḥ al-dam): Legally subject to execution for crimes such as apostasy or highway robbery.
عيب (ʻayb): A hidden defect in the purchased item that existed at the time of sale, entitling the buyer to return the item and claim the difference in value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
An intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa treats pre-existing capital crime as istihqaq (full refund), while the Sahibayn treat it as a defect (partial compensation); the practical consequence is the amount of remedy.

Abu Hanifa vs. Sahabayn: Pre-Existing Cause as Istihqaq or 'Ayb
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar al-‘ayb for pre-existing causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the return of a slave for a pre-existing cause and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahabayn.

Core Idea
A pre-existing cause leading to loss is treated as a title defect (istihqaq) by Abu Hanifa, entitling full refund, not merely depreciation.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced case in khiyar al-‘ayb (option of defect). When a purchased slave suffers harm due to a cause that existed at the seller's—such as a theft liability leading to amputation or a blood liability leading to execution—the jurists differ on whether the buyer's recourse is full refund or only compensation for depreciation. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals the Hanafi method of analogical reasoning (qiyas) and the limits of istihsān.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text discusses two cases: the slave's hand is cut after possession due to a theft liability, or the slave is killed due to a blood liability that existed at the seller's. In both, the cause is pre-existing.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa rules that the buyer may return the slave and take full price, or keep the slave and take half price. The cut slave can be returned; the dead slave cannot be returned, so the buyer takes full price.
The commentator clarifies the buyer is not forced to return; he has a choice.
3
Explain the legal basis (dalil)
Abu Hanifa treats the situation like a title defect (istihqaq) because the cause existed at the seller's and directly led to the destruction. By analogy, if a third party claims rightful ownership, the buyer recovers full price; similarly here.
The term 'bi-manzilati al-istihqaq' is key: 'having the same legal status as a title defect.'
4
Contrast with the Sahabayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that it is a defect ('ayb), not a title defect. The buyer recovers only the reduction in value (the difference between the slave's sound value and defective value). They analogize to a pregnant slave-girl who dies during childbirth.
Their analogy: the pre-existing condition (pregnancy) does not cause death, so only depreciation is recovered.
5
Analyze the key distinction
Abu Hanifa responds: the cause here (theft liability) leads inevitably to cutting or execution, whereas pregnancy does not cause death. Thus the analogy fails, and the ruling follows istihqaq rules.
This illustrates Hanafi preference for direct causation over superficial similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قطع يده بعد قبضه، أو قتل بسبب كان عند بائعه ردَّه ، وأَخَذَ ثَمَنَه) الرَّدُّ في صورة القطع ، أما في القتل فلا رَدّ ، بل أخذ الثَّمَنَ عند أبي حنيفة . لأنَّ هذا بمنزلة الاستحقاق عنده ، فيكون مفضياً إليه ومقرّراً . و قوله : ردَّه وأخذ ثمنه ؛ ظاهر كلام المصنف له أنه ليس بمخير بين إمساكه والرجوع بنصف الثَّمن، وليس كذلك، بل مخير ، فله إمساكه وأخذ نصف الثّمن ؛ لأنه بمنزلة الاستحقاقِ لا العيب، حتى لو مات بعد القطع حتف أنفه رجع بنصف الثّمن كالاستحقاق. و قوله : لأن هذا ؛ أي استحقاق القطع أو كونه مباح الدم بمنزلة الاستحقاق عنده ، فإذا ظهر مستحق يرجعُ بكل الثمن، فكذلك هاهنا. فأما عندَهُما فيرجعُ بالنقصان ؛ لأنَّ هذا بمنزلة العيب، فيقوم بدونِ هذا العيب، ثمَّ بهذا العيب، فيضمن البائع تفاوت ما بينهما كما إذا اشترى جارية حاملاً، فماتت في يده بالولادة، فإنَّه يرجع بفضل ما بين قيمتها حاملاً، وغير حامل، ولأبي حنيفة له إن سبب الهلاك كان في يد البائع ، فإذا هلك في يد المشتري يكونُ مضافا إلى ذلك السبب بخلاف الحمل، فإنَّه ليس سبباً للهلاك.
If he cuts his hand after taking possession, or he is killed due to a cause that existed at the seller's, he returns him and takes his price. The return in the case of cutting; as for killing, there is no return, rather he takes the price according to Abu Hanifa. Because this is like a case of title defect (istihqaq) in his view, so it leads to and confirms it. And his statement: 'return him and take his price'; the apparent wording of the author is that he is not given a choice between keeping him and taking half the price, but it is not so; rather he has a choice, so he may keep him and take half the price, because it is like a title defect, not a defect, such that if he dies a natural death after the cutting, he takes back half the price like a title defect. And his statement: 'because this' means that the entitlement to cutting or being of permissible blood is like a title defect in his view, so if a rightful claimant appears, he takes back the full price, and likewise here. As for according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he takes back the deficiency, because this is like a defect, so it is valued without this defect, then with this defect, and the seller guarantees the difference between them, as if he bought a pregnant slave-girl and she died in his hand during childbirth, then he takes back the difference between her value pregnant and non-pregnant. And for Abu Hanifa, the cause of destruction was at the seller's, so if it is destroyed in the buyer's hand, it is attributed to that cause, unlike pregnancy, for it is not a cause of destruction.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter on option of defect (khiyar al-‘ayb) in the book of sales. The author discusses two scenarios: first, where a purchased slave has a hand cut off after the buyer takes possession, due to a previous theft at the seller's; second, where the slave is killed after possession due to a reason (e.g., retaliation for murder, apostasy, or highway robbery) that existed at the seller's. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, in the first scenario the buyer may return the slave and recover the full price, because the cutting is a direct consequence of a cause existing at the seller's, making it akin to a title defect (istihqaq) where the buyer has full recourse. In the second scenario (killing), the buyer cannot return the slave (since it is dead) but still takes the full price, again because the cause existed at the seller's. The commentator clarifies that the buyer is not forced to return; he may keep the slave and take half the price, because it is like a title defect, not an ordinary defect. If the slave dies a natural death after the cutting, the buyer still takes half the price, analogously to a title defect case. The Sahabayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they treat the situation as a defect (not a title defect), so the buyer recovers only the difference in value between the slave in the defective state (as a thief or one whose blood is permissible) and the sound state. They analogize to the case of a pregnant slave-girl who dies during childbirth; there the buyer recovers the difference in value between pregnant and non-pregnant because pregnancy is not a cause of death but a separate condition. Abu Hanifa responds that here the cause (theft or liability) directly leads to the destruction, whereas pregnancy does not cause death but often ends safely, so the analogy does not apply.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): A title defect where a third party has a rightful claim to the sold item, entitling the buyer to full refund.
بمنزلة (bi-manzilati): Having the same legal status as; used to analogize one ruling to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a purchased slave's hand is cut after possession due to a theft at the seller's, what can the buyer do?
A) Only return the slave and get full price.
B) Only keep the slave and get no compensation.
C) Either return the slave for full price or keep him and take half price.
D) Recover only the difference in value.

Key Takeaway
When a pre-existing cause leads to destruction, Abu Hanifa treats it as a title defect (istihqaq), entitling the buyer to full refund or half if retained, unlike the Sahabayn who treat it as a defect with only compensation for depreciation.

Validity of Sale with Blanket Immunity from Defects
Ruling statement with cross-madhhab and intra-Hanafi variation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blanket immunity in sales and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position and the intra-Hanafi variant.

Core Idea
A blanket immunity from all defects is valid in Hanafi sale; the buyer accepts the item with all faults.

This lesson addresses a common commercial practice: a seller declares immunity from all defects without listing them. According to the Hanafi school, this sale is valid, and the buyer has no recourse for hidden defects. However, Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, and even within the Hanafi school there is a variant opinion. Let us examine the ruling and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale where the seller stipulates blanket immunity from all defects. The Hanafi ruling is that the sale is valid (sahih).
The matn explicitly says 'sahih'.
2
Note the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such a sale is invalid because the immunity must specify the defects.
The Shafi'i view is presented as a point of ikhtilaf.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi narration
There is a narration (riwaya) that the sale is valid but the condition is void, leaving the seller liable.
This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the main Hanafi view, the buyer cannot return for hidden defects; under Shafi'i, the buyer can return despite the clause.
The mufta bihi is the main Hanafi view.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of a sale where the seller stipulates that he is immune from liability for any defects in the sold item, without specifically listing the defects. According to the Hanafi school, such a sale is valid (sahih) even if the seller does not enumerate the defects. The buyer accepts the item as is, with all defects. However, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such a blanket immunity is invalid; he requires that the seller specify the defects from which he is immune. The commentator notes that according to one narration (probably from Imam Muhammad or Abu Yusuf), the sale itself is valid but the condition of immunity is null, meaning the seller remains liable for defects. This is a nuanced position within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
براءة (bara'ah): Declaration of immunity from liability for defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Voidable (fasid).
D) Suspended (mawquf).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a seller can validly sell 'as-is' with blanket immunity from all defects.

Selling with Immunity from All Defects: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on validity of blanket immunity in sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a sale with blanket immunity and explain the usul basis for the difference with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Ibra' is isqat, not tamlik: dropping an unknown right is valid and does not require specification.

This passage addresses a key point of disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: can a seller validly stipulate immunity from all defects without enumerating them? The Hanafi school says yes, based on their usul distinction between isqat (dropping) and tamlik (ownership). Al-Shafi'i considers it invalid. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The sale with a condition of immunity from all defects is valid (sahih), even if defects are not enumerated.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i holds such a condition invalid because ibra' from unknown defects is like selling an unknown, which is invalid.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i dalil
Ibra' is a form of tamlik (ownership transfer); owning an unknown is invalid, like selling an unknown.
This is based on their usul that ibra' is tamlik.
4
Follow the Hanafi response
Ibra' is isqat (dropping), not tamlik. Isqat of an unknown is valid because it does not lead to dispute. Analogy: divorcing unknown wives or freeing unknown slaves.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi, buyer cannot return item for any defect. Under Shafi'i, condition is void so buyer retains option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb).

Book of Sale: Option of Defect. And if he sold and declared himself free from every defect, it is valid even if he did not enumerate them. So he values (the item) without this defect, then with this defect, and the seller guarantees the difference between them, as when someone buys a pregnant slave girl, then she dies in his hand due to childbirth, for he has recourse for the difference between her value as pregnant and non-pregnant. And according to Abu Hanifa, the cause of destruction was in the seller's hand, so if it is destroyed in the buyer's hand, it is attributed to that cause, unlike pregnancy, for it is not a cause of destruction. And if he sold and declared himself free from every defect, it is valid even if he did not enumerate them: and according to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid.
[1] His saying: 'because this is like...'; i.e., because this is like a defect that was in the seller's hand, and the buyer became aware of it, and returning it became impossible, so when such a defect appears, he has recourse for the decrease due to the defect; such that a slave who has become subject to amputation (for theft) is appraised, and a slave who has not become subject to amputation, then he has recourse for the amount of the decrease from the price.
[2] His saying: 'it is attributed to that cause'; because the cause existing with the seller necessitated the deserving of the act (cutting), and the deserving necessitated its existence, and its existence necessitated the loss (of the hand), so it becomes attributed to him through these intermediaries.
[3] His saying: 'it is not a cause of destruction'; because the cause that existed with the seller necessitates the separation of the child, not the death of the mother; rather the usual outcome in childbirth is safety; the cause of destruction is illness, and it occurred with the buyer, not the seller.
[4] His saying: 'it is not valid'; immunity from every defect (is invalid) unless he specifies that it is concerning such-and-such, so the sale with a condition of immunity from every defect is void, and in one narration, the sale is valid and the condition is void. This is stated in al-`Inayah.

(2) Al-`Inayah (6:39).
Based on his (al-Shafi'i's) principle that immunity from unknown rights is not valid according to him. And according to us, it is valid; dropping an unknown does not harm him because it does not lead to dispute. Then this immunity covers the existing defect, and also the defect arising before delivery according to Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad, it does not cover the defect arising.
[1) His saying: 'it is not valid according to him'; because in absolution (ibra') there is a meaning of ownership, such that if the creditor absolves the debtor of his debt, then the debtor returns the absolution, the absolution is not valid. And ownership of an unknown is not valid, like its sale. And Ahmad agreed with him.
[2) His saying: 'and according to us, it is valid...'; its explanation: that absolution according to us is a dropping (isqat), not ownership; because ownership of a physical object is not valid with this wording, and absolution is valid by his saying 'I have dropped your debt' without acceptance, just like if he divorces his wives or frees his slaves without knowing how many they are or their identities. And ignorance does not nullify dropping because it does not lead to dispute. And further detail is in al-Fath.
[3) His saying: 'this immunity'; he only said 'this immunity' to distinguish from the immunity that the seller stipulates in his saying 'on condition that he is free from every defect in it', for then he is not immune from the arising defect by consensus, because when he said 'in it', he confined it to what exists. As in al-Binayah.
[4] His saying: 'according to Abu Yusuf...'; and he mentioned it with the Imam in al-Mabsut, and in al-Khaniyyah: it is the apparent view of both of them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) because the intended meaning is the binding of the contract by dropping the description of safety, and that is achieved by immunity from the existing and the arising.
[0] His saying: 'does not cover the defect arising'; because the objective is immunity from the existing defect, not generally, so the non-existent does not enter. And the detail is in al-Bahr and other detailed works.

(3) Al-Mabsut (13:93).
(4) Al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah (2:216).
(5) Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6:72).
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the ruling on a sale where the seller stipulates immunity from all defects. The base text states that such a sale is valid even if the seller does not enumerate the defects. The commentary then explores a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding a specific case: if a pregnant slave girl dies after being sold, the buyer's recourse. Abu Hanifa holds that the cause of destruction (the theft that necessitated amputation) was present with the seller, so the loss is attributed to him, unlike pregnancy which is not a cause of death. The companions analogize the situation to a defect, allowing the buyer to claim the decrease in value. Then the passage addresses the Shafi'i position that such a stipulation is invalid. The super-commentary (hashiyah) clarifies the reasoning: al-Shafi'i considers absolution (ibra') as a form of ownership transfer (tamlik), so dropping unknown rights is like selling an unknown, which is invalid. The Hanafis, however, consider absolution as a dropping (isqat), which is not invalidated by ignorance, as it does not lead to dispute. Finally, an intra-Hanafi difference is noted: Abu Yusuf (and apparently Abu Hanifa) hold that the immunity covers both existing defects and defects arising before delivery (qabl al-qabd), while Muhammad restricts it to existing defects only. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the sale is valid, and the scope of immunity depends on the wording of the condition.

Key Terms
البراءة (al-bara'ah): Immunity or disclaimer from defects. In this context, it refers to the seller's stipulation that he is free from liability for any defects in the sold item.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Absolution or release from a claim. In this text, it is used in the context of the seller releasing the buyer from any claim regarding defects.
إسقاط (isqat): Dropping or relinquishing a right. According to Hanafis, isqat is valid even if the right is unknown, as it does not lead to dispute.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty. Here, it refers to the buyer not knowing the exact defects from which the seller is immune.
العيب الحادث (al-'ayb al-hadith): A defect that arises after the sale contract but before delivery (qabl al-qabd). The passage discusses whether immunity covers this type of defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi basis for validating a sale with immunity from all unknown defects?
A) Immunity is a form of ownership transfer (tamlik) that is valid despite ignorance.
B) Immunity is a form of dropping (isqat), and ignorance does not invalidate isqat.
C) The buyer is deemed to have accepted all defects by agreeing to the sale.
D) The seller's statement is considered a guarantee against defects.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a blanket immunity clause in a sale hinges on whether ibra' is classified as isqat (Hanafi) or tamlik (Shafi'i); Hanafis uphold the clause because isqat of unknown rights is permissible.

Scope of Immunity: Defects Arising Before Delivery (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on coverage of defects arising before delivery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding whether blanket immunity covers defects arising before delivery, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The mufta bihi covers defects arising before delivery, as dropping the description of safety binds the contract.

Once we accept that a blanket immunity clause is valid, the next question is: what defects does it cover? Does it include defects that appear after the contract but before the buyer takes possession? The two leading Hanafi imams, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differed. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and identifies the operative ruling (mufta bihi).

Guided Path
1
Recall the validity of blanket immunity
Sale with immunity from all defects is valid (from previous lesson).
2
State the disputed issue
Does the immunity cover defects that arise after the contract but before delivery (qabl al-qabd)?
3
Position of Abu Yusuf (and Abu Hanifa)
Yes, it covers. Reason: the purpose is to bind the contract by dropping the description of safety, which is achieved by covering both existing and arising defects.
4
Position of Muhammad
No, it covers only existing defects. Reason: the wording 'from every defect' refers to defects that exist at the time of the contract; the immunity is a waiver of known rights, not future possibilities.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view (zahir madhhab) is that of Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa, so immunity includes defects arising before delivery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(١) في «المنهاج» (٢) (٥٣): ولو باع بشرط براءته من العيوب، فالأظهر أنه يبرأ عن عيب باطن بالحيوان لم يعلمه دون غيره وينظر: «الأم» (٧): (١٠٥)، ومغني المحتاج» (٢: ٥٣)، وغيرها.
(٢) «العناية (٦): ٣٩)
بناء على أصله أن البراءة عن الحقوق المجهولة لا يصح عنده، وعندنا يصح اسقاط المجهول لا يضرّه؛ لأنه لا يفضي إلى المنازعة، ثم هذه البراءة تشمل العيب الموجود، وأيضاً العيب الحادث قبل القبض عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد لا تشمل العيب الحادث.
[١) قوله: لا يصح عنده؛ لأنَّ في الإبراء معنى التمليك، حتى أن رب الدين إذا أبرأ المديون عن دينه فردّ المديون إبراءه لم يصح الإبراء، وتمليك المجهول لا يصح كبيعه، وبه قال أحمد له.
[٢) قوله: وعندنا يصح...الخ؛ تقريره: أنَّ الإبراء عندنا إسقاط لا تمليك؛ لأنه لا يصح تمليك العين بهذا اللفظ، ويصح الإبراء بقوله: أسقطت عنك بلا قبول، كما لو طلق نسوته أو أعتق عبيده ولا يدري كم هم ولا أعيانهم، والإسقاط لا تبطله الجهالة؛ لأنها لا تفضي إلى المنازعة، وزيادة التفصيل في «الفتح».
[٣) قوله: هذه البراءة؛ إنّما قال: هذه البراءة احترازا عن البراءة التي شرط البائع في قوله على أنه بريء من كل عيب به، فإنّه لا يبرأ عن الحادث بالإجماع؛ لأنه لما قال به اقتصر على الموجود. كذا في «البناية».
[٤] قوله: عند أبي يوسف له؛ وذكره مع الإمام في «المبسوط»، وفي «الخانية»: إنّه ظاهر مذهبهما؛ لأنَّ المراد لزوم العقدِ بإسقاط عن صفة السلامة، وذلك بالبراءة عن الموجود والحادث.
[0] قوله: لا تشمل العيب الحادث؛ إذ المقصود هو البراءة عن العيب الموجود لا على العموم، فلا يدخلُ المعدوم هذا، والتفصيل في «البحر»، وغيره من المبسوطات.
(١) «فتح القدير (٦: ٣٩).
(٢) «البناية» (٦: ٣٧٢).
(٣) «المبسوط» (١٣: ٩٣).
(٤) «الفتاوى الخانية (٢: ٢١٦).
(٥) «البحر الرائق (٦: ٧٢).
Book of Sale: Option of Defect. And if he sold and declared himself free from every defect, it is valid even if he did not enumerate them. As for according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he (the buyer) has recourse for the decrease because this is like a defect. So he values (the item) without this defect, then with this defect, and the seller guarantees the difference between them, as when someone buys a pregnant slave girl, then she dies in his hand due to childbirth, for he has recourse for the difference between her value as pregnant and non-pregnant. And according to Abu Hanifa, the cause of destruction was in the seller's hand, so if it is destroyed in the buyer's hand, it is attributed to that cause, unlike pregnancy, for it is not a cause of destruction. And if he sold and declared himself free from every defect, it is valid even if he did not enumerate them: and according to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid.
[1] His saying: 'because this is like...'; i.e., because this is like a defect that was in the seller's hand, and the buyer became aware of it, and returning it became impossible, so when such a defect appears, he has recourse for the decrease due to the defect; such that a slave who has become subject to amputation (for theft) is appraised, and a slave who has not become subject to amputation, then he has recourse for the amount of the decrease from the price.
[2] His saying: 'it is attributed to that cause'; because the cause existing with the seller necessitated the deserving of the act (cutting), and the deserving necessitated its existence, and its existence necessitated the loss (of the hand), so it becomes attributed to him through these intermediaries.
[3] His saying: 'it is not a cause of destruction'; because the cause that existed with the seller necessitates the separation of the child, not the death of the mother; rather the usual outcome in childbirth is safety; the cause of destruction is illness, and it occurred with the buyer, not the seller.
[4] His saying: 'it is not valid'; immunity from every defect (is invalid) unless he specifies that it is concerning such-and-such, so the sale with a condition of immunity from every defect is void, and in one narration, the sale is valid and the condition is void. This is stated in al-`Inayah.
(1) In al-Minhaj (2:53): 'If he sold with a condition of immunity from defects, the most apparent view is that he is immune from a hidden defect in an animal that he did not know, but not others.' And see al-Umm (7:105) and Mughni al-Muhtaj (2:53) and others.
(2) Al-`Inayah (6:39).
Based on his (al-Shafi'i's) principle that immunity from unknown rights is not valid according to him. And according to us, it is valid; dropping an unknown does not harm him because it does not lead to dispute. Then this immunity covers the existing defect, and also the defect arising before delivery according to Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad, it does not cover the defect arising.
[1) His saying: 'it is not valid according to him'; because in absolution (ibra') there is a meaning of ownership, such that if the creditor absolves the debtor of his debt, then the debtor returns the absolution, the absolution is not valid. And ownership of an unknown is not valid, like its sale. And Ahmad agreed with him.
[2) His saying: 'and according to us, it is valid...'; its explanation: that absolution according to us is a dropping (isqat), not ownership; because ownership of a physical object is not valid with this wording, and absolution is valid by his saying 'I have dropped your debt' without acceptance, just like if he divorces his wives or frees his slaves without knowing how many they are or their identities. And ignorance does not nullify dropping because it does not lead to dispute. And further detail is in al-Fath.
[3) His saying: 'this immunity'; he only said 'this immunity' to distinguish from the immunity that the seller stipulates in his saying 'on condition that he is free from every defect in it', for then he is not immune from the arising defect by consensus, because when he said 'in it', he confined it to what exists. As in al-Binayah.
[4] His saying: 'according to Abu Yusuf...'; and he mentioned it with the Imam in al-Mabsut, and in al-Khaniyyah: it is the apparent view of both of them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) because the intended meaning is the binding of the contract by dropping the description of safety, and that is achieved by immunity from the existing and the arising.
[0] His saying: 'does not cover the defect arising'; because the objective is immunity from the existing defect, not generally, so the non-existent does not enter. And the detail is in al-Bahr and other detailed works.
(1) Fath al-Qadir (6:39).
(2) Al-Binayah (6:372).
(3) Al-Mabsut (13:93).
(4) Al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah (2:216).
(5) Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (6:72).
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the ruling on a sale where the seller stipulates immunity from all defects. The base text states that such a sale is valid even if the seller does not enumerate the defects. The commentary then explores a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding a specific case: if a pregnant slave girl dies after being sold, the buyer's recourse. Abu Hanifa holds that the cause of destruction (the theft that necessitated amputation) was present with the seller, so the loss is attributed to him, unlike pregnancy which is not a cause of death. The companions analogize the situation to a defect, allowing the buyer to claim the decrease in value. Then the passage addresses the Shafi'i position that such a stipulation is invalid. The super-commentary (hashiyah) clarifies the reasoning: al-Shafi'i considers absolution (ibra') as a form of ownership transfer (tamlik), so dropping unknown rights is like selling an unknown, which is invalid. The Hanafis, however, consider absolution as a dropping (isqat), which is not invalidated by ignorance, as it does not lead to dispute. Finally, an intra-Hanafi difference is noted: Abu Yusuf (and apparently Abu Hanifa) hold that the immunity covers both existing defects and defects arising before delivery (qabl al-qabd), while Muhammad restricts it to existing defects only. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the sale is valid, and the scope of immunity depends on the wording of the condition.

Key Terms
البراءة (al-bara'ah): Immunity or disclaimer from defects. In this context, it refers to the seller's stipulation that he is free from liability for any defects in the sold item.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Absolution or release from a claim. In this text, it is used in the context of the seller releasing the buyer from any claim regarding defects.
إسقاط (isqat): Dropping or relinquishing a right. According to Hanafis, isqat is valid even if the right is unknown, as it does not lead to dispute.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty. Here, it refers to the buyer not knowing the exact defects from which the seller is immune.
العيب الحادث (al-'ayb al-hadith): A defect that arises after the sale contract but before delivery (qabl al-qabd). The passage discusses whether immunity covers this type of defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, does a blanket immunity clause cover defects that arise after the contract but before delivery?
A) Yes, it covers them
B) No, it does not cover them
C) It covers only if the seller knew the defect
D) It is a matter of consensus that it does not cover

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that a blanket immunity clause covers defects arising before delivery, based on the purpose of binding the contract through isqat of the description of safety.
